THE RED RAGE.
A MARCHING SONG .
We mix from many lands,
We march for very far;
In hearts and lips and hands
Our staffs and weapons are;
The light we walk in darkens sun and moon and
star. ...
O nations undivided,
0 single people and free,
We dreamers, we derided,
We mad blind men that see,
We bear you witness ere you come that ye shall
be . . .
France ! from its grey dejection
Make manifest the red
Tempestuous resurrection
Of thy most sacred head !
Break thou the dovering cerecloths; rise up from
the dead.
And thou, whom sea-walls sever
From lands unwalled with seas,
Wilt thou endure for ever,
O Milton’s England, these ?
Thou that wast his Republic, wilt thou clasp their
knees ?
These royalties rust-eaten,
These worm-corroded lies,
That keep thine head storm-beaten
And sunlike strength of eyes
From the open heaven and air of intercepted
skies;
These princelings with gauze winglets
That buzz in the air unfurled,
These summer-swarming kinglets,
These thin worms crowned and curled,
That bask and blink and warm themselves about
the world;
These fanged meridian vermin,
Shrill gnats that crowd the dusk,
Night-moths whose nestling ermine
Smells foul of mould and musk,
Blind flesh-flies hatched by dark and hampered in
their husk;
These honours without honour,
These ghost-like gods of gold,
This earth that wears upon her
To keep her heart from cold
No memory more of men that brought it fire of
old;
These limbs, supine, unbuckled,
In rottenness of rest,
These sleepy lips blood-suckled
And satiate of thy breast,
These dull wide mouths that drain thee dry and
call thee blest; . . .
These hours that mock each other;
These years that kill and die,
Are these thy gains, our mother,
For all thy gains thrown by ?
Is this that end whose promise made thy heart so
high ? . . .
O sorrowing hearts of slaves,
We heard you beat from far !
We bring the light- that saves,
We bring the morning star ;
Freedom’s good things we bring you, whenoe all
good things are ...
With us the fields and rivers,
The grass that summer thrills,
The haze where morning quivers,
The peace at heart of hills,
The sense that kindles nature, and the soul that
fills ...
The strife of things and beauty,
The fire and light adored,
Truth, and life-lightening duty,
Love without crown or sword,
That by his might and godhead makes man god
and lord.
These have we, these are ours,
That no priests give nor kings;
The honey of all these flowers,
The heart of all these springs;
Ours, for where freedom lives not, there live no
good things.
Rise, ere the dawn be risen ;
Come, and be all souls fed;
From field and street and prison
Gome, for the feast is spread ;
Live, for the truth is living ; wake, for night is
dead.
Algernon Charles. Swinburne .
The Case of Kipling.
H’M ! here we are again.
Dear Bookfellow,— ln your ingenious
answer to my letter (B. 18/5/01), through
the desire to bring in a neat little
parable, you have missed the point at issue
X merely stated that Kipling had done no
worse than thousands of his predecessors, and
therefore was m no respect more blameworthy
Whether—and this is the point you take up—they
ought not all to be censured, is quite another
question. 1 have no doubt some contemporary Aris-
tarchus solved it for them. Thus, you can hardlv
blame one without mcludingall. It has now become
1 un . usual to reproach (say) Shakespeare
with borrowing and improving ideas. Yet if vou
blame Kipling and not Shakespeare, it would
surely be like passing over the delinquency of a
plutocrat, rich and influential in the applause of
many generations, and “running in” a bee-e-ar
who has barely had the time to gain that of one.
1 he distance between Shakespeare and Kinline- is
of course, very great, but the principle is the
same. Finally, surely it is better to borrow and
improve ideas as Kipling and others have done
than not to borrow and not to improve or to
deteriorate.—Believe me, &e., M. L. Mao Oatthm-
Elizabeth Bay Road, IG/5/’OL Vallum.
“ Miss « d the point,” quotha ! Then it was only
pecause the point was in M.L. MacCallum-riirht
m his controversial vitals. 8

predecessors will not make it less offensive. That
was proven by a reference to the standard of com-

S ciety. If he defended himself by the example
of Jabez Balfour, the Court would stare ! P
But M.L.M. thinks it is an extenuation of Kii>
ling s offence that others have offended similarly •
mon X . CU Tk fe™ because criminals are com.’
mon ! That is bad ethic.
M T r M a f 0 thought sufficient to point out that
M..L.M. a argument was vicious in itself. But the
tK wa ? also vitiated by false comparisons.
Three examples were given of the way in which
veraes accredited to Kipling were written—“ Bill
A /7i k mu ’ - Recessi0rial i ” “ Love and Death.”
no evidence whatever to show that
hmriJt f k0re - V as 8uch a raan ) ” deliberately
took credit for originating his heroic sagas in the
way m which Kipling deliberately took credit for
originating these pieces. There is no evidence
whatever that Homer put himself forward as
of the epic fragments which have
crystallised round his name. Lacking evidence
there can be no comparison. ’
d , su £ffif 8 t that in saying, without qualifica-
taon that . Vergil took both the matter and
metre of his from Homer,” M.L.M
makes too wide a statement. The Latin and the
Creek epic are co-ordinated, but dissimilar. And
X point out that, in so far as Virgil borrowed from
Homer, he borrowed from a famous source—his
borrowings were obvious and avowed. Kipling’s
borrowings in “Bill ’Awkins ” and “Love
Heath were from obscure sources. Thus
Virgil s plagiarism was practically acknowledged ;
Kipling s was not. Virgil’s epic cannot be oalled
a paraphrase of Homer’s in the way that Kipling’s
verse was a paraphrase of Flecknoe’s. As to
Recessional ’’ Virgil “ took ” Homer’s metre as
took Quarles’s-metre. But Kipling’s
Quarles’s metre ; and Virgil’s hexameter
is not Homer s hexameter. Virgil’s epic does not
(generally speaking) transfuse Homer’s ideas in
the way in which Kipling transfuses Newman.
T1 '*’■. Ks far as Virgil s imitation of Homer and
Hesiod extends, it has the value of translation—
it gave to many Latin readers their first
glimpse of the literature of Greece. An acknow-
lodged translation has original merit: and Virgil’s
sources in Homer and Hesiod were so famous
so well known, so much studied by Latin writers
that they cannot fairly be compared with “ My
Lulu,” with Flecknoe’s piece, and doubtfully with
Newman’s “ England.” _ Thus M.L.M.’s parallel
of Virgil and Kipling is a false parallel. Had
Kiplmg imitated Dante or Petrarch it might have
held good.
(4) There is no analogy whatever between
Lucretius versification of Epicurean philosophy
He licrwr/i; Natura and Kipling’s imitation of
My .Lulu, his theft from Flecknoe, or his trans-
fusion of Newman’s piece.
(5) It is not possible to deal with all Chaucer’s
and bhakespeare’s multifarious debts in this space
But to say that “ many of Chaucer’s ‘ Canterbury
bales are merely adapted translations from
Boccaccio” is to speak loosely. According to
Courthope’s table (Hist. Eng. Poetry, vol. i) of
twenty-six “ Tales’’ only five are owed to Boccac-
cio. As regards these five, Chaucer was trans-
lator ; “ Boccaccio ” was a classic of his era • and
the argument in 1T 3 applies. In none of these
fivois there a fair analogy between Chaucer’s use
° t Boccacci ° and Kipling’s use of the originals
(6) Shakespeare is the best card in M.L M’s
poor hand; but I doubt if he can show'that
Shakespeare’s debt m any play is of an identical
kind with Kipling’s debt in “Recessional.”
(7) As to the debt of Milton, Malory, and
Tennyson to Geoffrey of Monmouth, there is no
analogy whatever with Kipling’s case. Geoffrev
of Monmouth wrote prose. If M.L.M. refers to
Wace’s Anglo-Norman versification of Geoffrey
apphes. In Tennyson’s day, at least, Geoffrey
and Wace ranked as early classics, historical, not
obscure. Malory s translation of Wiice’s metrical
romance into English prose is not comparable with
Kiphng s transference of preceding verse-rhythms
and ideas to his own work. Comparison of Tenny-
son’s debt (by descent from Malory, through
Wace) to Geoffrey, with Kipling’s direct imitation
or theft m the cases cited, is erroneous. To com-
pare Kipling’s imitation in “Recessional” with
Milton s invention in “Comus ” is equally erro-
neous.
M.L.M. has now been followed through all his
examples of plagiarism similar to Kipling’s.; and
with the doubtful exception of Shakespeare!
there is not one parallel. “Why gird at Kipling
for do ing this?” But Kipling did not do “this”;
so M.L.M.’s argument was as vicious in its illus-
trations as it was vicious in itself.
Remains scope for brief references to the letter
at head of this article.—The blamo- of Kipling
implies blame of all who have done as Kipling
dld - k akespeare is dead ; Kipling is alive
Who knows but, if Kipling is “ reproached ” often
enough, lie may mend his life, leave the primrose
path of plagiarism, and die in the odour of origi-
nality ! Certainly it is better to borrow and im-
prove than not to borrow and not to improve—
if the debt is acknowledged and paid. Ethically
m literature and other fields, it is not better to
steal and improve, than not to steal and not to
improve. Cellini must not steal my nugget to
make a statue. Kipling must not steal my little
ewe-lamb, feed it and shear it, and call'it his
sheep.—Amen.
We Croak.
When Stork succeeded Log as king
The poor frogs fared but ill;
We’ve both at once—the senseless thing,
The damnable long bill.
James Thomson.
♦
There was excellent occasion to bring all t
senior classes of Sydney schools to the G.P.<
last week, and address them in some such wore
as these:—
“Boys and Girls: You are brought here
witness and profit by a shining* example of bac
sense, bad taste, and bad art. You see tha
avenue of composition pillars in Moore-stree
from George to Pitt. That is designed in honou
mb i° York, son of the British King
The Duke of York is personally a harmless nonet
tity ; officially he has never been of the least us
to Australia, he never will be of any use. T
waste public money in erecting an avenue o:
columns for him to pass through is an example o
bad sense. Always remember that we shculc
keep public honours for those who earn them.
“Next, consider the columns and arches as a
example of bad taste. Observe that the desig
of them is not original; it is merely imitatec
from the art of Greece ; and the imitation is no
particularly good. The procession in which th
Duke of York figures is an evanescent thing
occurring once only; and good taste would sug
gest that the decorations on its path shoulc
be of an evanescent character. Arches of Aus
tLilian foliage, garlands of Australian flowers
perhaps interwoven with gaily-coloured stuffs, o
varied by bright flags ; —these were the natural
the appropriate setting for thousands of happy
Australian faces—the best processional decoratioi
of all. These truly typify rejoicing. Those
pillars typify bad taste. For they are neithe
bright enough for momentary gaiety, nor solic
enough for a permanent memorial: they fal
between two categories, and fail to symbolise
joyous welcome without achieving triumpha
perpetuity.
“Lastly, consider the avenue of pillars as an
example of bad art. Observe that Moore-street
from George to Castlereagh, is the only wide
street in the central city through which the pro
cession will pass. Remember that it was widenec
at great cost in order that the Post Office building
should be seen to advantage, and in order that the
eye should see with pleasure one noble space in a
city of crooked lanes. Now, notice what the pro-
cessional decorator has done with this noble space.
He has filled it up ! crowded it into insig-
nificance with his array of composition columns
Further, look at the other side. What do you
see? A piece of littered ground, with tem-
porary stands for sight-seers, surrounded by
an ugly hoarding. You descend from the super-
lative of the Post-office —which, with whatever
blemishes, is a considerable architectural per-
formance in this city—past the comparatively
worthless of the decorator’s columns, to the posi-
tively disgusting of the hoarding. Of the three
potential decorative factors in Moore-street, the
Space was first, the Post-office next, and the
piece of littered ground was an eyesore. Any-
one with an artistic sense would have left the
Space alone, left the Post-office alone, and spent his
decorative talent on the eyesore. The least he
could do was to spend his decorative talent at
the street-side, hiding his eyesore, transforming his
hoarding, and making Moore-street its own
avenue. Or he might have covered the waste
plot with flowers and shrubs and fountains,
and replaced the hoarding with a suitable
barrier against the crowd—or removed it
altogether and guarded his temporary park by
police on procession-day—at a less price than it
has cost to point this descent from aristocratic
stone, past bourgeois stucco, into squalor.
“ Once more, boys and girls, see with how little
wisdom a country is governed ! ”

.“ The Feast of the Bunya, ” bv Cornelius Moy-
nihan (Gordon and Gotch, Brisbane), is a rough
versification of some episodes of Q. aboriginal life
legend. Without having much poetic value,
the little book is readable; and the author has
taken pains to make his picture accurate. There
are many interesting sketches by A. J.
Hingston, by way of illustration ; and anybody
who has never seen a bunyip may gloat over th,e
artist’s faithful presentment reproduced on this
page.
From the dense scrub at midnight
Is heard a fearful noise:
The gins draiv closer to the fire,
And tremble for the boys.
For well they know the bunyip,
From out the clear lagoons,
Hath come to swallow up the youths,
Now absent two full moons.
This is plainly “ The Feast of the —Bunyip.
J.M. : The (deceased) Rev. Hans Mack was a
Wesleyan, not a Primitive Methodist minister.
Libel, undoubtedly; yet Pallas guided the
blade (not rEneas or the comp.) Quote
‘‘English Bards”—“So the struck eagle. ...”
Every care is taken of Wesleyans on this
page, but no, responsibility incurred for
Primitive Methodists. Baptismal certificates of
Presbyterians are required, not necessarily for
publication, but as a guarantee of the real Dill-
Mackay.
Randolph Bedford’s Clarion excites journalists’
admiration : they know what energy, what
organising capacity, are necessary to bring such a
result. The new Victorian number is as effective
as its predecessors. The pictures are the best of
it—a comprehensive collection of Vic. scenes and
people; some of them from the “Picturesque
Atlas ” —that local mammoth whose huge skeleton,
pounded to bone-dust, fertilises so many ingenious
farmers’ fields. The letterpress is done with
labour; and the account of Eureka is not bad ;
but the whole wants re-writing with a style:
Astley’s fine Higinbotham article, from The Bul-
letin, stands too high above the level. The Clarion
pages are terrible reading : Bedford’s ideas are a
size larger than ordinary folks; but his type
on his pages !

Of Lordly acquaintance you boast,
And the Dukes that you dined with yestreen ;
Tet a louse is a louse at the most,
Tho’ it crawl on the curl of a Queen !
Robert Burnt.
Dear Bookfellow, —When in Rome three years
ago I found a man reading the current Bulletin
amid the ruins of the Coliseum. The anti-climax
struck me a year later, when I was reading the
current Bulletin on the field of Gethsemane.—
William Allan Lloyd.

THE ROOKFELLOW.
NATIONAL ANTHEM.
God prosper, speed, and save,
God raise from England’s grave
Her murdered Queen !
Pave with swift victory
The steps of Liberty,
Whom Britons own to be
• Immortal Queen.
See, she comes throned on high,
On swift Eternity !
God save the Queen !
Millions on millions wait
Eirm, rapid, and elate,
On her majestic state !
God save the Queen !
She is thine own pure soul,
Moulding the mighty whole,—
God save the Queen !
She is thine own deep love
Rained down from heaven above,-*
Where’er she rest or move,
God save our Queen !
Wilder her enemies
In their own dark disguise,—
God save our Queen !
All earthly things that dare
Her sacred name to bare,
Strip them, as kings are, bare;
God save the. Queen !
Be her eternal throne
Built in our hearts alone—
God save the Queen !
Let the oppressor hold '
Canopied seats of gold ;
She sits enthroned of old
O’er our hearts Queen.
Lips touched by seraphim
Breathe out the choral hymn
“ God save the Queen ! ”
Sweet as if angels sang,
Loud as that trumpet’s clang
Wakening the world’s dead
gang—
God save the Queen!
Percy Bysshe Shelley.

Bards of the Backhlocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the Milking-Time.
When the first faint rays of morning thro’ the
frosted window peep,
When our limbs are stiff and weary and our eye-
lids press for sleep,
When the calves and kookaburras make a most
unearthly din,
Young Teddy goes on Bellman out to bring the
cattle in.
He hears the distant lowing,
And sets the noddy going
Like wild-fire down the sidling to the fern-log
bridge below.
His dog goes past him flashing,
Madly barking, madly dashing;
And he feels the young nerves tingle and he feels
the young blood glow.
They go by tracklets winding down the gorge in
snaky turns,
To where the cattle harbor in the orangewood
and ferns,
To where the giant windfalls with the thick-laced
brackens make
From storm and rain a shelter, to the sleet-edged
winds a break, —
To the cosy banks of clover
Where white, tender gums grow over,
And the warm breath like an incense hangs upon
the foggy air.
And Rowdy sends them running
From their coverts warm and cunning,
From many a steamy couch of fern and many a
leafy lair.
They get them all together, and at last they’re
homeward bound ;
The music of their lowing fills the sounding hills
around ;
- misty rises like a phantom herd they
wind,
A sp~~ rid dog and horseman rousing stragglers
up behind.
Then the ringing voice of Teddy
“ Rouse them up there, Rowdy! Steady!”
And Rowdy’s bark imperious the big gorge
answers plain.
Over loam and sapling bridges,
Up the sidling on the ridges.
They spread and bunch and scatter, string, and
spread and bunch again.
Past the orchard, thro’ the sliprails, they go filing
one by one,
Their coats like satin gleaming in the newly-risen
sun ;
In the shed they line-up gladly, for none better
know than they
The sweet smell of the silo and the rustle of the
hay.
Then the warm white wreaths ascending,
And the voices gently blending
In cadence low and tuneful with the separator’s
hum.
Oh, the lilting and the singing
With the milkstrokes rhythmic ringing,
As the deft and supple-handed milkers make the
white froth come!
While the white mists yet are clinging to the
gullies down below,
And the waving tops are gleaming in the morn-
ing’s sunny glow;
While from the mottled mintwood comes the jay’s
sweet matin clear,
Or maggie pipes and warbles in the blackwood
standing near;
And the bleating and the lowing
Down the morning gale are going,
And gorges unto gorges on the wind the echoes
fling;
While the morning’s golden changes play along
the purple ranges
We are busy, busy milking down in Gippsland in
the spring.
V.
KARDELLA.
Aboriginalities.
I read the paper every week,
I find it interesting ;
I love to hear of blokes out-back,
And whalers pub-bars breasting ;
Of kangaroos and cockatoos,
Emus, and other flyers;
In “ Aboriginalities,”
That home of cheerful liars.
Their simple truths are staggering,
Their cod-fish are appalling ;
Their drunks come off uninjured, though
Down deep mines they keep falling ;
The balmy coves out on the plains
Are past all classifying ;
And dogs and bunyips fill the page
Where these coves keep on lying.
I love their snakes, which are so long
They have perspective in them;
And even when these snakes are dead
It takes a week to skin them ;
I love to read of shearers with
Bush-rum infuriated ;
Life would be blank without that page
Where Rougemont graduated.

A REPETITION OF HISTORY.
JOHN FORREST: “If the transcontinental railway is not constructed I’ll bust up the Union.”
GHOST OF JEFF, DAVIS: “That’s the way to talk to ’em! That’s the way I talked to old Abe Lincoln in 1861. And when they caught me in this rig four years later they
were going to Lang me to a sour apple tree, but they didn’t. They couldn’t for laughing!”

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
U'estralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
11, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Ouri.ng to the imposition of lid. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease of in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage —l Id. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition o/The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and ail Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that roe
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in JtlacTc and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, JUNE 1, 1901.
Machine Politics.
The object of government is good living.
The system of community-life is based on
the principle of protecting others in return
for self-protection. That involves a sacrifice
of individual freedom in some respects, in
order to gain unhampered freedom in other
respects. So government is devised to define
the character of the community’s protection,
the limits of the individual’s freedom, in the
form of law. Government is not a good
thing in itself. If the community were
sufficiently intelligent, its members would
rule themselves in relation to each other as
the members of an intelligent family rule
themselves in relation to each other— with-
out need of formal government or registered
law. The less government that is needed
the better. It does not profit to heap laws,
to enrol officials, just because the laws are
laws and the officials are officials. Every
working law is a restriction on somebody’s
freedom. Every official is maintained at
somebody’s cost. Unless the'law can show
its reason, the official his necessity, both are
noxious.
* * * *
The -democratic form of government is in
practice the best which the human brain has
devised. When every individual is called to
take his share in the business of government
in the making of laws, the presumption is
that the laws and the government will repre-
sent his interest in life to the extent of his
power to maintain or change them. The
unison or conflict of interests gives a result-
ant of community-action which represents
the community’s wishes more or less justly
in proportion as the machinery is more or
less perfect. The more intelligent the com-
munity, the better the machinery, and the
more profitable the government.
Much of the machinery of government is
comprised m the term politics. As the
object of government is good living, so the
object of politics is good government. The
community is the agent ; politics its means :
government its end. And just as an artisan’s
tool, while he is shaping his work, reacts on
the artisan aitering the conformation of his
and, the conformation of his brain—so the
politics with which a community shanes its
eii ot government react on the community.
Jiivery man who casts a vote, if the machin-
ery be perfect, wields an influence over the
government. Every man who casts a vote,
under ordinary circumstances, finds that the
vote wields an influence over himself. The
community’s politics are a factor in the
individual's education. They are indeed
a most valuable factor; in virtue of the
aw that exercise strengthens, responsibility
breeds power to meet it. Every man who
votes has cast upon him the duty of con-
sidering government, of weighing the can-
didates who ask to represent him ; and
in ordinary circumstances his interest
impels him to his duty. He desires
government which coincides with his in-
terest ; he realises that his vote will be
effective in proportion as his interest coin-
cides with the interest of the majority of
voters. And the process of voting develops
his intelligence, heightens his sense of com-
munal responsibility, and makes him a more
efficient citizen. Thus the democratic system
is calculated not only to secure better govern-
ment, but in the course of government to
secure a better community.
The intrusion of machine politics into
Australian government is to be deprecated
and resisted because machine politics not only
decrease the resultant of good government,
but also decrease the community’s power of
achieving good government They injure
both the community and the individual.
“ Machine politics ” is an American term to
indicate the system by which voters are
driven like sheep to the poll by
the “boss” of the “party.” The unintel-
ligent voter in America exercises no
discrimination whatever ; he votes as the
“ boss ” tells him, simply because the “ boss”
is the “boss.” The more intelligent voter
under machine-politics sacrifices his intelli-
gence on the altar of “party.” He votes
for this or that candidate, not because he ap-
proves of him, but because the “party bosses ”
teach him that he must put “party ” first, his
personal wishes and opinions last. Two
things result from this system of “machine
politics.” The first is that government, the
end of the community’s efforts, becomes it-
self a means—a means of enriching the
“ party bosses,” of securing their interests at
the expense of the community’s interest.
The country is subordinated to the “ party.”
The second result is that the individual voter,
no longer called upon to exercise his power
of judgment, becomes incapable of judgment.
He sees good government thwarted ; he sees
the machinery of government used for the
aggrandisement of individuals ; he sees the
community robbed and corrupted ; and, so
far from protesting against public spoliation
for private benefit, his thought is to be a
better “party ” man, that he may share in
the “ party ” spoils.
♦ * * *
It is easy to see why a dishonest press and
dishonest politicians advocate machine-poli-
tics and the “ party ” system,—shepherding
voters without opinions of their own, num-
bered and regimented and led to the poll to
vote in a block for the “ticket” which the
“bosses” give them. In a democracy, as
everywhere else, dishonesty cares nothing
for good government as long as its own ends
are served. And enlightened voters, desir-
ing good government, exercising their own
brains, voicing their own individual opinions,
are a constant menace to dishonesty. But
if the voter can be taught that thinking for
himself is a crime, that blind obedience is a
virtue, that a “party” shibboleth always
comes before the country’s welfare—then
what a potent weapon does the voter become
in the hands of dishonesty, eager to make
laws to suit itself, to divert " the oublic
revenue into its own pockets !
“Party organisation has already pro-
gressed far in Australia. Notoriously un-
worthy men have been sent as representa-
tives to the Commonwealth Parliament—
because they placated or represented “party
bosses,” and the mob of voters was driven
by the “ party ” drum to vote for them. In
some of the State Parliaments, for “ party ”
ends, the cry of “ Freetrade v. Protection ”
is still maintained ; though the State
Parliaments can now decide nothing
either for Freetrade or for Protection.
The plan is one which sensible men,
who value their own independence and
the community s interest, should actively
oppose. The only vital issue which can
divide Australia permanently is the issue of
Liberal v. Tory, Progress v. Stagnation—an
issue rooted in humanity. Local interests are
different, and often opposed ; the national in-
terest is one and single. When there is ques-
tion of the means of attaining it, there are
really only two parties—those who are con-
tent with the means we have, and those who
desire to secure better means—the eternal
Man Who Moves and the Man Who Stands
Still. And, whichever side he takes, The
Bulletin counsels every voter to whom
machine-politics are preached for private
ends to answer in Ingersoll’s words : “I’ve
got a thinker of my own ; and 1 mean to do
my own thinking.”
A Plain Tale from the Coast.
Fred. Glynn was probationary assistant store-
keeper at N.S.W. State Coast Hospital at Little
Bay ; and on January 17, 1900, after six months
trial, was informed that his services were not
satisfactory, and, on the recommendation of
Chief Medical Officer Thompson, his appointment
was not confirmed. Glynn appealed to the
Public Service Board and stated in writing that
his services were unsatisfactory because he had
consistently refused to allow the stores contractor
to supply the hospital with food unfit for human
consumption, and not up to sample. He also
deliberately charged his superior officer, C. G.
Willman, with having obstructed him in such
refusal, declaring that that officer had tried to in-
timidate him into passing bad goods, and that
Willman was conniving at the acceptance, by the
hospital, of stores that were infamous in quality
and short in quantity.
» * * *
Nothing could be more direct, and yet the
officer who was accused was not even suspended.
One, Brodie, was sent out to make a departmental
investigation, and from what information this
paper can gather, to hush the thing up. He is
said not to have called the Medical Superinten-
dent, and excused himself for failing to do so on
the ground that Glynn (at this time an outside
civilian) was the prosecutor, and should have had
his witnesses ready. In other words, the Chief
Medical. Officer, it would seem, though informed
that an institution supposed to be under his con-
trol was in a frightful state of mismanagement,
didn’t start a thorough investigation, but was
willing to sit down and listen if a private person
did his work for him. And, by way of helping
that private person to do this, it is alleged that he
wasn’t even notified of the day of inquiry. He
only heard of it by accident. Glynn’s address
was at the Public Service Board’s office, but the
Health Office was too tired to use the telephone.
But the thing would not lie down, and the Public
Service Board held two inquiries, and made two
memorable reports. The first was dated August
2nd, 1900, the other came later. Though this
paper has asked for a copy of that second report,
the See Government refuses to allow the document
to be even seen, let alone published.
* * » *
In the first report, that of August 2, 1900, the
Public Service Board says :
The Matron, in her evidence, says she held Willman In
contempt with regard to stores. Her opinion of the
goods supplied is as follows :
Poultry: atrocious, old, small, tough, and as hard as
wood ; vegetables: peas as hard as bullets, cabbages old,
vegetable-marrows washy and full of fibre, turnips so hard
you could strike a match upon them ; butter, rancid ;
fish, very bad and unfit for human consumption, and had
to be returned nearly every day ; fruit, bad ; vermicelli
and macaroni so bad that when soup was made of it the
nurses refused to drink it, as weevils were floating on the
top. Other officers describe the fish as bad, and sometimes
stinking ; vegetables slimy, and in a state of fermenta-
tion ; potatoes, rotten ; butter not eatable, and hardly
any of the goods up to standard. Everything had to be
weighed or counted, as many articles were often short in
weight. It was a usual thing for the hospital authorities
to have to send to Sydney to purchase goods in lieu of
the articles supplied by Mr. Kidman, and refused delivery,
and thus a great loss of time and considerable inconveni-
ence and expense was entailed, and, if anything, a gain
to the contractor, as the goods bought at the contractor’s
risk were nearly always cheaper and better than what
were supplied.
In the opinion of the Board the evidence points very
clearly to a partisanship on the part of Mr. Willman
towards the contractor. Constant bickerings were going
on between Mr. Glynn and Mr. Willman as to the refusal
or otherwise of the provisions supplied —Glynn being in
nearly every instance correct when the matter was re-
ferred to the Medical Superintendent. Reports were
constantly sent to the Health Office by Dr. Violette res-
pecting the bad and inferior provisions supplied, and, in
some instances, samples were sent, but with little or no
result. In one instance Mr. Kidman was threatened to be
fined the next time he was complained of, but, on the
next time arriving, he was merely cautioned.
Mr. Willman, it appears to the Board, has been too
long at the institution, as he has apparently the idea that
all that is required of him is to superintend and not to
work, and the Board have therefore decided that he be
transferred to some other position in the Public Service
at a reduced salary.
Mr. Glynn, no doubt, has done good work in endeavor-
ing to expose the contractor’s attempts to palm off bad
and inferior provisions, and is entitled to some considera-
tion. The Board are making further inquiries with regard
to him, and will deal with his case in a further minute.
In the meantime they recommend that he be paid three
months’ salary at the rate he was formerly receiving.
At the conclusion of the inquiry it was ascertained that
the Tender Board proposed to accept the tender of Mr.
Arthur Kidman for the supply of groceries to the hospital
for another year, and that no exception was taken to its
acceptance by the Health Office authorities, and the
Board suggested to the Honorable the Premier that fur-
ther action might be deferred until he had perused the
evidence taken at this inquiry, and the report thereon
Mr. Arthur Kidman, hearing of this, asked that the in-
quiry might be re-opened, in order that he might call
witnesses in his defence. This was consented to. Mr.
Kidman gave evidence, and endeavored to show that
there was a conspiracy on the part of the employes of the
hospital to injure him by rejecting provisions which were
in accordance with his contract. This, in the opinion of
the Board, he failed to prove. Permission was given him
to call any evidence he pleased which might throw a light
on the defence he set up, and to cross-examine any of the
witnesses already examined, and at his request the fur-
ther hearing was adjourned, when, after taking further
evidence, the inquiry was concluded.
In the opinion of the Board, Mr. Kidman did not rebut
any of the evidence given at the inquiry, and altogether
failed to prove his defence. The Board therefore invite
the attention of the Government to the evidence, so that
the question whether Mr. Kidman should be allowed to
take up any future Government contracts may receive
due consideration.
* * * *
Nevertheless, Glynn, in the coarse language of
Woolloomooloo, got the boot, and has it yet. He
is still walking the streets of Sydney, unem-
ployed, and his sole reward has been six months’
salary at the rate he received as a probationer at
the Coast Hospital—six months’ salary in over 16
months. Willman, charged not only by Glynn,
but also by the Public Service Board in the above
report, with what, reduced to plain English,
amounts to wilfully conniving at thefeedingof hos-
pital patients with food unfit for human consump-
tion, and causing the Government heavy loss,
drew full pay till sometime in March, 1901, when
he was suspended—nearly 14 months after the
charges were laid against him.
* * * *
Asked by a Bulletin man to explain the long
delay, the Public Service Board replies that it has
done its work. It investigated and reported,
and the rest was for the permanent head and the
Government to do. It was declared that these
shameful practices had existed for seven years.
The P.S.B. had got to the bottom of them in about
12 weeks, and made its recommendations for the
punishment of the guilty parties, and had also
secured the cancelling of the contract. Asked
why Glynn had not received from itself the con-
sideration recommended by itself, the P.S.B.
replies that Glynn had been paid six months
salary—in over 16 months —at the rate he received
at the Coast Hospital. Griffiths, M.L.A.,
stated in N.S.W. Assembly that the adoption of
Glynn’s suggestions had resulted in a saving of
over £6OO per annum on the face of the contract.
What further saving resulted through the Govern-
ment receiving full value for its money has
not been calculated. Glynn’s action secured for
the patients proper food, and generally made the
Hospital a fit place for human beings to be
invalided in. Nevertheless, this service has not
secured him an appointment in the department he
has served so well, and he is still walking about
the streets of Sydney, picking up casual work for
the support of himself and his little child.
* * * *
The secretary of the P.S.B. says the Board is
going to do something for Glynn, some day.
Apparently the truth is that the P.S.B. does not
want to give Glynn a billet. It pleads he is an
indifferent penman and slovenly book-keeper.
Also that he is slow. The sublime impudence of a
Board that has taken over 14 months to procure
the suspension of a man charged with such
offences as it charges against 'illman, talking
about anybody being slow, is beyond comment.
The P.S.B. is not going to employ a man who,
in its own opinion, broke up a system of gross
corruption which had been going on for seven
years because he is not a pretty writer,
and is slow. Has the P.S.B. no fellow-
feeling ? This writer had some of its own
caligraphy in his possession, and if Glynn’s
is any worse, the Board ought to supply him with
a type-writer. Glynn’s apparent offence is that
he went about his reforming in an irreverent
manner; and the P.S.B. apparently likes crawlers
and things that will lick its varnished boots. And
the P.S.B. is a fool. If it throws Glynn over-
board, as it has apparently done, what other officer,
as long as the Board lasts, is likely to come for-
ward with ideas for reform ? Under the same
conditions any private employer would promptly
reward Glynn. The P.S.B. goes out of its way
not to further employ him.
* * • *
Then The Bulletin man saw Dr. Ashburton
Thompson, the “permanent head” of the Health
Department. Asked to explain the long delay in
oonvictmg Willman, or acquitting him and clear-
ing his character, he shrugged his shoulders and
said it was a matter for the P.S.B. Informed
that the Board threw all the blame on him, he
declared that such conduct was monstrous. The
P.S.B. had held the inquiry; it was created to
tt ll 1, 10 Service. His affair was Public
Health. As for what happened at the Coast
Hospital the Medical Supt. had ample powers.
(Shrug.) The trouble was all due to the Reid
Government ignoring his minute to the effect that
there was no one in the Service fit to take charge
of the Coast Hospital. The Reid Government
had insisted on a man being appointed from the
service. Dr. Yiolette was the senior officer, and
this (shrug) was the result. He didn’t remember
Glynn, but had adttazy recollection of Willman.
Later on, his memory became more clear, and he
said Glynn was no beauty (the Hr. - is exceedingly
charming in every way), and that “poor old
Willman had got a lot more than he deserved.
He didn t explain how Dr. Violette had hung
up the finishing of the case for 16 months. He
denied that Glynn had done anything for the
benefit of the Hospital, and said it was all due
iu hu “’ ? r " Thompson. It was also quite untrue
that bad provisions ever reached the patients.
(I he matron swore they reached the nurses, and
that the soup occasionally had weevils floating on
it. This writer was once a patient, and he prefers
to take the matron’s word.) He once more
declared that it was not his fault there had
been any delay ; he was quite powerless to hurrv
things on. 1 his helplessness of the permanent head
was pathetic, but when asked if he could have
suspended Willman, had that storekeeper bashed
him (the doctor) on the nose, the Chief Medical
Officer admitted that he could have suspended
him instantly. Aj-sked was it not strange he could
not do the same when Willman was charged with
conniving at fraud and supplying bad food to sick
and dying people, the doctor shrugged his shoul-
ders and said Ihe Bulletin was a very brilliant
paper. Which, although very true," is quite
irrelevant.
* * * *
The Thompson potentate cannot escape front
his own admission that, had Willman been guiltv
of violence to HIM, he could have dealt with him
at once. If he could in the one case, he could in
the other, and in much less than 16 months—had
1m wanted to. Presumably, he didn’t want to.
A permanent head ” that doesn’t-want-to to such
an extent, and in such an awful business, ought to
be shifted The permanent trunk would be a lot
better without him. His admission also bears out
the statement of the P.S.B. secretary and Mr
Gritchett Walker that the responsibility of the
whole disgraceful delay rests on the shoulders of
Dr. Ashburton Thompson, Chief Medical Officer.

sub judice; “poor old Willman was being tried

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Cheap Sailor-Man.
f J, ve th . e cr ® w of a British ship were up
at Melb. District Court, the other day, charged
with refusing duty. Whilst the iniquitous system
obtains on the Australian coast of allowing
shark owners and charterers to run vessels be-
tween inter-state ports with crews signed on at
the rates of pay obtaining in European waters
such prosecutions are inevitable. The storv is
always much the same. Crews sign on in an


English port for a period of (usually) two years,
at about £3 10s. or *£4 10s. a month for firemen
and sailors respectively. It is understood by the
men (though it isn’t specified) that the proposed
voyage will be entirely a deep-water trip. The
fact that the vessel is to be chartered on this
coast for a period of, perhaps, six months and up-
wards is most carefully kept dark. Then the
men find that they are trading on the Australian
coast at far less than Australian wages—that
they are not only being underpaid, but are help-
ing to cut down local wages and “turning
dog ” on their class—and they naturally assume
that they weren’t informed of the real pro-
gramme of the voyage for the express purpose
of getting them at low wages. As a matter
of course, trouble follows. In these cases the
actual master of the ship is usually unable
to appear against the men, owing to his name
being pro tem on the ship’s register as purser, or
in some other subordinate capacity. The prose-
cutions are carried on by the man who is known
as the exempt master (shipped for the purpose of
evading pilotage dues), who has practically no
connection with the vessel at all, other than taking
her m and out of pilotage waters. This system
has increased amazingly of late years, owing to
the loose way in which Navigation Acts are ad-
ministered, and its extinction can’t come too soon.
The cheap imported sailor man is almost as great
a grievance as any other cheap imported laborer -
the Kanaka included. The difference is that the
sugar-planting Kanaka comes here for the
express purpose of being a cheap toiler; the
sailor-man often finds himself filling that
character wholly against his will. The short
and simple remedy is a Minimum Wage Law for
all seafarers, which will apply to every vessel
while trading between Australian ports —a law
which will begin to apply the moment a vessel
touches at its first Australian port of call, and
apply through the whole intermediate period.
And this law should absolutely override any con-
trary agreement which may exist between owners
and crew. When once a vessel begins journeying.
between Australian ports cheap-labor agreements
should be mere wrapping-paper, and should remain
so till the ship leaves these waters. And, if it is
found that the extra wage paid while in Australian
waters is afterwards deducted, the law should
have a heavy fine to collect from that vessel on her
next appearance, even if the masts and the big
anchor have'to be sold by auction to recover the
amount.
The Case of L. T. Heydon.
There has been received at this office an enor-
mously long letter from Mr. Louis Francis
Heydon, with reference to his connection with
the Moss case—the case in which he was engaged
on one side and took commission from the other
side, and was thus interested in both sides—an ar-
rangement which ended in the refunding of the
commission, a £SO fine, and some very severe
language from the Bench. Mr. Heydon requests
that, “ in fair play,” his letter shall be published.
One of the griefs of a newspaper office consists of
the men who, being commented on to the extent
of a few inches of space, send a reply about a
fathom long, and demand “in fairness” that it
shall be printed. Mr. L. F. Heydon explains in
a general way that he didn’t start the Moss suit,
that lie didn’t get all, or anything like all, of the
£SOOO costs, that he was of great service in mini-
mising costs in various directions, &c., all of which
are quite irrelevant to anything this paper said.
The points of Mr. Heydon’s letter which seem to
be really relevant are these :
I have been blamed for accepting commission from pro-
fessional gentlemen to whom I recommended clients, and
they also see the costliness of the litigation, and they
think that my receiving commission has increased the
total costs. That is not so. The gentlemen in question
lost what they paid me ; and the estate would have been
none the richer had they retained all their costs
My own bills, throughout the four years of the
suit, totalled £1622; of which barristers’ fees con-
sumed over £7OO ; and -I received as commission
£320 more, making a total of £1220 I may
quote some instances of my efforts to keep down the
costs. Four of the adult children of the testator had
consulted me, and under the statement of claim as drawn
the law forbade me to act for them, so that I sent them
to other solicitors. Two other adult children did not
consult me, but sought other advisers. In the end, the
total costs of these two latter children was £BSO 7s. 5d.,
or £425 per head, while the total costs of the four chil-
dren who had consulted me was £662, or £165 10s. per
head. ... To some persons the taxations seem a grave
point. During the course of the suit taxations took place
live times. Only on the last occasion is there any com-
plaint made of me, and the total of all the bills at that
time for taxation in which interest could be supposed to
influence my conduct was £460. On that occasion, for
the first time, the Master in Equity gave directions that
the taxation of such bills should be attended by the
plaintiff’s solicitor alone—that would be myself or my
clerk. I had no reason to expect this change, which
was made for economy’s sake, and, in fact, I did not
know it, my managing clerk not informing me of it. . . .
It is not the solicitor, but the taxing officer, who taxes.
He is a most experienced and capable official, who goes
through the bill item by item, and taxes off improper
items, whether they be objected to or not; though I ad-
mit that the solicitor attending is there for the purpose
of assisting the taxing officer in his taxation. ... At the
taxation in question all the bills filed were most impar-
tially reduced by a percentage hardly varying. That is
to say, the bills of the solicitors to whom 1 had sent
clients were reduced by the taxing officer in the same
proportion as the bills of the other solicitors.
The relevant part of Mr. L. F. Heydon’s state-
ment appears, therefore, to amount to this: The
solicitors whom he imported into the case charged
only a very moderate amount for the work they
did. Compared to other solicitors in the case
their moderation, according to Mr. L. F. Hey-
don, amounted to a disease. And out of their
scanty payment lie had the awful front to take the
large sum of £320. His case seems to be that
instead of shamefully plundering the estate he
shamefully plundered his fellow-solicitors. And
out of the £320 which he thus unjustifiably re-
ceived he seems to have returned £977 to the
estate. As to whether there was any reason why
the parties to the suit who consulted Mr. L.
F. Heydon should not have had one solicitor
among them, as seems to have been their original
intention, and whether, by introducing them to a
solicitor apiece (Mr. L. F. Heydon received com-
mission from all these solicitors), he wasn’t
bringing various extra and needless solicitors
into the business, Mr. L. F. Heydon says
nothing. In one part of his letter he
certainly says that every party having sepa-
rate interests must be separately represented,
but it doesn’t follow that the interests which Mr.
L. F. Heydon entrusted to all this batch of soli-
citors (from whom he received commission) were
separate interests. And if they were not, then he
did heap needless, costs (of which he received
a large share) upon the estate —unless four or five
solicitors cost just the same as one. Mr. L. F.
Heydon has declared that his commission didn’t
increase the bills of the very economical soli-
citors whom he introduced into the suit. If
it can be further proved that all these
very economical solicitors whom Mr. L. F.
Heydon introduced were essential, or, if they
weren’t all essential, that the extra ones made no
difference wtiatever in the expenses, then Mr. L.
F. Heydon will be fully acquitted of doing any
injury to Moss’s estate. ' And, by way of variety,
he will be convicted of doing the injury to his
professional brethren instead. Certainly, this
paper would rather see a solicitor eat another soli-
citor than see him eat the public, but the differ-
ence, though to the public interest, doesn’t neces-
sarily raise its opinion of the solicitor very much.

X A Spectre from ’9l. /
N.S. W. Ex-Treasurer William MM ill an, ofiill
men in the world, lately raised his brief figure in
the Federal House of Representatives, on the
Freetrade side, and made a long and whirling
address on the advantages of a Freetrade policy.
And M'Millan, of all men in the world, said that
he was a person who had always been a Freetrader
and who had walked consistently in the narrow
path of Shoddy. And yet MMtllan was Trea-
surer of N.S.W. for a number of years, and his
tariff heavily protected bricks, beer, bacon, hams,
tongues, biscuits, butter, candles, cement, cheese,
chemicals, cider, cigars, cigarettes, tobacco, con-
fectionery, cornflour, fish, almost every kind of
fruit, almost every kind of wooden or galvanised-
iron wares, preserves, condensed milk, molasses,
treacle, sugar, paints and oils, salt, varnish, white
lead, wine, newspapers, and other things. He
didn’t need to protect these things for the sake
of revenue; a very moderate amount of direct
taxation and one or two countervailing excise
duties would have enabled him to dispense
with every protective item in his tariff.
He certainly didn’t protect the things that he
imported himself—if local industries had arisen in
that line to supply the people with employment it
would doubtless have been bad for his importing
business. But outside his importing business
there were very few industries that he didn’t
assist with some'measure of Protection. And
M’Mtllan declares that Protection in any shape
is injurious and a curse to the country, and poses
as a man who has always been of that opinion and
has neVer fallen away into fiscal darkness. So the
spectre of the old MMtllan tariff rises up before
Softgoods William and offers him these alterna-
tives :
(1) Either he protected all theie goods, believing that
Protection was a curse and a disaster, and thereby
wilfully aud maliciously laid himself out to injure and
impoverish the country. In which case it is difficult to
distinguish between him and any dynamiter or other
public enemy.
(2) Or he protected all these industries believing that
Protection was good, which implies that he is a man who
doesn’t adhere to the truth when he says he believes
Protection is bad. In that case it may possibly be inferred
that he tried to think he believed in Freetrade in order to
avoid establishing Protection all round, which would have
damaged his own importing- business. And that puts him
in the position of being a man who deliberately sacrificed
the interests of N.S.W., so far as they were sacrificed by
his failure to protect the local softgoods manufacture, in
order that his Calico Jimmy connection might prosper.
And that again makes him a traitor, and a very mercenary
and sordid one, to the country which he represented
(3) Or else he hadn’t the courage of his .opinions ; or
else again, outside his own Calico Jimmy interests, he had
no real opinions, in which case all the froth and fury of
his Freetrade orations were mere self-seeking and self-
interest, and his political career was simply a means
towards his private ends—mostly calico ends.
The Bulletin has no desire to call M’Millan
bad names, but it can’t help hearing the names
his own tariff calls him. And it can’t help hear-
ing the names which the same tariff calls Bruce
Smith, who was also Treasurer under it, and took
his share of the responsibility of it. No man who
joined the Parkes Ministry, and took the responsi-
bility of the Parkes tariff, can claim to have
been a consistent Freetrader without the old
tariff arising out of its tomb to call him a double-
dealer, or an incapable, or a person who dared
not do what he believed was right. The virtuous
pretensions of the remnant of the old Parkes
gang—the gang that was responsible for the pro-
tective Parkes tariff —to be consistent Freetraders
of many years’ standing ought to sicken any decent
Cornstalk citizen who knows the history of his
own country.
Unclaimed Balances.
“South Australia” writes re unclaimed bank
balances : By Act 539 of 1891 and section 185
of Act of 1892, South Australia compelled banks
and others to disgorge into the Treasury any un-
claimed balances they held. £16,000 was brought
to light by the Act of 1891, £6OOO of which was
promptly claimed by its proper owners, and the
balance the Treasury holds awaiting claimants.
It was the publicity which the Government gave
to the matter which enabled those owners to get
their due. They might have waited long enough
till the banks informed them that money was due
to them. I need scarcely say the banks fought
this Act bitterly, the late Samuel Tomkin-
SON, ex-bank manager, and then M.L.C., leading
the forces; but the Act was carried mainly because
of a scandal which came to light some little time
before. It appears a man was entrusted with a
large sum of money some 30 years ago, and sent
to our south-eastern district to disburse it. He
lodged it in a bank, and afterwards died suddenly.
As no trace of the money was found among his
papers it was assumed he had embezzled it, and as
the bank did nothing to clear his memory his
family bore the stigma until accident revealed the
fact that the bank had foully wronged the dead
man by silently retaining the money. And the
management showed not a vestige of shame over
the infamous transaction. This is no fairy tale.
If your readers will refer to our Auditor-General’s
11th, 12th, and 13th reports, 1893-4-5, Parlia-
mentary papers No. 4, pages XLI., XLLI.,
XIII., and XI., they will find the above corrobo-
rated, and will also get information respecting ihe
workings of the Acts. Yet I see that your
Sydney Telegraph denounces the proposal lately
made in England to stop such shameful practices
as an organised attempt to plunder and despoil
the banks, Some people have a curiously inverted
way of looking at things.
Politics and Platitudes.
The average calibre of the Federal representatives
from New South Wales is a figure which, in mere
contemplation, is humiliating. Sid. Smith,
Wilks, Gould, Neild, Pulsford— if all the rest
were Solons, these names would pull the average
below zero. But even Neild is a statesman com-
pared with the average candidate for the State
Assembly. About half the lawyers and aldermen
of N.S.W., and all the men who have ever held a
bottle or a sponge for a candidate, are already
announced, and their platforms are gs ordinary as
their names. They are, without exception, in
favor of amendment of nearly everything, and
particulars about the exact direction in which they
propose to amend any tiling are as scarce as bear’s
eggs. In a hazy fashion and with an entire
absence of detail, they favor “amelioration of the
laws for the benefit of the masses ” —and they are
all simply yearning for “ liberal legislation.” It
is a good word —“liberal.” It seems to imply
something a lot better for everybody than any-
body has had before, and it is so useful
that the slowest and most retrograde human tomb-
stone in politics calls himself Liberal nowadays.
“Liberal legislation,” a “progressive policy,”
“judicious economy,” and “ a wise system of re-
trenchment ” are the stale stock-in-trade of the
drag-net politician. So are these beautiful general
phrases: “ the development of the resources of
this great country,” “lightening the taxpayers’
burdens,” “promoting settlement,” and a “care-
ful regard for the great producing industries,”
likewise “the encouragement of commerce.”
'Some of the larger men having gone away, the
small, dull platitudinarians are even more numer-
ous than usual, and their dead platitudes stand up
like a forest of ring-barked gums in a flat country.
Nothing could be much more shadowy than the
programme of Premier See, except it be the pro-
gramme of would - be Premier Lee. See and
Lee both want to “develop the resources of the
country by wise legislation,” and See and
Lee each says that his legislation will be
found remarkably wise when it is revealed.
And that’s where the Labor-party will score.
While other candidates talk platitudes about
developing the resources of the country, the
Labor-party, with a visible platform and a solid
vote, should be able to top the poll in enough
electorates to give New South Wales progressive
legislation for the next three years. The
Bulletin has said before, this next election will
give that party the chance of a lifetime. It wants
a John Burns or Dick Seddon (the old-time
Dick Seddon) to lead it, and it wants a financial
policy (hitherto its only financial policy has con-
sisted in having no opinion about the tariff ques-
tion, aud now even that is gone, for there is no
tariff question left to have no opinion about). Witli
these two things it should be able to blot the old
indefinite potterers off the political slate.

The Groans of B Smith.
Bruce Smith, who promises to annex the leader-
ship of the rank Tory party in the House of
Reps., threatens an anti-Labor move when occa-
sion seems to require it. To the statement that
the Federal Labor Party will “hold the balance
of power ” and will give support in return for
concessions, he replies: “There are, it will be
found, too many thoughtful men returned to the
Federal Parliament—men determined to check
these methods at all hazards —to admit of this very
sanguine little party selling its support from time
to time for political concessions.” Perhaps;
though they don’t look like it—and the Laborites
are, besides, getting used to that threat; they
heard it first eight or ten years ago. But, on the
general question, as to the awful immorality which
B. Smith professes to see in the party’s tactics,
there’s another side. Thus Mr. B. Smith :
The effect of this rule (to vote solid) is to lead fre-
quently, and possibly in most important issues, to govern-
ment by minority—the one thing above all others that
the principle of democracy was designed to make im-
possible. A great question of State policy might come
up in the Commonwealth House of Representatives.
There might be among the 72 members 28 ayes and 24
noes, and of the remaining 20 Labor members 9 might be
ayes and il noes—at heart. If the Labor Party were free
to vote according to conscience, the result would De :
28 and 9 ayes = 37
24 and 11 noes = 35
giving a majority of two for the ayes. If the Labor
Party apply the caucus so that the majority of that party
rules, they will give :
24 and 20 = noes 44
28 and 0 = ayes 28 '
a majority of sixteen for the noes. Thus, what would
have been a majority for the ayes becomes a majority for
the noes. And this is Democracy !
This is all very true, and the Labor Party’s solid
vote is quite undemocratic. But the amusing
feature _ of the case is that the dull B. Smith
. thinks it is all a special iniquity of the Labor
Party, and doesn’t understand that he and nearly
everybody is doing exactly the same thing.
For instance, suppose while Reid was Premier
some Oppositionist had moved that Reid’s policy of
spending £BOO,OOO a year of loan money for ordinary
expenditure purposes was a fraud and an infamy
—a motion which, if carried, would have ejected
Reid and put the Protectionist party in power.
Possibly among 40 miscellaneous members, not
counting the Reid party, there would be 27 ayes
and 13 noes. And among, say, 60 Foreign-traders
present, including B. Smith, there might be—if
the Foreign-traders were at liberty to vote accord-
ing to their consciences—4o ayes and 20 noes.
Result: —
27 and 40 ayes = 67.
13 and 20 noes = 33.
Majority of 34 for the ayes. But the Foreign-
traders, including B. Smith, would all vote “ No ”
—not because the caucus told them to, but because
George Reid told them to, and in order to keep
the Foreign-trade gang in office. And the result
would be 73 noes to 27 ayes. The difference is that
in one case a great part of the Labor Party fre-
quently outrages its conscience by voting the way
the Labormajority bids it. And the other parties—
including the one that B. Smith belongs to—quite
as often outrage their consciences by voting the way
the party boss bids them. Where 21 Labor mem-
bers are present, not more than 10 can be called
upon to vote against their convictions in order to
keep the party together. In a party of 21 Foreign-
traders (including B. Smith) it can easily happen
that 12 or 15, or perhaps 20, vote against their
convictions—to keep the party together, and the
party’s boss in office, and the hated other fellow out
of office. The Labor party’s policy is bad, and the
policy of the other parties is similar, only
worse. But the other parties started the system.
The most visible outward difference is that the
Labor party votes solid after discussion, and the
other parties don’t discuss but vote as the boss
tells them.
“ Bull’s Wool ” cries from the West:
The only conclusion one can come to after reading the
evidence to the Western Lands Commission at Hay is that
fanning and grazing are equally a gamble. Seasons can
never be relied upon for even three months at a stretch.
Except in some isolated instances, no one has done any
good who has had to make much outlay—the record has
been one monotonous failure after another. “ Big risks,
big returns,” is an axiom of all business-men, therefore
give the man in the West a show. Let him have land at
a nominal rental, tenant-right in improvements, and a
long lease, and the chances are he won’t starve if he
got a decent area (say a 10-mile block). If good induce-
ments are not held out, the land can’t be taken up by
bona-fide tenants, because it won’t make a living except
under nominal expenditure. It is better to take a little
rent than have the country abandoned, and not only non-
producing, but a harbor for vermin of all descriptions.
Moreover, money is very tight for Western properties.
Banks and financial companies have been bitten until
they are “ not taking any,” so there is little or no hope of
financial help. The abandonment of the country is not
over yet unless some strong inducement is given. Many
of the large holdings are only waiting to see how the
Government is going to act on the report of the Lands
Commission, and, if no help comes, lots will have to
pass in their checks. Each Minister for Lands in turn is
going to solve the problem, but until one arises who has
eaten tinned dog, suffered from Barcoo rot, and “ dropped
his bundle ” by personal experience in the West, “ there is
one hope, and only one,” as the hymn says, and that’s
Buckley’s.
A Victorian paper called Woman’s Sphere
breaks out in its May issue with an appeal to all
women to sool on the authorities to a more rigid
enforcement of the law re criminal assaults on
young girls, which is about as superfluous as a fur
overcoat in Gehenna. And, strangely enough,
this moral and globular publication makes not one
single illusion to the fact that an enormous pro-
portion of the alleged cases of assault on girls
■prove to be lying accusations concocted by malicious
and dirty-minded females—often levelled at com-
plete strangers out of a mere horrible desire for
notoriety. And even when the unsavoury female
admits her perjury, she isn’t prosecuted by the
astounding rulers of this country. Also, in a very
large number of cases where convictions are ob-
tained, there is good reason to believe that the
conviction were wrongfully obtained. The ob-
jectionable she-perjurer knows that she runs no
risk even when she is bowled out, and as a result
she is numerous, and out of a multitude of girl
perjurers some are bound to escape discovery.
Woman’s Sphere need not worry about the need
for enforcing the law against men who assault
girls—the law is so well enforced already that the
Australian man has every chance to get seven
years whether he assaults a girl or not. A paper
that wants to sool the local girl on to make more
charges against men, has a capacity for carrying
buckets of salt water to the sea that rises to the
level of genius.

Nothing except Christian burial could have been
more dismal than the first debate in Australia’s
first Parliament. The standard was no higher
than that of the church literary society. In the
House of Reps, the speeches upon the Address-in-
Reply were severely platitudinarian. W. H.
Groom, of Queensland, mumbled on in the man-
ner of “ the lean and slipper’d pantaloon.” Most
of his remarks fell in childish treble whispers into
the thin hair on the head of Treasurer Turner,
who sat immediately below him. Crouch, of
Victoria, at once plunged into the mighty national
question of the pay of the Victorian Artillerymen
who happen to have votes in the district for which
he sits. Reid was amusing in a way, as all good
clowns should be. He worked hard, like the
funny man in the play, and became damp with
dishonest perspiration, but his efforts to make the
derned thing go were lost. In the Senate a worse
state of affairs existed. Simon Fraser made a
disconnected screech about all things under the
sun, and Gould, Neild, and Millen had all the
cheerfulness of a tea-meeting under chloroform.
♦
Rightly the See Govt, commuted the death sen-
tence passed on Ernest Montgomery Jones,
convicted, but with a recommendation to mercy,
of having shot Alfred Pittman, at Tamworth.
And a point that should be early taken is that
where a jury recommends to mercy the death sen-
tence should not be pronounced by the Judge, but
reserved for Cabinet consideration. As matters
are, the prisoner is sentenced to death, even when
the case is one where it is practically certain that
the sentence will not be carried out ; and then he
is put in irons by way of going through the pre-
liminary torture, and kept in them for weeks
until the painfully-slow wheels of Government
procedure have time to roll over his case.
“ S.S.”: The proclamation of the Commonwealth
in Sydney was diverted into a grand gluttonous
and guzzling glorification of the swaddy. The
opening of the Commonwealth Parliament in
Melbourne was forgotten in a great abasement of
fat toads before the children of a king. The Com-
monwealth (shade of O. Cromwell !) was nothing
—nobody remembered it, nobody cared a two-
penny objurgation about its claims, its dignity.
There was nothing seen or thought of but a royal
Duke and a royal Duchess surrounded by gaudy
satellites, around whom again assembled the grey
toads balanced on their paunches, whilst the crowd
gathered beyond and craned its million necks,
giraffe-like, in its vulgar frenzy, sweating to catch
one glimpse of a royalty. Australia ! Where was
she during all this ? I pictured her a deserted
figure standing afar off, her stern, white face
turned from the saturnalia of fuss and grease,
waiting for that time when her people should go
back to her, penitent, like prodigals who have
been eating husks which even the swine reject.

“Melb.”: There is already urgent necessity for
an overhaul of the Press telegraphic arrangements
in regard to the Australian Parliament. Those
arrangements are wholly inadequate to meet any
strain. I will confine myself to Sydney. The
telegraphic department has made no special
arrangements worth noting for such a tremendous
new development as the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment. The department seems to think that Par-
liamentary telegraphy is going to be nothing more
than the ordinary thing. I write on Friday.
Parliament is up till Wednesday. The three
nights’ experience have been enough. Officials
complain that the wires were overloaded. Yet a
debate on an Address, as every pressman knows, is
mere mush compared with what is to follow when
big questions come up. lam informed that the
Sydney Telegraph complained of getting im-
portant copy two hours late, and too late
for the paper. Some Parliamentary copy
has actually been lost and cannot be
traced at all in the Department. The assumption
of the Sydney papers was that the admir-
able arrangements in Sydney are paralleled
in Melbourne. Not at all. Everything
wants overhauling. To begin with, the
Sydney reporters in the Commonwealth Par-
liament will need entirely reformed arrangements
for getting out copy and sending it away.
The Electric Telegraph Department sends up two
or three little boys in red as messengers. A
Sydney reporter happened to go out after he had
given telegraphic copy for despatch, and when he
returned he found the urchin still there. He
hadn’t even started, and the whole Department is
just like that small boy.
The “Light-Load Bill” now before the British
House of Lords provides—
That every sea-going- vessel other than those of under
80 tons reg. employed in the coasting trade, yachts and
fishing-boats, shall before Jan. 1, 1902, carry on their
sides, in such a position, which shall be left to the Board
of Trade’s officials to determine, a mark similar to the
maximum-load-line mark. When proceeding to sea in
ballast or partially loaded, this new load-line must be im-
mersed, or at least at the water’s edge, otherwise the
vessel is to he liable to detention, and the person respon-
sible to similar penalties as for overloading.
This much-needed measure should go far towards
preventing the practice of sending vessels
(especially steamers) to sea insufficiently ballasted
—the latter sometimes having only half of the
propeller-boss immersed. It will doubtless re-
ceive the strong support of Lloyd’s, who have
been so badly bitten during the last few years
over broken tail-shafts and dropped propellers,
that the period of tail-shaft inspection has been
reduced from two years to one. Compliance with
this rule ought certainly to be added to their
other requirements by Australian Marine Boards,
as the practice it is aimed at is very prevalent‘on
this side of the world.
“ N.” : On every railway bridge under
which the Duke of York passed on his trip from
Melbourne to Brisbane there was a man with a
red flag ready. No one else was allowed on the
bridge when the train was going past. The
authorities had a vision of an anarchist on each
bridge with a keg of dynamite ready to drop
on the duke as he went by. Yet is there a
eal practical working anarchist in Australia ?

“Mignon,” as adapted from a more sombre
story in “ Wilhelm Meister,” is a rather patchy
tale of not much motive, but as in opera the music
is always supposed to wipe its feet on the words
this is a matter of small importance. Briefly, as
embodied by Musgrove’s company, at Sydney
Royal, it is the story of a stoutish, phlegmatic
young student who buys from her master a bare-
legged girl of no importance. She earns her
living by doing an occasional egg dance—the
dance in question is not given, though the eggs
are placed tantalisingly before the audience. If
Wilhelm had been a Frenchman this transaction
would have resulted in immediate Romance, but
being a German, he merely tells Mignon to warm
her hands by the fire, and endeavors, as far as can
be ascertained from his grand-opera fashion of
saying everything in scales, to inspire her with
the desire to be something useful. Later, his
orderly soul being upset by her habit of annex-
ing his other best girl’s dresses, he advises
her to take courage and depart. He does not
seemingly ask her to take anything else in
the way of coin or luggage, but he refrains from
doing so in such a beautiful voice that no one
notices the omission at the time. An act
further on, Mignon's as yet undiscovered father
fires a castle for no adequate reason, while the
people to whom the castle seems to belong stand
outside and sing a cheerful chorus, giving a strong
impression that the insurance money is a matter
of some importance, and that they have a good,
large policy in a solid office. Later on, Wilhelm,
Mignon, and the harping old father meet casually
in another castle, when, happening to hear Mig-
non murmur his name in her sleep, it occurs to
that excessively steady young man that she may
possibly be rather fond of him. He then signifies
that he has no objection, after which Mignon fills
in time by fainting for a few minutes. She is
revived by three bars of music, and the curtain
falls with the three singing songs together, it
being understood that Mignon and Wilhelm are
to be married, though nothing very definite is
said on the subject.
* * * *
Madame Slapoffski, who takes the part of the girl
who comes to fortune through refusing to earn her
living, has an exquisite voice of light and shade,
but she requires face exercise, so that she may
learn to control her features when she kneels with
her mother’s prayer-book in Ijer hands, or dis-
covers her long-lost father by means that would
not establish her claim in a court of law. The
impression left in this writer’s mind was that
clever Madame Slap, has a sense of the ridiculous,
which is bad in grand opera. Miss Lilian
Coomber, as Felina, occasionally helps the
prompter to earn his bread. Her voice hardly
manages the staccato notes in “Titania.” Salvi
is nonchalant as the young man who makes love
to one girl in front of another; he sings in perfect
tune but without much enthusiasm, and when we
read in the programme that he is a young student
in search of adventure, it seems incongruous that
he should look so much more like a young man
who wants to be left in peace. If he was described
as being in search of a second-hand chest of
drawers or a durable kitchen-chair, the situation
would be more understandable. He looks like a
man in pursuit of a useful substance that is going
cheap.

There’s a whisper that “Florodora” has
nearly run its course at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), and
that the “ Casino Girl ” is hovering about some-
where behind the wings ready to float on the first
opportunity. Still, so many changes have been
made in “Florodora” itself that it is almost a
new opera again, and with a few more changes it
might start its career anew, and do the whole
business over again.
** # *
In “ The Night Birds of London,” due to
flutter round Sydney Lyceum some time longer,
the first act, with its gauzy ballet, is unquestion-
ably the best, and next to it comes the final up-
roar when the public anxiety over the Missing In-
fant and the Lost Will is relieved by a bright and
deserving gas explosion. At the same time the
conduct of the heroine in following her husband
into the dubious drawing-room of the Night Birds
Club is certainly peculiar; whilst still more un-
feminine is her action in leaving him there, with
the remark that she intends to go home and pray
for him. The wife who prays for her husband in-
stead of protecting him would be the first to feel
annoyed if he did the same by her in time of
temptation. For the rest, the drama plods along
the old weary road of the vitriol-throwing wife
and the inconvenient husband, and continues to be
not nearly so wicked as its name infers.

“The French Spy” continues to find
out nothing of any real value at Sydney
Criterion, where Russia is depicted as a place in
which no one dares to make the most ordinary
observation without looking out of the door, in
case an emissary of the Czar should be about.
The evolutions of the Mysterious Document need
careful study during the first act, its peregrina-
tions being varied, though, later, it is permitted
to die out of the play altogether, having done its
fetal work of getting everyone into trouble,
ihereis a fine, but exaggerated, touch about the
woman who wants to wear her best things before
her rival, when the latter is being led out to
execution. The fact that the rival is blind, how-
ever enjoyable in itself, is, under the circum-
stances, distinctly regrettable.
