The gigantic and utterly selfish stranger
who came all the way from Mars to teach Horace
Parker the gospel of Otherdom is back again at
Sydney Palace, not from any love of Horace
but m order to work out his own sen-
tence, and _ get back home again. As the
equally selfish, earthly person who was con-
verted, Herbert Ross is absolutely natural, and,
therefore, good. Presumably a messenger who
had been heaved out from Mars to Earth would
act somewhat strangely, and that assumption, no
doubt, explains the dolorous voice of Stephenson,
lhis writer doesn’t know any Martians, and, there-
fore, cannot say if the long, grey individual is
natural or pot, but, viewed from a worldly stand-
point, he is decidedly forceful, and that covers a
multitude of eccentricities. Roxy Barton does
the worldly Mrs. Clarence to perfection, and Aunt
Martha struck this warm-hearted scribe as the
prettiest woman he ever saw—on the stage.
* * * *
The Duke and Duchess arrive in Melbourne
nightly per animated picture at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall, afterwards proceshing per medium
of the same instrument. The Duke, as in real
lif®, gives the impression of a small nervous man
with a strong tendency to become invisible. We
see him alighting from the gangway, after which
he seems to get lost, and a large crowd of great
people move about with their backs to the
audience looking on the ground for something.
For a considerable time he continues lost,
generally seeming to be stowed away behind the
big drum, or the Duchess’s hat, or some
similar erection. The man who explained
things occasionally advised the audience to note
the enthusiastic crowds, but all the crowds this
scribe could see appeared to take the whole affair
in a haughty spirit. Some curious effects are
given by these animated pictures. The Melbourne
illuminations are a dazzling show, and the ten-
dency of the instrument to make what is dark
darker and what is light lighter is weird and
ghostlike. For instance, when the Highlanders
are marching in a long even row their gleaming
white socks stand out conspicuously and appear
to be going entirely on their own, the balance of
the brawny Scots being merged in the general
murkiness of the street.
* * * *
At Sydney Empire, Fisko, the Boy Juggler,
spins plates in the air, sometimes to the final de-
struction of one of them, but when he is a little
older and has quite overcome his stage fright, he
will probably make himself complete master of
those tricky pieces of china. A song on the Boys
of Australia is well rendered by one of the com-
pany, but the audience is thinking of the Dooko-
york, and while their eyes are on the stage their
thoughts are with the fleshpots of Government
House. It is difficult to fix the attention of an
audience these times.
* * * *
The Imperial Concert season at Sydney Cen-
tennial opened well. The callow-looking Raimund
Pechotsch and his handsome dad have got a strong
grip of the Muse, and make her express her feel-
ings through their violins. There are thirteen
items on the programme, but the multitude of
encores runs it to nearly three times that length,
and as long as big Avon Saxon and Mdlle. Vir-
ginia Cheron respond with items like “ Down the
Swanee River ” and “ Cornin’ thro’ the Rye,” the
encores will come thick and fast. Yet this is a
land where the aged papa is merely the old bloke,
and where the rye is infested with snakes, and
to smile at a girl therein means an action
for breach of promise in the near future.
As for Madame Belle Cole, the tremendous
demonstration that greeted her appearance
after over seven years’ absence showed how well
she was remembered.
* * * *
One of the most meritorious of the flood
of little shows which has poured in on Sydney in
the day of its ducal affliction is the dialect and
character entertainment at the Y.M.C.A. Hall,
where the versatile Mr. Parkyn recites “Banjo’s”
poems, sketches a Cornish farmer rousing his
youngsters up at an early hour, and recites in
English, Scotch, Welsh, Irish, and Cornish.
Between whiles, accompanist Bromley lifts up a
serviceable tenor voice in a manner which gener-
ally moves the limited audience to an encore. But
the one-man show has fallen on evil days, and
dropped by the wayside generally.
* * * *
Wirfch’s Circus commenced its latest Syd-
ney campaign on the old battling ground opposite
Redfern railway station, Thursday last, and
although the interfering authorities had
ukased that members of the show should not
go into the tiger’s cage to be damaged by
the raging contents thereof, The game o! French
tigue, the players of which, being mounted, try
to grab a rosette on the other fellow’s right
shoulder from the left hand side, is the sort of
thing that should become intensely popular in
the back-blox if the Australian is only half as
horsey as he is cracked up to be. For the rest,
the lady who juggles on horse-back with knives,
firebrands, and other things, reels and whirls
on her giddy way, and the tiny ponies fight to a
finish, smiting each other with great dexterity
on the chest and point, but finding it difficult to
reach the solar-plexus— the corn-sack. At leaps
and handsprings and general ability to cast them-
selves through the air over several horses and an
elephant, Mr. Salvini and his merry men do won-
ders. It makes one tired to think of all the brain-
toil that must have been expended on all those
brutes to make them appear as intelligent as the
ordinary man, and much more so than some men
one sees dodging the tram-cars in George-street.
This last refers to the performing animals, and
not to Mr. Salvini’s men.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Arthur Crane’s “ I
Trust You Still” floats over the jasper river that
shimmers through the city of haze, and, altogether,
Crane is the soulful element of the Tivoli just
now. Tod Callaway’s dreary song about some-
thing being b-e-a-u-t-i-f-u-1 may pass without fur-
ther comment. Austin Rudd is as refreshing as a
whisky-and-soda after a cold-tea party. The
newest feature is the American Bioscope, by
which the audience is taken for a ride over the
Lake railway, Hong-kong—a line with some re-
markably steep grades and rather pleasant
scenery. There are also views of London, from
which one gathers that King Edward’s metropolis
consists mostly of ’busses carrying ads. for Li pton’s
teas. Swarms of people and vast piles of
buildings fill in the background, but the ’busses
and their flaring ads. are aggressive. The very
best animated picture is the cat’s head which the
little boy saw through the magnifying glass; but
the one that pleases the audience most is that of
the mystic wrestlers who tear each other to pieces
and put the fragments together again by a process
of resurrection made easy.
« it # *
“Turned-up,” played by the Sydney
Comedy Club at the Queen’s Hall, one night last
week, relates to the vicissitudes of a certain
married couple who have both married again—the
wife because she considers her sailor husband
safely disposed of in the deep, deep sea, and the
husband from pure cussedness. Consequently,
when he returns to rebuke his missus for bigamy,
he is rebuked by his new black wife, who follows
him. Therefore a dark cloud of suspicion rests on
him, as does the box-lid on his first wife’s second
husband—a son having decided that the best way
out of the difficulty is to conceal him in that
piece of furniture. Whenever a wrong husband
or brother turns up on the stage, the other
characters invariably think the best thing to do is
to hide him in a box, though a moment’s thought
would convince them that he can’t remain there
permanently, and the trouble will be just as bad
when he comes out. The returned husband’s
second wife is alleged to be a Kaffir, but she is
dressed like an American negress and yabbers
Australese. The first wife’s taste has also gone
to black of another type, her second husband
being Carraway Bones, undertaker. Peace is
restored by the two accidental spouses, so to
speak, marrying each other, and the grown-up son
and daughter marrying other people without
further worry as to the number of their parents.
It is the sort of play that ought to go fast and
furiously, and therefore it is the play which an
amateur company, which is generally slow and
peaceful, always insists on playing.
Miss OLIVE KINGSTON , a Melbourne Songbird.
Jacotin, Photo., Paris .
Herewith The Bulletin publishes the
photo., taken in Paris last year, of a sweet young
Melbourne songstress. Some few months have
elapsed since Miss Olive Kingston returned from
the Gay Capital, where she studied awhile at
L’Ecole Marchesi, in company with Regina Nagel
and Amy Castles. The Marchesi system proved
very successful in her case. A high mezzo-
soprano, with a pure, flexible voice, she had been
accepted as a comparative artist among amateurs
ere she went to Europe, and a year’s tuition from
Madame served to bring out her charming voice
and give her the confidence of her ability. The
initials of Miss Olive Kingston’s name speak the
truth. She is O.K. Melbourne expects to feel
proud of the clever vocalist who makes her first
professional appearance at the Gerardy Concerts
in the Town Hall.
** * *
The strange, family-vault drama wherein
Dampier restored himself to Melbourne notice at
the Bijou has passed away like a dream, and
“ Black Flag” waves in its stead. The banner of
gloom is casually alluded to as a bit of bunting
which flies from the masthead at Portland prison
every time a convict gets out upon the loose. It
doesn’t flutter within view of the audience, but in-
asmuch as two felons go forth from durance vile
and join a picnic party in a cave hard by there
can be no doubt that the Flag pops up somewhere
during the proceedings. One of the illicit pic-
nickers is an innocent Dampier, sentenced to a
long term of captivity for hitting a long-winded
capitalist on the head. The blow was really dealt
by Dampier’s half-brother, and the other convict,
who wears a false nose, is aware of this fact.
Likewise, a fresh, ingenious youth pervades the
drama, protesting all the while that Dampier was
wrongfully sentenced. After he has been shot by
a brutal interloper the youth returns to life and
brings the bad half-brother in sorrow to the
Criminal Court. Meanwhile, Miss Lily Dampier
has repented of her foolishness in giving evidence
against the good man of her heart. She helps to
repair her error by rowing one convict to the pic-
nic in her little boat, and saving the other from a
watery swim, but on Saturday night something
went wrong with these exploits, and the curtain
fell inopportunely.
* • • *
“The Black Flag ” is a dull, unintellectual
entertainment, and Dampier makes a mistake in
staging it. As the innocent Bill, of Portland, he
is required to fight a series of encounters with four
warders, who fall down when he deals them
a gentle push. Dampier doesn’t shine
in such struggles, neither does he escape
with alacrity when the four men have in-
tentionally lost their balance. Master Fred
Dampier, as the youth with the long-lost evidence,
is a bright, promising novice, and John Forde,
the comic convict, provokes a few kindly smiles.
But the humor of Forde pale 3 into -significance
whilst the ocean tide comes creeping in creakily
upon the sandy margin of the picnic. The ripples
make their approacli in the manner of a window-
blind, and retire up-hill in jerks. No previous
ocean has ever seemed so much in need of oil upon
the troubled apparatus.
• • * •
Dampier produces his thoughtful and in-
teresting Shylock of Venice on Friday evening,
and if he is well advised he will keep Shakespeare
going for longer than one consecutive night. The
chances are that the immortal Israelite who
sharpens his knife on his boot would be an accept-
able item for at least a week.
* * w
At Her Majesty’s (Melb.), the strictly un-
true story of Napoleon and his Josephine will be
heard no more after this (Thursday) evening. On
Friday night the theatre is to be closed for a final
burial of the men who, for all public purposes, died
at Waterloo, and on Saturday the first performance
of J. C. W’s Italian Opera co. falls due. Great
things are expected of the musical organisation
from Italy. The principals are said to be worthy of
the 48 Australian voices engaged for the chorus, and
the 41 instrumentalists for conductor Hazon
beats the air. Also, the scenery and fixings have
a private reputation of the highest Williamsonian
character. If other things prove to be all that
rumor promises, the price-list will get general
approval, for it goes no higher than six shillings,
with a minimum of two “bob.” Moreover, the
seats at 6s. and 4s. can be purchased for a shilling
less in lots of 40, and this concession to the class
of people who love to consign themselves to the
theatre in wholesale quantities is already showing
good results. The season opens with Verdi’s
“Aida.”
** * *
The marble stairs of Rickards’ new Opera
House are sustaining the heavy tread of innumer-
able Melbourne feet these times, and the popular
opinion re Marie Lloyd, and the animated first-
part, and other features of the bill is quite favor-
able. Marie is considered to be artistically warm
in her songs, but not too sultry. Her coster gal
has a genuine appearance, and her salt-sea bath-
ing song earns many larfs for the droll little party.
Mdlle. Bobe, the xylophoneiste, is another im-
portant favorite, but alas ! the music which this
remarkable performer belts from an unorthodox
instrument is not charming. Why devote an ad-
mirable talent to the xylophone 1
“ Women and Wine ” looks good enough
for another week or two at Melbourne Royal,
although none of the characters look good, with
the exception of Miss Frances Ross and the mild
old blind father who walks around Paris with an
outstretched hand turned downwards. The aver-
age blind gentleman in the street puts his hand
the other side up to catch the sunbeams and aught
else that may greet his palm, but Walter Baker’s
stage parent is a cultured person, depending for
means of subsistence on the flower-trade of a
prettily-gowned angel in a flopsome hat. Nobody
can fathom the commercial mystery of Miss
Ross’s life. She buys half-a-dozen penny bunches
of flowers in the early morning, and offers them
all day long to a non-purchasing public. When a
lurid demi-mondaine proposes to “tip” the wan-
derer handsomely, she rejects the piece of gold
because it burns her, she says. And yet she can
afford to keep herself and the old man clean and
beautiful every evening until further notice.
** * #
At tie Town Hall the Dolores (Trebelli)
song recitals are presumably off for the present,
seeing that Gerardy, the ’cellist, is about to have
the loan of the vast municipal apartment. Mdlle.
Dolores has warbled French songs, old and new
English ballads, and Italian operatic airs, and her
work has been such a sweet artistic success from
first to last that Melbourne will not be happy
till it hears her again. A brilliant soprano singer,
whose personality is as charming as her voice, the
placid Dolores seems fairly entitled to be called
Great. In fact, the present scribe considers her
Greater than the Duchess.
To-night (Thursday) is to find Jean
Gerardy, the famous violincellist, appealing to the
alleged musical taste of Melbourne at the Town
Hall. This very sudden and unexpected stranger
will have the support of Edward Scharff at the
piano, and a new vocalist —Miss Olive Kingston—
will make her first appearance on the concert
platform.

A Chinese opera is preparing at a place in
Exhibition-street, Melbourne, opposite to Her
Majesty’s Theatre. Hitherto this mysterious
corner house—corner of Lilly Bulke-street—has
been noted only for the bursts of applause that
issued from the top-floor on Saturday nights, when
Bill Blinker and Natty Ninkum, or similar
“talent” were understood to be bringing off a
“ scrap.” But the noises which disturb the neigh-
borhood these times are evidently the work of a
Celestial orchestra. They go on for hours at a
stretch, without the least variation. To unedu-
cated ears the first bar of a Chow opera is identical
with the last bar, and all the intervening bars

placed an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany for a pair of powerful winding engines to haul from
2000 feet. These engines are rapidly approaching com-
pletion, and will be delivered about first of next month.
The same firm of engineers have also supplied the Occi-
dental mine, Cobar, with a handsome pair of winding
engines, as well as a boiler. This machinery was delivered
in very quick time and should give great satisfaction.*

The Argus of 24th October, 1900, writes re the
Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society, Limited : “ The
affairs of the Society in all material respects are based on
a sound footing.”*
The Mount Drysdale Gold Mining Co. has
recently installed a rock-drilling plant, which should be
the means of largely increasing the output of this
property. Following the lead set by the Great Cobar
Copper Mining Syndicate, and other mines in the district,
it has adopted the Ingersoll-Sergeant type of drills and
compressor. The boiler is of the most modern return-
tubular pattern, and the whole plant was supplied by
Parke and Lacy Company, Limited, the Ingersoll agents.

Particulars have come out of the ’Murkan lead
“ combine,” built on the plans of the ditto copper ditto.
But there is this difference: whereas the U.S. bosses 50
per cent, of the world’s copper, it has its claw on only 25
Eer cent. of the lead. Yet, the fact that there is a com-
ine, and that its promoters have been squeezing out-
siders in, gives Australian lead producers more hope.
According to Dr. R. E. Roth, recently
returned to Sydney from Europe, all teachers
appointed to English Board-schools are now
required to be able to swim, and therefore, pre-
sumably, to teach the natatory art.
Dublin Weekly Independent (13th April)
says, in reference to the recent Melbourne floods :
“ The two towns of Richmond and Melbourne,
situated on opposite banks of the River Yarra,
have been submerged. A large iron bridge con-
necting the two towns was carried away by
immense ice floes.” What a blessed thing it is to
have sound, reliable information concealed about
you !
From the letter of a M.L. officer in
S’Africa; —
History tells us of nations that have fought for inde-
pendence. Probably never has a people fought with so
much tenacity as the Boer. He knows the value of the
country that is slowly being taken from him, and is
fighting with the courage of despair. . . . The Boer
—the average Boer —is not as black as he is painted.
About a month ago, when we had just taken the position
we occupy, four of the I.L.H. were captured. The Boers
took them to their laager, gave them a good meal and a
tot of rum (probably out of the convoy captured from
Clements), and sent them back to us.

now. (Some people might say——but no matter.)
Some of it is a little rich for the diet of a family
journal, but Pearson’s prints it without a mur-

It was the passion which belongs of right (the right of
Nature) to the wife, not the passion of the wanton. Such
a woman could snatch her husband’s rifle from the wall
and defend her honor, saving the last cartridge for her-
self, but she could never yield to any man’s blandish-
ments, no matter how strong the current of her blood
might run ; and, believe me, there are many such women
on God’s green earth, more women than men, my masters.

Thank heaven, one returned contingenter
has got his reward, anyhow. Witness this, from
Melbourne Argus, May 18 :
Tatura, Friday.—Private James Lockwood, who left
here with the second Victorian contingent for South
Africa about 17 months ago, was accorded a most en-
thusiastic welcome by the townspeople on his return
home last night. Although the train did not arrive until
nearly 11 o’clock, he was met at the railway station by a
number of riflemen, the fire brigade, and a combined band
from Murchison and Tatura, as well as hundreds of towns-
people. As the train entered the station the band played
“ See the Conquering Hero Comes,” and cheers were
given. He was on arrival carried to a chariot, which was
in waiting. A torchlight procession was then formed
(the chariot being drawn by several young men), and pro-
ceeded to the Mechanics’ Institute. On the way volleys
were fired and fireworks displayed. On arrival at the
Mechanics’ Institute, after the audience had sung “ Home,
Sweet Home,” addresses of welcome and congratulations
were delivered by the Rev. A. Chambers, Messrs. Bazeley
and Calder. Private Lockwood suitably responded.
Several patriotic songs and selections by the band were
given during the evening.
They must have mistaken him for Wellington
returning after Waterloo.

are built on exactly the same line of discord. Yet,
if you question a complacent Lin Fat, or Wung
Wing, as to why any rehearsals of this dull
monotony are needed, he smiles in much the same
pitying manner that Marshall Hall adopts
towards Philistines. There must be something m
these works of the great Chow masters. A Euro-
pean, in his denseness, concludes that the artist
who hits the tom-tom with a stick is merely
under contract to make one kind of noise mechani-
cally, regardless of consequences, until the
conductor bids him cease. But probably a bang
on the abdomen of a heathen drum, at one par-
ticular point of the tumult, means half a page of
descriptive hysteria such as Prof. Hall publishes
on his Wagnerian programmes. It is, percha,nce,
the cry of an agonised soul in a paroxysm of re-
morse. Or it signifies the first streak of day-
dawn, followed by a drowsy awakening of Nature
in a sullen mood. We don’t know what it means.
Cheong Lee Beethoven, the composer, and his
girtinacious interpreters at the corner of Lilly
ulke-street —they know.

The 10 per cent, dividend of the Bank of New. South
Wales appears to have become a permanent institution
again. Profits are equal to £6 2s. 3d. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds. The B.N.S.W. has now
£21,590,076 of deposits ; nearly a million of note circula-
tion, and it has the stupendous cash reserve of £5,891,673;
while cash and liquid assets together amount to over
£8,000,000. It easily keeps its place as the boss bank of
Australasia, and it also ranks very high among the banks
of the world. Among about 150 banks enumerated by
Whitaker’s Almanac as doing business in London, only
12 have larger reserves, and even such national institu-
tions as the Bank of Ireland and Bank of Scotland can’t
approach it in this respect. Australia may have a good
many doubtful institutions, political, financial, and per-
sonal, but its B.N.S.W. seems able to fairly claim that it
is beyond suspicion.

Investor : The Bank of Adelaide’s 8 per cent,
dividend represents about 6s. sd. per share annually, or
about 3s. 24d. per share each half-year. Only a few banks
state their dividend at so much per share.

The profits are equal to £7 6s. Bd. per cent, per annum
on the shareholders’ funds as they were at the beginning
of the half-year (capital, £lOO,OOO ; reserves, £221,368 =
£321,368). The Westralian Bank pegs away at building
up its reserves with a persistence which can’t be too much
commended, and it pays the largest banking dividend in
Australasia. At the same time, Westralia isn’t what it
was, and the profits of Westralia’s one big bank aren’t
what they were either; and though the reserves continue
to accumulate, they do it with less than the old velocity.
During the half-year just finished the business
of the Westralian Bank has shown steady progress. De-
posits are now £1,735,005, an increase of £111,569. Of
these no less than £1,006,302 are current accounts, bear-
ing no interest. The cash reserves are enormous—specie
and bullion, £1,013,051 ; and State securities, £47,400 ;
total, £l,o6o,4sl— equal to 55 per cent, on all liabilities to
the public. This seems a needlessly large reserve, and
why the Westralian Bank doesn’t invest at least another
£200,000 in interest-bearing State securities (not neces-
sarily Westralian securities) it is difficult to understand.
Even allowing for the fact that the bank’s gold-buying
business requires a great deal of coin always available,
the cash reserve looks large enough to be a serious waste
of money.

* * * *
The Wellington and Manawatu Railway Co. is
commonly held up, by reason of its undoubted success,
as a brilliant example of the blessings and advantages of
private, as compared with State, railway ownership.
Certainly the Maoriland State railways don’t earn £8 os.
fid. percent., and if the Wellington and Manawatu Co.
did this by its own efforts, and without charging any
more than Government rates, it ivould be a shining ex-
ample. But the co., like most of its kind, owes a great
deal of its success to State aid. The shareholders put up
£170,900 capital. The State aided them with land,
valued in the co.’s balance-sheet at £96,570, and by mak-
ing them a present of a bit of unfinished line valued
(also in the co.’s balance-sheet) at £51,838. This means
that the shareholders put up only £170,000 and received
State aid to the extent of 1148,408. The rest of the
money (£680,000) was borrowed on debentures at 5 per
cent and the co. was able to raise the money partly by-
reason of having all this large State grant to give as
eeurity. Altogether the co.’s complaints about the
treatment it has received from the State re taxation, &c.,
don’t appear to rest on any very massive foundation. In
fact, it appears to have made a much better bargain with
the public than the public did with it.
The Northern Steamship Co. (Auckland, Maori-
land) shows a profit of £5094 for the year ended 31st
March, 1901. A dividend at the rate of 7 per cent, per
annum absorbs £4567, leaving £527 to be added to the
reserves, subject to any deductions which might be made
at the annual meeting by way of fees voted to the direc-
tors. (It appears that the directors of the Northern
S.S. 00. receive no fixed remuneration, but are dependent
on the good feeling of the annual meeting.) Paid-up
capital is £69,642, and reserves are £40,638 (insurance
fund, £32,554 ; reserve for boilers and repairs, £6647; and
undivided profit, £1437).

Ballarat Woollen and Worsted Co. (Ballarat,
Victoria) shows a profit of £2lBl for the half-year to 31st
March, 1901, and a dividend at the rate of 15 per cent,
per annum absorbs £2249. This leaves the co. with total
reserves amounting to £17,469 —a very solid figure for a
small concern with a total paid-up capital of £29,924.
At the same time, the policy of paying away more than
the earnings in order to keep up the very large dividend
of 15 per cent, is wholly objectionable.

The Tasmanian Loan, Guarantee and Finance
Co. (Hobart) is a very small Tasmanian institution which
seems to strike a rather continuous patch of only moder-
ate luck. Some figures :
Dividends and Total
Profits. Dividend Tax. Reserves.
September, 1898 .. £2572
March, 1899 .. £342 4 per cent. = £315 2599
September, 1899 .. 349 4 „ = 315 2633
March, 1900 .. 351 4 „ = 315 2669
September, 1900 .. 314 4 „ = 315 2698
March, 1901 .. 375 5 „ = 394 2679
The Tasmanian Loan, Guarantee and Finance Co. has a
paid-up capital of £15,000. Its reserves consist of depre-
ciation fund, £2600, and undivided profit, £79. Its chief
investments are £19,965, lent on mortgage. If the de-
preciation fund is really wanted for depreciation pur-
poses, to cover losses, or probable losses, on these
mortgages (and as the co. suspended payment of divi-
dends for a considerable time to build up this fund, it may
be reasonably inferred that it was urgently wanted to
cover actual or very probable losses) then the only real
reserve is the £79 above mentioned. Under all the cir-
cumstances the policy of increasing the dividend to £l9
more than the earnings looks very unwise. The earnings
for the half-year are only equal to £4 4s. 9d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ capital and total reserves. If
the mortgages are all perfectly sound, and paying full
interest, and there is no depreciation to write off, the
return seems very low.
* m • *
A plain, true tale. A certain building society had
large overdraft. The bank called on it to pay up. The
society, then in extremis, offered a mortgage on its city
premises, not worth nearly the amount of the overdraft.
Bank refused with scorn. A director and large share-
holder of the bank was also director of a certain finance
co. He recommended his co. to invest the amount re-
quired to clear the bank overdraft, in the
proposed mortgage. This was done. The money
so applied was almost the entire fortune
of an orphan girl, which had been placed with
the finance co. for safe investment. The bank got its
money—which was good for the aforementioned director,
he being a large shareholder. The director also got the
usual commission on the investment. The finance co.
lost nothing. The building society broke, however, and
the girl has a great hulking block of offices which yields
her about lj- per cent, on her money.

Out of this large writing down £7500 is described as

vision for bad and doubtful debts, the appropriation of
such amount, or portion of it, to be dealt with at a
future date,” but as the £7500 wholly disappears
out of the balance - sheet it is written off so far
as that document is concerned. The total debts,
before this amount was written off, were only £56,373,
and it is difficult to suppose that so large a proportion of
them could have gone bad, or partially bad, all in one act,
and quite unexpectedly. And if the loss didn’t come
unexpectedly—if it has been foreseen for any considerable
time—then some of the 10 per cent, dividends were
wholly unjustifiable. When the big drop in the profits
arrived, in 1894, the dividend should have been corres-
pondingly reduced. The management did not face this
necessity, and the consequence is a great disappearance
among the reserves.

« * * *
After a long silence, Victoria again hears the
persuasive voice of the Mount Deddick gentleman. What
that gentleman (his name is Sheddon) is really after
passes all reasonable comprehension, unless he simply
wants a concession to sell at a profit to the confiding
British investor, and thinks that almost any kind of a
concession will do. Mount Deddick is a casual kind of
mountain in Gippsland, up towards the N.S.W. border.
It lies on the borders of two large counties with a joint
population of about 5000. The Deddick region has been
harried for years by prospectors, who have decided very
unanimously that it is a poor field. Mr. Sheddon, so
far as is known, has found nothing there that seems even
remotely payable; reports describe Deddick as looking
just as bad as ever it did. Yet Sheddon has, for about
two years, assured the Victorian Government that he
is prepared to float a company in England which will
open up the great mineral wealth of the region, and also
build a railway from the coast to Deddick—a line through
90 miles of barren mountains where there can be no pas-
senger traffic worth speaking of, and where there is
practically no agricultural produce to carry. The line is
simply to fetch down to the coast the inexhaustible
riches of Deddick —which no Victorian mining authority
can find to have any riches worth removal. Sheddon
wanted a grant of a strip of land for his railway to stand
on, and the astonished Victorian Government proposed
that his co. should deposit £lO,OOO as proof that it really
meant to build a railway, so that it could lay the matter
before Parliament without being suspected of playing a
ridiculous hoax on that august body. Sheddon pro-
mised that the deposit should be forthcoming, and after
a dead silence of nine months he promises it again, by
cable from England this time. The deposit doesn’t
materialise, but the cable is satisfactory as showing that
a very hopeful and interesting person isn’t dead yet. To
all appearance Sheddon is either a very hilarious joke,
or else he is a person who wants to get a concession
for building a railway to somewhere, even if it is only a
useless and uninhabited mountain, with a view to selling
his rights to someone else. There is no reason to believe
that the right of building a railway to Deddick is
worth any more than the right to carry all the rocks
on the east coast of Australia over to the west coast of
Australia. If it was built it would hardly do Victoria any
good. A company could hardly be floated to build it
unless some most unjustifiable things were said in the
prospectus about the awful Deddick country. If the
Victorian Government contradicted these necessary state-
ments it would wreck the Deddick enterprise ; and if it
didn’t contradict them it would be accused afterwards of
helping, for a sinister purpose, to ruin British widows and
orphans by letting them put their money into the Deddick
“cat.” Altogether, Victoria had better decide to have
nothing to do with Sheddon.
* * * •
At the South Mine, at Broken Hill, on Friday,
24th May, a sudden and totally unpremonstrated fall of
earth entombed six men. A relief party immediately set
to work, and two of the bodies were soon recovered ; but
subsequent falls of stuff rendered operations so danger-
ous and tedious that all the corpses were not unearthed—
after ceaseless labour in four-hour shifts —until Sunday
morning. All the unfortunates were crushed almost out
of recognition. Four of them were married, and leave
behind them, in all, 16 children. Truly Broken Hill is a
great grim factory for making of widows —grass and
otherwise, and the need for a compulsory and universal
life insurance system for all workers is very great.
* * ♦ *
B.H. Junction has got its six months’ suspen-
sion of labor conditions ; and the local Municipal Council
held a special meeting (only Laborites attending) to
denounce it. But as the co. was going oyer £4OO to the
bad every week, and hadn’t even paid a div. with lead at
£l7 10s. (it is now £l2 ss. or so), it isn’t easy to see what
else the Minister could do. The last div. the co. paid
was in the first months of ’97 ; since then it has paid a
quarter of a million in wages, so that Broken Hill town
has done better than the shareholders by a long chalk.
• * * *
The immediate future of the British B.H. has
been hanging in the scales the last fortnight or so. The
“colonial” directors, it is said, wanted to shut up the
mill and do only much-needed prospecting ; the London
Board favored going on, exercising economies wherever
possible. Among which economies a further cut in
wages is feared. But the other co.’s won’t move without
the lead of the B.H. Prop. ; and the big co. isn’t feeling
the pinch like some.
* * • •
Block 14’s half-yearly report, to March 31, is as
exasperatingly short of detail as usual. It sticks out,
however, that, in Order to pay the two divs., amounting
to £lO,OOO, it was necessary to reduce the surplus of
liquid assets by £8783. The brutal fact, therefore, is that
the profit only reached £1217. And that was with silver
at about present price and lead (of which the co. won
between 4000 and 5000 tons from its own mine) worth over
£2 10s. more than it is at present. Still, that doesn’t
mean that the co. is going back now at the rate of £12,000
or £15,000 a half-year. The directors explain : “ Your
directors have persistently pursued the policy of bringing
the large bodies of low-grade ore which exist in the mine
within the scope of treatment; but the present lovv prices
of lead, zinc, and silver have obliged them to curtail their
efforts in this direction, and to work on a smaller scale
with richer ore, thus reducing the output of crude ore by
about one-half.”
• * # *
The turn-out of several hundred men from the
Broken Hill mines would lead to the co-operative work-
ing of a good many little outside silver and copper shows,
if there were a smelter handy. It is proposed, therefore,
to try and get the State to set up a plant, which would
be useful, too, in connection with the fine technical college
now being built at Broken Hill.

just completed experimental crushing to ascertain value
of large ore bodies in northern levels and stupes above,
where reef is 6ft. thick. Result—362 tons yielded 1119 oz.
melted gold; being very satisfactory. Fortnightly clean
up of Golden Gate 3 and 4 South crashing yielded 1047|0z.
from 267 tons. Rogers’ Golden Gate (Bull co.) crushing
again for good returns. Golden Gate and Nancy Lee
United just completing crushing of 500 tons for ljoz. per
ton yield. Diamond drill down about 950 ft. on mission
to find lost Iguana reef ; struck quartz recently in pro-
mising country. Fresh capital being arranged for to
sink Morgan’s Block shaft, with aid of adequate pumping
appliances. Water, beat the management when within
supposed measurable distance of lode. Shares held
largely on the Towers. Prospects of field improving
daily. Last month’s average yield 3oz. 6dwt. per ton.

“ Poor Duchess : ” Consider these daily-
paper reports :
At Port Melbourne: “ The Mayoress . . . gracefully
handed the bouquet trophy to the Princess, who smiled,
smelt the flowers, and thanked Mrs. Edwards.”
At Seymour: “Two little mites presented a basket of
beautiful roses. . . . The Duchess, who graciously ac-
cepted the gift,” ...
At Euroa : “ A little girl presented a magnificent bou-
quet of flowers.”
At Benalla : “ A little child was lifted up and handed
in a bouquet of York roses and violets.”
At Aliiury : “ Her It >yal Highness was presented with
a, oouquet and basket of flowers, and expressed gratifica-
tion at receiving the gifts.”
There is a chronic legend that all royal ladies are
passionately fond of flowers. Yet they must
heave the bouquets out of the window after the
train starts, else they would be smothered under
the accumulation.

Clarence, in 1881. The trees are both growing well, but
the latter tree requires a great deal of attention, for
when the Prince who planted it died it seemed to lose its

another tree.

, T ]l e syndicate that'ran the erand-stand at
the Melbourne Anglican Cathedral in connection
with the recent drunkery left the place in a
hideous, sordid mess. After the timber work of
the stand was removed, it was found that the
ground around the sacred edifice was littered
deep with lunch-papers, beer bottles, broken fruit
orange-peels, and rags. The beautiful lawns that
at one time surrounded the Cathedral had disap-
peared, having been trampled into the earth by
the workmen and attendants, and it will cost the
Cathedral authorities most of the price they got
fiom the syndicate to put the place in order again.

According to the Age reporter, who
travelled by the “ York ” train from Melbourne
to Brisbane, there was no doubt that G-od Almighty
made a bit of a demonstration in the Jook’s honor.
As the train rushed past Wodonga platform,
“the sun was setting grandly in the west, with
gold broadening into red, and chastened by a
streak or two of regal purple. A haze had settled
down over the tree-clad hills. The Duke and
Duchess were departing with the sun.” And God
said-—“ Let there be no more light.”

The parson is not content with the threepenny
bit of the healthy live believer; he pushes the hat
under the nose of the dying, and bangs his plate
on the tombstone of the dead.
* • * •
After all, the Brisbane dirty chicken came
home to roost in due time on the bosoms of the
ratepayers. For a score of years they returned
aldermen who permitted good old private entev-
prise to attend to sanitary matters, and left their
undrained town a prey to Microbe and co. Now,
the Plague resides in Brisbane, and refuses to be
evicted ; the Admiral declines to bring his nice
clean ships to the dirty port; the Duke cuts his
visit short, and Brisbane is struck on its tenderest
grovel. The Brisbane Telegraph says franti-
cally that this is another of the dreadful results of
Federation. Its idea evidently is that Sydney
and Melbourne conspired to borrow the fleet to
Brisbane’s undoing, and that the Duke really
wanted to come and stay a long time with Bris-
bane’s Plague-germ, only he was frustrated by a
dark and unprincipled plot.

o * * *
Detective Roche, the Blue Mountain man,
and hero of numberless sensational arrests, and
Detective Rochaix, the linguist, were put on to
guard York while in N.S.W. Rochaix has the
best detective nose for sly grog in the service ;
but, after all, it was neither a fighter, like Roche,
or grog-smeller, like Rochaix, who had much chance
to be of any real use in such a case. A man like
Lombroso, the mad doctor, a student of cranks, a
man with a genius for spotting abnormalities, is
wanted for work like that. Only a lunatic, in
some stage of lunacy, could see anything in
damaging Duke York, and the man who can
diagnose the approaching lunatic comes in useful
at such times.
* * * *
The experiment of getting a band of
alleged Australian stockmen—mainly collected
from the suburbs—to gambol before the Dukan-
duchess in Melbourne was certainly not inspired
by pleasant recollections of an experience of the
Duke of Edinburgh, in Colac, long ago. Genuine
stockmen were gathered in great numbers from
the Western-district runs to give H.R.H. a
rattling welcome, and the men dashed up to the
royal carriage as they might have charged a
break-away, cracking their whips and yelling
“Coo-ee!” with all their lung-power. Some of
them lost their heads, and said other things
besides “Coo-ee ! ” The display was well-meant,
but it missed the ’bus, for Edinburgh was
frightened almost out of his senses, and marked
his royal displeasure by passing through Colac
sitting as rigid as a brick chimney, and staring
blankly before him. Colac was disgusted, and
followed H.R.H. out of the town with observa-
tions which also were not “ Coo-ee !’’—observa-
tions of a kind that would turn the loyalists of
to-day green with horror.

* * • *
The “Long-Haired Man” of Melbourne
—that being the common business sign and trade-
mark of Paul Perrier, palmist—struck trouble
recently. The worst trouble arose over an alleged
theft of 10s., but a minor difficulty related to a
question of 2s. 6d. One of the Long-Haired Man’s
lady clients alleged that after he had read her
future for the ordinary fee he held on affectionately
to her hand, and she had to pay him 2s. 6d. to let
go. The Bulletin here started to think so hard
over the inequality of things that it forgets how
the case ended. Just consider ! The world is
full of unappreciated women and spinsters and
ungathered blossoms—whose hands no men have
squeezed ; and here is a woman whose hand is in
such demand that she has to pay a man 2s. 6d. to
stop squeezing it.
There are some queer women round
Castlemaine (Vic.) way. A little while back a
young woman from those parts calmly labelled
herself a person of poor character by registering
the birth of a baby that never existed—in fact,
there never appeared to have been either infant
or father or “fall ” or anything. Lately, another
Castlemaine woman, who already had a three-
year-old youngster, went to Brighton and obtained
another small incubus, took it home, called in a
midwife, and went through all the outward
formalities attached to the birth of an infant
already 10 days old. This she passed off on her
husband as his own offspring and everything went
smoothly till the real mother called for her child—
when there were “explanations.” In the mean-
time the infant died—probably from being born
too frequently. The old man’s opinion of his
wife’s little caper has not, so far, transpired.
* * * *
The police authorities protest too hard
that the extraordinary precautions taken in
searching the Ormuz were not prompted by dread
of an anarchist plot aiming at royalty. The
instructions apparently were: “Let no Italians
ashore ” ; anyhow, it was the Italian passengers
who were most particularly scrutinised. In fact,
they are still kept under the zealous eye of the
law. The action, it is said, was prompted by an
anonymous letter received by the police, who
appear to have run against a very dull hoax and
fallen in.

As I wandered

“ What a pity

LOW HUMOR.
It was a young Australian bard
Was walking down the street;
The way through life no more was hard
For his poetic feet,
For there would soon appear a neat
First volume of his verses sweet.
He met a friend ; they had a drink ;
He told the news—said he :
“This book of mine will run, I think,
Into editions three.”
“ Good,” said the friend ; “ no doubt they’ll be—
Er—published simultaneously.”
C.R.

“JS.”: The Salvation Army began in
Australia only 18 years ago_, without a
single roof over its head, owning not a
ha’porth of property. “Major” Barker
went on Oollingwood Flat like the •
foxes to their holes, and the
Hallelujah donkey - rider, “Capt.”
Gore, as the Adelaide pioneer, had
to do the same thing.
Now look at the enor-
mous property of

rather Booth
Solus, all over

isation in Melb.l
The Army pro-
perty in and
around Melb.
alone, is
honestly worth
£100,000. With
all this, they
are cadging
pitiably every-
where, every
night. L will
foot up their
whole Australian

tor of a million.
Paupers in 1883,

* »
The member-
ship roll of N.S.W.
Typographical Asso-
ciation stands now
at 450. This must
leave many times
that number outside.
In Sydney and sub-
urbs wholesale sweat-
ing in the type-setting
business is openly car-
ried on. If the 450 union
hands would put a vigor-
ous shoulder to the legis-
lative wheel they could
compel a minimum-wage
over the entire printing field
of—at all events—Sydney and
suburbs. At present the firms that pay
union rates and live up to union hours
are forced into unfair competition
with firms that scoff at both. The
Union should fight for straight employ-
ers by starting a crusade against the
crooked ones. There are compositors in
Sydney, not apprentices but one-time members
of the Typo. Association, working 50 hours a week
for 27s. 6d.

“ The women and their needlework,”
writes Mrs. Hirst Alexander, in United Aus-
tralia, “are as fixed a feature of the Maori-
land House of Representatives as are the Repre-
sentatives themselves. Just below the gallery
runs a sort of trough of green baize. ‘ What
is it for ? ’ I asked, on my first visit to the House
* Oh ! ’ was the reply, ‘ it is just to catch anything
that may happen to fall from the gallery. Some-
times the ladies drop their thimbles and knitting-
needles.’ I looked over and beheld several bald
and shining pates, and quite comprehended the
necessity for their protection.”

A casual letter:
Dear Bulletin.—'To paraphrase an old hjann, “The
world in solemn stillness paused to hear the Duchess
snore.” Last Sunday things along the Northern railway
line were only pretty middling. By order of the Railway
Commissioners, the universe held its breath while a train
conveying the heir to the British Crown stood for a brief
space'on the loop near Homebush. The very roosters
hushed their morning challenge .by request, officials
moved about with velvet feet, and a stalwart policeman
from Burwood urged a small nurse and a baby which had
strayed near the scene with a perambulator to keep dead
silence. One rank old billygoat was the only unawed
creature on God’s earth, and his remarks were quitr
unfit for publication and ultra-democratic. An enginr
which wheezed fearfully in the vicinity was ear
marked for future investigation, and the loco, superin-
tendent was observed to jot nasty remarks down in his
book about the engine-driver. A near approach enabled
the timorous visitor to discern that, while the Juke s
party slept, numbers of disguised detectives and press-
men kept watch, and bowed themselves in reverence when
the chorus of snores mounted up into the clear morning
air from the wonderful carriage which has cost the Stat/>
so much. Things will take some time to wilt down mt
their customary condition up Strathfield and Hornsl
way.— Norah.

For sex-disproportion in a fairly-compacb
settlement can this record of White Cliffs opal-
mining camp be beaten in N.S.W. —Males, 1068 ;
females, 474 ? For all-Australia, excluding the
mainly-pastoral outback of Queensland and S.A.,
Peak Hill goldfield (W.A.) probably holds the
record—sßß males to 83 of the other sort.
According to a paper read before the
Royal Society of Queensland by Sydney G-.
Martin, phthisis in that State kills annually
19 out of every 10,000 population, but, if
Polynesians be excluded, the rate sinks
to 10 per 10,000. One half of the Q.
Kanaka death-rate is due to tuber-
culosis, while the rest nearly all arises
from pneumonia and dysentery.
Queensland is afflicted, like the rest
of Australasia, with a falling birth-
rate. Its most portentous out-
look, however, is the rapid in-
crease of cancer—especially cancer
in the stomach. Bananaland
death-rate from this cause is
just double the rate in
England.

Notwithstanding the efforts of N.S.W.
Govt, to give relatives ample chance to carry
away the family remains from Devonshire-
street cemetery, hosts complain of the Minister
responsible as a Hun and a Vandal. But the story
that the old cemetery is being trodden flat by the
pathetic feet of Japhet in search of a father has
little foundation. The general refusal of Japhet to
come for his father is the trouble, and what to do
with the discarded old man is the great difficulty.

In spite of the howl of indignation' at the
time of the great scalding accident at Red fern
station, the bars on the suburban railway carriage
windows have never been removed. Most of the
long carriages on the Sydney suburban lines are
veritable death-traps, if anything goes wrong.
Another point: The long corridor cars on N.S.W.
lines have two doors each —one at either end of the
car. The Yic. corridor cars have about six doors
apiece. * * *
Be several late chloroform-deaths in
Maoriland, the Christchurch Press reprinted The
Bulletin’s recent article (by a medical man) on
the subject of anaesthetics, with the result that an
ether-versus-chloroform controversy has arisen
amongst the local medicos. Opinions are, upon
the whole, in favor of The Bulletin contributor’s
contention that chloroform’s day as an anaesthetic
is nearly over.
* • • •
Those swashbucklers who went to South
Africa for the sake of the reception they were to
get on their return are meeting with bitter disap-
pointment. Hear the wail-ewm-boast of one of
them:
To the Editor op the Argos.
Sir,—We have been fighting in Africa for a year ; dur-
ing ten months we did not sit down at a table or sleep in
a bed. Our company can account for over a Boer per
man killed, besides wounded and prisoners. We were
continuously on outpost and scouting duty until
wounded or invalided. We arrived expecting at least a
welcome. Our comrades, who were unfortunately
obliged to come back months ago, without in many cases
having a chance to meet the enemy, we hear, received
some recognition. So far, we are strangers in a strange
land—no pay or welcome. The writer, with over £IOC
due to him, is, fortunately for himself and mates, a Jus-
tice of the Peace in his own colony, and has investments
in Melbourne, and considers it his duty to put his com-
rades’ case before the public, with 70 others invalided
home per s.s. Suevic.—Yours, &c., Trooper 207, Q.M.I.
As to the lack of cheers and beer and banners on
their return, a whole lot of these warriors de-
parted saying they were animated only by a stern
sense of duty. Not one of them said he was
animated by a hope of being kissed by many
strange girls on his return, or stipulated for a roar
of applause and free drink on landing. The arrears
of pay may be a real grievance, but the arrogant
demand for more public excitement is a demand
for something that wasn’t in the bond.
V * * *
Owing to the pronouncedly different views
held by N.S.W. Judges as to the right of a police-
man to ask an accused questions without having
first cautioned him, Parliament should legislate
one way or the other. Judge Cohen, the other
day, so strongly animadverted on police evidence
acquired without the caution, that the accused,
for that and other reasons, was acquitted. On
the other hand, the Chief Justice accepts such
evidence without a murmur. The present practice
is perplexing to the police, perplexing to the
accused, and perplexing to the jury.
* * * *
“ Orcus ” (a dentist) to The Bulletin
Re chloroform. I had a patient who insisted on having
her teeth extracted, although I strongly advised filling—
an operation too long for use of laughing-gas. An ex-
perienced medico whom I called in advised against
giving chloroform for a time, so I filled temporarily. But
the patient went to another dentist, and a medico ad-
ministered ether. The woman died four days after-
wards from acute pneumonia—in my opinion consequent
upon ether-administration.- The gist of the whole
matter : they die from chloroform at the time, but with
ether they die some days afterwards from pneumonia,
bronchitis, &c.
Maoriland Public
Health Officer has de-
cided that bodies of victims
of infectious diseases shall not hence-
forth be carried to the cemetery in vehicles
ordinarily used for that purpose. Point
is that a vehicle ordinarily used for some
innocent trade purpose will come into
requisition, and the germs will be distributed un-
officially along with eggs, millinery and road-
metal.

One of the saddest appeals that ever
struggled into print was that issued recently by a
West Maitland (N.S.W.) citizen signing himself
“Ironbark” “I.B.” wanted to know if the
Jook’s train could slow down a little as it passed,
through the town that sends o.x-merchant Brunker
to Parliament, and if arrangements could be made
for the ducal party to put its joint and several
head out of the window, and thereby get a large,
hot cinder in its eye, or strike its ear against a
tank, so that it couid be inspected by the loyal
inhabitants. (This made about the twentieth
appeal to the Jook to put his head out of the
window.) “ Ironbark ” finished his letter by say-
ing that, in the absence of the necessary arrange-
ments, and through the want of organisation and
public spirit, he feared West Maitland would have
to trust to chance for the “great honor” of the
Jook’s head being out of the window. As some
forgotten philosopher said: “There is as much
difference m some people as anybody.” Geelong
was enraged when the Jook agreed to stop only 15
minutes there, and when it was further suggested
that the train should pass through the town slowly,
and that George of Cornwall and York might be
visible at the window, the offer was denounced by
Geelong as a gross insult and an outrage. And
now West Maitland writes to the papers for the
husks that Geelong rejected with contumely.

SERENADE FROM ITALY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I think of thee all day, my beloved,
I think of thee all day !
The brown sails swing as the fisherfolk sing,
And the boats slide over the bay
With sunshine and tinkle of mandolines,
But thou art so far away ;
And I think of thee all day, my beloved,
I think of thee all day !
I dream of thee all night, my beloved,
I dream of thee all night 1
When on city walls the moonlight falls
So cold, and calm, and white ;
When sea and sky and the earth are still
I am with thee, Heart’s Delight,
For I dream of thee all night, my beloved,
I dream of thee all night!
My heart is all thine own, my beloved,
My heart is all thine own !
Whether sunlight glows on the lips of the rose
Or the Queen-moon’s on her throne.
In the land of laughter and light and love,
I linger alone, alone,
For my heart is all thine own, my beloved,
My heart is all thine own !
M.L.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, May 28, 1901.
My Dear Arini, —
The Mighty Atom and his Duchess were landed
at Farm Gove to the roaring of cannons, blasts of
music, and bending of bodies—concave bodies like
those of the Gov.-General, and convex bodies like
those of Messrs. Barton and See. His landing
was of the correct, time-honored type, and the
Mighty was affable, and signified his pleasure
with hand and hat-brim. But the loyalty of this
great country didn’t grow keener than it was the
day before yesterday. Now, if the Duke had
stepped ashore with that absurd cocked hat on
the back of his head and waving a bottle with the
saluting hand, all Australia, from Farm Cove to
Cape York, from State Minister to opossum-
snarer, would have loved him with a love surpass-
ing the love of our poet for the gum-tree. The
tie between us and the Mother Country would be
tied tighter (no, no pun intended) than ever it
was by contingents to South Africa hauling at it.
The Duke’s humanity, his nearness to us, would
have been proved. This is not my suggestion ; I
wouldn’t be so rude. It is an artist man’s belief.
And he added meditatively : “ What a splendid
advertisement it would be for me if my name was
on that bottle.”
During the long wait for the procession the
volunteers lining Castlereagh-street, opposite the
Australia, slouched at ease (every woman who
grew tired by standing to her dressmaker’s fittings
felt for those young men) and facetious persons in
the crowd behind them slyly spitted matchboxes
or scraps of paper on tempting bayonets.
National Guardsman Dibbs rode between the lines,
tn route to his own post, smoking an able-bodied
cigar. Same cigar the Prince of Wales gave him
years ago, doubtless. He had saved it up for this
day of days. But some elderly officers on the
stand near me went almost blind with rage at sight
of this breach of military etiquette. Soldiers—
soldiers padding the hoof and soldiers on pigskins,
and a band—then the Mighty Atom’s escort.
Then the Mighty themselves. He, a little weary
by now, and making the salute with half-closed
hand, as if he were picking bees off the brim of
his hat. She, looking very nice and fair, in black
coat and skirt, with violet posy, her light-brown
hair crimped under her small black toque; toque
stabbed through with initial hat-skewer. Now
the initial hatpin will be with us in great plenty.
With the passing of George and May went the
crowd’s interest, and, like a long, uninteresting
boa-constrictor, more soldiers trailed along.
The Yorks are said to be homely people, but
why glorified candlesticks and mantelpieces should
therefore be erected to their honor is inexplicable.
That is the impression some of the arches made
on the mind of this inkpotess. The gleam of the
white was not alluring ; it was cold, spectral,
dreary. The red and blue flags, gleaming against
the dark grey walls and the gloomy sky that
marked our latest Procession Day, had a rather
cheering effect ; not so the arches, with their not
particularly appropriate inscriptions—“ Peace on
Earth, ” and so forth. There is not likely to be
any more peace on earth than there usually is, on
account of the Dook.
Many of the women onlookers were clothed in
mauve from head to heel. One Chinese girl, in
four different shades of this color, had an umbrella
of still another shade, which her Chinese sweet-
heart was Anglicised enough to hold over her
head. While waiting for the procession people
told one another that they were not to clap ; there
seemed to be an impression that this would be
high treason. In the dull atmosphere the banners
spread out brightly and bravely, but there was
rather a redundancy of those small, triangular
affairs that whirl tightly round their strings and
refuse to blow out at the critical moment. All
the horses that had a chance went up and nibbled
at the decorations, while the band played “ Only
a Leaf,” as if gently deprecating their own long
festoons of oreenery put up in honor of Majesty
to be.
I heard one man in the street: “ All the time I
was in Melbourne he never rose his hat once, but
here he’s been rosing it all the time.” Another
woman, as the Royal carriage was approaching,
got excited. “My handkerchief’s dirty, Emma;
lend me yours, quick, to wave to the Duchess.”
The Duchess, like the defunct Trilby, has a
kind, wide smile and the happy, dairymaid look
that speaks of a good temper and good digestion.
When she had moved on, people turned to one
another and said that she was just like their
Annie, and passed similar compliments on her
large, bright personality. Then they tramped
over each other and elbowed one another viciously
beneath the arches marked “Peace on Earth”
and “A United People,” and asked each other
angrily who they were shoving, and went home by
tram-loads. The procession had even more inter-
vals between each item than was usual, and one
man, who had evidently got lost early in the pro-
ceedings, was seen carrying his flag all by himself,
conscientiously doing every street, some time after
the rest of his comrades had passed on.
In the evening the crowd swarmed out once
more, and the city retired behind festooned lights
and long glittering colonnades of color, while on
the invisible Town Hall and other masonry were
drawn delicate tracings of reds and blues and
greens shining far out into the rain -moistened,
noisy darkness.
At the review, on Tuesday, Duchess May’s
tall figure was topped with a small, black, crino-
line toque trimmed with black ostrich tips, and
worn very much on one side. Her skirt
was finely tucked, her coat Eton, and her
black boa was of chiffon edged with flower
trimming. The lady must be unusually tall, for

without a special permit from Captain Walling-
Fear of anarchists is the cause. So, Arim
don t try to write your name now, for your eyes
are set too close together and your ears are set too
low, and the sentry would note these signs of
anarchical tendencies and shoot you through vour
best hat, at sight. b J
Hordern Brothers’Arch of Welcome is one of the
few of the city’s decorations that are in good taste
and don’tobstruct the vehiculartraffic, soitdeserves
honorable mention. The arch simulates veined
marble, and is erected inside the firm’s Pitt-street
windows. It is draped with royal mourning
colors, and festooned with pearls and ostrich-
feather boas, and niched in its pillars are wooden
ladies wearing beautiful imported frocks. At the
back of the main arch a lion and a unicorn rage
for a crown, and neither makes much progress
Horderns are showing some toques that are more
becoming and better worth fighting for than the
crown between the two beasties. The firm is also
responsible for some of the best dresses that we
are seeing this week in camp and court and grove
(I like the look of. that phrase, Arini).
The Belle-Cole Concert Co. at the Town Hall last
Saturday struck a moderate audience. The de-
corated and illuminated city and the first night of
“ Mignon ” at the Royal probably accounted for
the dead-marine look of the house. There was a
young Pochotsch, who tootled, years ago here, on
the violin ; he is still a young Pechotsch with a lot
of hair and a wooden expression, and his tootle is
more matured and convincing. With a male
parent he did some harrowingly hilarious lumps of
Bach, and was otherwise remarkable for loitering
in the artists’ exit, hungrily pouncing on encores
Avon Saxon, the manly baritone, who
runs the show, _ belongs to the genus of
artists who “give it lip.” Accordingly,
he sang with his head, shoulders, legs, and all the
rest of him. One expected him to “biff” the
“ Yeoman’s Wedding ” violently in the left optic ;
stupidly, I think, he refrained. His wife, Mdlle.
Cheron, has a brilliant soprano, with (after
Antoinette Trebelli) a suggestion of bricks in it;
which voice, however, she certainly knows how to
use. Belle Cole is a wonderful woman. She
sang “Sweet Genevieve” as an encore, and it
was not only a work of art but a Great Creation.
Rhodda Perry, the weird male alto who was with
Newbery, sang “Sweet Genevieve” here nearly
every Saturday for a long season, and when he
had finished with it it was a ragged, disreputable
ditty that slept in a dust-box, and would have
been hustled off any respectable door-mat. Belle
Cole made a star of it, and it twinkles graciously
in your memory. That’s art !
All women singers seem to be very well in the
matter of shoulders and arms, and consequently
carry evening-dress bravely. Madame Cole and
Mdlle. Cheron are no exceptions. The star wore
white silk with a black over-dress of embroidered
net. Mdlle. had a frock of a beautiful shade of
blue under ivory-white lace, with corsage edgings
of black velvet, a black velvet waist-band, and a
knee-trimming of black velvet. Mdlle. has my
permission to remove that Euclid-looking pattern
from her skirt. It has the effect of shortening
her legs, and it’s a crime for a woman to have
short legs. If a man’s legs are long enough to let
his feet reach the ground when he stands up, that
is quite sufficient; but with a woman it is
different.
The Royal Marine Band concert held at the
Town Hall on Wednesday week was noticeable
for the number of men nursemaids. These men
seemed to be largely responsible for the vocifer-
ous encore given to the cock-crowing in the
“Students’ Return from the Ball.” It was the
sort of item that helped to keep the baby quiet.
So far, Pa’s Wednesday half-holiday has not,
apparently, done him much good; it seemingly
only serves to make a nursemaid of him, and gives
him a chance to be ordered by his wife to take
the children out. But when there was an interval
the male nursemaid, _in many cases, dropped the
small infant in the seat, asked the nearest lady he
was unacquainted with to keep an eye on it, and
went out to see the imperceptible dog which is
always supposed to be getting wet on the front
steps. It is many years now since I heard the
first man say he was going out to see that canine.
It must be very old now, and probably requires
more looking after on that account.
Not standing-room for an eel was there at the
Town Hall when Dr. O’Haran received a cheque
for seven-fifty from Sydney women-sympathisers.
It was a wildly enthusiastic crowd, and it didn’t
spare its lungs or keep its handkerchief in its
pocket. The Cardinal sat back looking at the
joyous storm with an upon-this-rock-I-have-built-
my-chureh expression. Mrs. Freehill, who, later,
presented the cheque, and read an address (speak-
ing downwards into the violets at her throat), was
on the platform, with Mrs. T. Slattery and Miss
Shiels. Dr. O’Haran has a good head for a Catho-
lic priest, not much back to it, and with consider-
able mental and moral swellings in the fore part.
He read his speech in a voice that didn’t carry
much beyond the water-bottle on the chairman’s
table. The Cardinal also read what he had to say.
There was a lot of it, and it cited many instances
where men of God, back even to the 12th century,
had been accused by “perfect ladies,” even as was
Dr. O’Haran. But these gentlemen mostly got
a verdict of “ not guilty ” by direct Divine inter-
position—a miracle happened. There was some
points in living in the good old days for good men.
They sought (and found) direct celestial aid. To-
day they seek Jack Want, and find him if he isn’t
away fishing in Japan.

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Mrs. Isaacs, who knows a thing or two about
languages, thinks the Royal pair talk with a very
German accent. But the Germans don’t think so.
Most of us failed to detect any particular breed in
their way of talking. It was just plain accent, or
absence of it. Of course, if everybody else has an
accent, then the only people who have an accent
are the people who have no accent at all.
“ During Celebration Week, Lady Hopetoun
seemed to make a point of dressing severely, and
leaving her brightest diamonds at home. The
tactful Fiarl, no doubt, advised his missus that she
would find favor in the sight of Royalty as an
understudy rather than as a competitor. Those
wiio have seen the Gov.-Generaless in white satin
decolletage, with sweeping train and the Hope-

“What fun about those letters the Duchess
threw out of the train-window ! Mrs. Cornwall
fancied, no doubt, that as she was cleaving the
heart of the primeval forest now was her chance
to get rid of rubbish. Loyal people, who beheld
the shower of manna, rushed with tearful eyes to
receive the supposed greetings of their future
Queen.' Also gangers, maintenance gentlemen,
pick-and-shovel artists, and the like, built their
thirsty hopes on delicate little offerings in the
shape of drink money, and swore savagely when
disappointed.
“ One of the most surprising circs, in connection
with the celebration shows, both day and evening,
was the amazing number of old women,—really
aged women, not mere chits of 50 or 60—who
flocked to see all the sights. Apparently infirm,
they waited for hours in densest crowds (O, that
awfully long dreary waiting for Royalty; how
much has this leg-tired city had of it of late !)
ate peanuts for sustenance, and sang the
Anthem for all they were worth, and waved
dingy hankies, with shrill hurrahs, always at the
wrong carriage. At the first Parliamentary ‘at
home,’ a lady sat on Parliament house steps, so
old and feeble that when the time came for her to
rise, her daughter could only move her by dead
haulage. What is the quotation that fits the case
of these decayed revellers ? There is one some-
where but I can’t think it up. Many a matron’s
bones are aching yet for this wildly exciting
break away from the monotonous grind of dailv
life. J
“The prevailing condition of things at the
Fmdon Hamers’ meeting last Wednesday was a
chilling dampness that tinged the nose like the
red, red rose and froze the bones. The small
party in the special pen gathered itself up into a
knot, while the elder part of it withdrew into a
rain-marquee. The Albert Millers played host
and plied their drag-lot with attentions. Chief of
this party was Mrs. Cruickshank in a violet frock
and a blue toque showing a topsy-turvy rakish pink
something in front. Newly-come-out Vera Clarke
wilted in grey. White-haired Ma Miller looked
sprightly in black, though her nose, like that of
the rest, was as rosy as the Jook’s.
“A dressy Dolly Varden figure in a greyish
gathering at the F. H. race-meeting was Mrs. H.
M. Chomley. The hair of the lady, whose hus-
band got a pair of sleeve-links from the dear Duke,
is not nearly as white as Mrs. Miller’s, and her
tastes are infinitely more Spring-like. With a
black costume she wore a tricky little vest, a
coquettish buttoniere and a hat all a-quiver with
bows on wire stems. Mrs. Chomley is frightfully
girlish—so much so that all her grandchildren
invariably refer to her and address her as the
‘Mater.’ No woman grows old these days unless
she happens to have been bom serious. Religion
ages people shockingly.
“On board the Brooklyn at the much-crowded
afternoon shivoo, some of the Melb. girls were
struck with the idea of having Chinese stewards.
They smiled at the U.S. celestial flunkeys and
John thought them ‘welly ni.’ But when the
giddy girls clutched at the Chinamen’s pigtails to
help them through the crowd to the refreshment
tallies, John changed his tune,- for to grab a
Chinky by his sacred pigtail is to o'fer him the
very deadliest of deadly insults. ‘ But the poor
girl didn’t know, you know.’
“ One of the hottest functions of the Mel-
bourne celebrations was an ‘afternoon tea’ given
by the Russian Consul. The commanders and
officers of the warships and several distinguished
Victorian officials, such as George Jenkins, were
invited. The Russian ‘afternoon tea’ began at
1.30 and howled down the corridors of drink and
glory till after 6 in the evening. It was a mag-
nificent banquet, with 20 or more items on the
menu. Toasts were proposed, not in do.iens,
but in hundreds. The toast of every txxly
in the wide world was drunk twice over
in bumpers. First the company drank
them in vodka in honor of Russia, and then
they repeated them in champagne. By 4
o’clock the Russian ‘afternoon tea’ was the
talk of the street, and it was still raging. Songs
were sung in half-a-dozen different languages at
once. The Russian, Dutch, and German officers
began to shake hands with each other and every-
body else, present. There was more vodka, and
the Russians, who apparently reckoned they
owed everything to George Jenkins, surrounded
him. They shook him by the hands, by the coat-
tails, and the beard. They even shook his feet.
Then they shook Jenkins bodily. Last scene of
all was a crushed and suffocated Jenkins sitting in
a big leathern chair with four hairy Russians on
top of him, a)l kissing his bulging forehead. In
the background another Russian was shaking the
leg of the table, with a wild idea that the table
was Jenkins.
“Every girl landing on the Gromoboi at the
Reception, the other Monday night, was met by
Die invitation, painfully enunciated by one of the
Jack Tars of sledge-land, to ‘kindly take her
close off.’ One of the first greeted with this
original request stood by to watch how the rest of
her sex would take it. Such a procession of ex-
pressions she witnessed ! Some cold, some mis-
chievous, some puzzled, some blushful, some
annoyed, some bursting with a desire to pass the
joke on to the shirt-fronted brethren. Tue latter
class was numerous.
Nance O Neil is gone, but one can’t forgive
that ‘ Macbeth ’ music. Intended, no doubt, to
express. Scottish sentiment and spirit, one finds no
fault with its reels and strathspeys, for Macbeth
died 500 years before Calvin was born to freeze
the current of Celtic gaiety. It’s a question
whether Sandy has been dour from the beginning,
or whether he owes his gravity to struggles for
national independence, Calvinistic doctrines and
Covenanting horrors. Admitting, however, that
Rab lamson s bairn may have been born asour-faced
loon scribe would not haveprotested had the ‘Mac-
beth music been pervaded by weird and solemn
melodies, descriptive of ‘ Caledonia, stern and
wild and her unsmileful children. But it was an
awful anachronism to play well-known Presby-
terian psalmody in connection with a person who
died before the Norman Conquest. Jumble
Puritanism and Macbethism up together and
you 11 spoil the flavor of both. One of the psalms
was dished up as sauce to Duncan’s murder, just
as if the Macbeths were religious fanatics, nerving
themselves to slay ‘the spoiler of Zion,’ instead of
a self-seekmg couple plotting a mercenary crime
if Nance was hankering after lofty motives she
should have got into a toga and played Brutus.
“Rickards’ new Opera House is a dream of
dainty carpets and jewelled lamps, but the trouble
is that one can’t see the wood for the trees ; since
opening night all the glories have been hidden by
a solid mass of audience. Jn the d.c., now of
nights, the costuming is mixed. Some circlers
evidently desiring to live up to the nice, clean
theatre, array themselves in evening splendor
while other women sport day-frocks and nurse
large, feathery hats (for every woman sits
hatless in a theatre now). On the stage
Diver wears a tasty suit, black satin with
blue flowers, and the best-dressed of the first-part
charmers strolled round in a glittering silver frock
with a blue lining. Blue is a favorite color with
Rickards co. Irene Franklin’s hose and shoes
are cerulean, and so are Marie Lloyd’s songs.
Mane has also a selection of fancy frocks, but her
most fetching is her trim barmaid’s black silk and
muslin apron.

Obit. : James Bath, secretary of S.A.
Education Dept. Came to S.A. in ’sl, and was
one of Adelaide’s earliest pedagogues. Bath was
secretary under 16 successive Education Ministers.
“ J. G. McN.” writes :
Jim Hopkins, Member for Boulder City, was never a
railway porter in Victoria, as alleged. He and his brother
worked in their mother’s store at Boolara, Gippsland
(Vic.).
One Vic. politician who had a surrepti-
tious seat on an oil-drum in the brake-van of the
Royal train on one of its journeys has since then
talked of only one subject—“ The time I travelled
with ’is ’lghness.”

tain and Commissioner of something or other,
meteorieally toured the world a few years ago,
and who on his return to N.S.W. expressed his

Hint to aspiring politicians : Become a
grandfather. Almost all N.S.W. Premiers have
been grandfathers. In late years, Lyne was a
grandfather, Dibbs ditto, Parkes ditto, and See
has just qualified. G. H. Reid was an exception.
The remains of Bishop Salvado, for half-a-
century head of the New Norcia Aborigines Sta-
tion in Westralia, are to be transferred from their
present resting-place in Rome to the Bishop’s old
aboriginal mission field. The Bishop visited Rome
last year and died there.
The dignity of Labor ! A Mount Lyell
magnate told the Hawthorn (Melb.) Bench the
other day that he hadn’t spoken to a trained nurse
called in to attend his housemaid, because “he
would not humble himself to speak to a person
employed by his servant.”
Curious early-day experience of a M.L.
medico—Dr. Moncton, formerly of Invercargill.
He received a message that someone “up Winton
way ” had broken his leg, and, packing his medical
appliances on his horse, he started for Winton.
There he was told the case must be at Castle
Rock station, at which place he was told the
word had come from Blackmount. There he
was told a tree had fallen on a man at Te
Anau Lake (100 miles from his starting
point), but when he arrived there he found
he had to go to Eglinton, 20 miles further.
His patient was found with a “ hopelessly-
smashed ” leg, requiring instant amputation
above the knee. “I ground down three
table-knives to narrow
blades and fine edges,”
says the doctor,
“improvised a tour-
niquet 1) y
three folds
of calico
with a
wine-
cork
stitched
in one
place
and a loop
foratwitch-
ing-stick in
another, gave
my volunteer
assistants their instructions, and with
whisky, taken in the absence of chloroform,
we achieved the removal of the limb in a work-
manlike manner.”

Died recently at Croixelles (M.L.) Renata
Te Kawharu, last chief of the Nga Te Koata tribe,
aged 88. He was a connection of Te Rauparara’s,
but a lover of peace. Nearly 60 years ago Renata
assisted in piloting the Expedition ships into
Wakatu, the then name of Nelson haven
Owing to ill-health, ex-Speaker Joseph
Abbott goes out of N.S.W. politics at the close of
present N.S.W. Parliament, some time this month.
He has had 21 years of it, and in that time has
represented only two constituencies—Gunnedah
for seven years, and Wentworth for fourteen.
Burra, the S.A. township which gave
Speaker Holder political life, and has kept him in
Parliament for 12 years without causing a
moment’s anxiety for the safety of his seat, is one
of the most dejected “extinct volcanoes” of the
continent —a pitiful example of that most pitiful
sight, a deserted mining field. Successively,
Holder was public school teacher (his father was
ditto), town clerk, and proprietor and editor of
the little woe-begone weekly sheet of the place.
Up to now Holder has been one of the humblest
men in the front rank of Australian politics. The
depressing influence of Burra would make any
politician humble.

London on private business, and on no (State job
or bnlet whatever. Still, if he can spare a week en
route to shake these cows, and thus end the
