When the Jook was going on his Vie.
shooting expedition a certain Melbourne tailor
was asked to lend two especially valuable dogs.
He wasn’t invited himself. Tailor replied by
blandly inquiring how it came to pass that his
dogs were considered good enough to associate
with the Jook while he wasn’t. Then a gloom fell
over the proceedings.
Seddon’s chief fault in his old age is an
incurable verbosity. At the luncheon to the re-
turned contingenters, at Dunedin, he got up to
propose a toast and, after observing, “ This is not
a time for long speeches,” he held forth for
over half-an-hour. Even in a Presbyterian church,
where they believe in a material hell, the sermon
is hardly as long as that nowadays.
Stated in Melbourne that one big poten-
tate flatly refused to have the Jook shooting on
his premises. Perhaps he didn’t like shooting
with a man of such high rank that, if the visitor
fired wildly and plugged his host, the host would
be expected to say in his last moments that he was
quite satisfied with the arrangement and rather
preferred things that way than otherwise.
Chevalier E. de Martino, “ marine painter
extraordinary to her late Majesty the Queen,”
and artistic lackey or dauber-on-the-premises to
the ducal party, has been “commanded ” to paint
the “Landing of the Duke at Hobson’s Bay.”
Nobody that anybody has heard of has seen any
work of this master, but as he was one of Vic-
toria’s “ discoveries,” his inartistic eminence may
be guessed at.
The remains of the six members of the
Jew Boy’s gang of bushrangers were carted out of
the Devonshire-street cemetery the other day.
Dead and alive, these six have stood a good deal
of cartage. They were carted to the gaol, carted
from the gaol to the gallows—then near Essex-
street; their dead bodies were carted to the ceme-
tery in Devonshire-street, and now they have
been carted to Rookwood.

Many people will regret to hear of the
death of Mr. Benjamin Thomas Lacy, founder of
the business of Parke and Lacy Co., Limited, of
Sydney and Melbourne, and of Parke and Lacy
Co., of San Francisco and London. Mr. Lacy,
who paid several long visits to Australia, was
well-known in mining circles, and had innumer-
able friends in this country. He was an Irish-
man by birth, and resided chiefly in San Francisco.
He died at Monterey, California.

Many most gracious acts of kindness of a personal
character have been bestowed upon the colony and its
institutions. In particular I remember with pride the
presentation to the Parliamentary library of two of her
own literary works which are still treasured and shown
there to every visitor as a mark of Royal favor.
Two whole books ! Why Alderman Cohen has
often given away two copies of his famous novel
“ How Will It End ” on his way home from the
office at night. And the present writer has
thrown more books than that at a stray dog.
The real power behind the prospective
throne of George V. is Lord Wenlock, the white-
moustachioed, military-looking old gentleman who
accompanies H.R.H. everywhere during the pre-
sent tour. Wenlock it is who pulls all the strings.
The Jookoyork has him by his side at all official
functions, and even when the dinners are so
arranged that there is no available space for Wen-
lock at the elbow of Royalty, he sits opposite with
a cold, calm eye ready to give George “ the office ”
by a Royal system of tick-tacking and get him out
of any embarrassing situation that may arise.
One of these days the public will marvel when
they see Wenlock standing a few hundred yards
away from the Royal party, energetically pulling
his nose, touching his right ear, slapping his thigh
and bowing vigorously to himself in order that
the utterances of the Jook may not involve a war
with Russia.

Charleston, one of S.A.’s Senators, who is
expected to vote on the Tory side in Eederal
politics, was originally elected to S.A. Council as
a Labor nominee, but was boldly included in the
Ass. Federal ticket last March. Charleston,
originally a working engineer and Protectionist,
is now one of the leaders of the S.A. Freetrade
and Liberal (?) Ass, and for years voted with the
Ass. party against almost every measure of the
Kingston Govt.
So far, Bigjolm Forrest looms rather
ridiculously in his new sphere. He threatens
members with his large stomach and strident
voice, and sadly misses the respect he commanded
in the West. John has a way of stating what he
will have and what he will not have, in the manner
of a nigger-driver who has been accustomed to see
his nigger jump promptly. And the House laughs
at him. In Federal politics John runs a terrible
risk of finding himself out.
Ap opus of the recent suicide of the Prime
Minister of Wurtemburg, a pamphlet published
150 years ago recorded the fate of 31 Prime
Ministers of England up to that date. Ten died
by the axe, four in prison, four in exile, three
were hanged, three were “killed by sturdy beg-
gars,” two “died untimely deaths by private
hands,” one “died penitent,” and four were
“ saved by sacrificing their masters.” During the
last 100 years only two English Premiers have
died tragic deaths. The billet is a safer one than
it was in the olden time.
“ P.X.” : Add to the doings of that Nolan

B. 18/5/T) that they projected the water scheme
which now supplies Broken Hill, and actually

Longevity is the last of all the privileges
that moneyed rank enjoys. The mother of Lord
Rosebery died the other day, aged 82. She was
a daughter of Earl Stanhope, and bridesmaid
to Queen Victoria. Her first husband, the eldest
son of Rosebery’s grandfather, happened to
decease before he had succeeded to the earldom,
and subsequently she put herself further beyond
the reach of poverty by marrying the Duke of
Cleveland. Presuming that this old lady was the
sole survivor of the party, can anyone state the
average age achieved by the late Queen and her
bridesmaids.
Mortimer Menpes, the South Australian
artist who is credited with making a huge income
in London, has a drawing of Cecil Rhodes repro-
duced in the last number of the Studio. The
drawing, evidently made with a brush of Indian
ink, consists of flat black masses ; and, though
nearly the whole of Rhodes’s face is in shadow,
the portrait is an excellent one, and the whole
character of the man is revealed in the small
circular high light on the tip of his Hebraic nose.
Is it possible for a man so utterly Jewish in
nature and appearance to be the Gentile that
Rhodes claims to be ?
Poor old Arabi Pasha, whose bungalow at
Colombo was one of the objects of pilgrimage by
curious Australian travellers, has been allowed to
go back to Egypt. He was a melancholy sight,
with his tall bent figure, and eyes almost blind,
and his constant complaint at the brutality of the
British in only allowing him a pitifully inadequate
allowance of four wives. But Arabi wasn’t quite
the ruin he looked. It is still remembered in
Colombo how a cocky Australian cricketer who
jibed at the old man about his defeat was passed
gently but firmly into the road by the six-foot-
three Egyptian exile.

The Duke and Duchess stand as object lessons to
students of medical science. The Duke’s weak frame,
nervous hesitant manner, and weary face are the in-
evitable results of centuries of cousin marriage and
other in-breeding, while the Duchess’s square, well-set
shoulders, broad hips, and full bust proclaim her plebeian

Isaac Nichols was the first Australasian
postmaster ; in fact, the first Postmaster-General.
He was appointed under an order of Macquarie,
dated “ Headquarters, 25th April, 1809.” It was
his business to board ships and take delivery of
the mails. “In consideration of the trouble and
expense attending on the duty,” he was authorised
to make these charges on delivery:—“ For every
letter, Is.; for every parcel not exceeding 201bs.
weight, 2s. fid. ; and for all exceeding that weight,
ss. Soldiers’ letters, Id.” Then there was this
precautionary provision : “ A list to be published
m the Gazette of the names and addresses of
persons to whom letters and parcels are directed.”
When the Duke of Edinburgh visited Bal-
larat, in ’6B, the directors of the Band and Albion
Consols Co. took him to the lowest level of the
mine, and, placing a double-pointed pick in his
hand, told him that if he worked on the face of
the drive he would unearth a handsome nugget
(the same had been planted there). H.R.H.,
being keen on gold, let fly with a will and struck
his forehead with the pick. A good deal of
ordinary red—not blue —blood came down on his
shirt-front. But the Duke persevered and got
the nugget. When portable property was in
view H.R.H. would have struggled on even if his
vitals had fallen on his shirt-shirt.
Eulogistic biographies of H. C. E. Cliilaers
(Victorian politician of the early ’so’s and after-
wards British Chancellor of the Exchequer) have
lately been appearing. Yet Childers was only
the plainest of mediocrities. He is remember-
some in Victoria for two things. For an immense
number of years he cost that State £866 a year for
having been a very inefficient imported Govern-
ment functionary in the days of Irresponsible
Government. Also he was Vic. Auditor-General
for about a year, and £283,745 of public money
vanished in that time, and hasn’t been accounted
for to this day. Childers’ auditing was apparently
a work of a genius.
The public moan of Alick Forrest (extract
from the West Austbalian) :
He had known that he was to get the C.M.G.-ship, and
he had believed that he would get the title of K.O.M.G.
However, while he was up in Kimberley, recently, he re-
ceived a telegram that, as the Mayors of all the Australian
capitals wanted knighthoods, that title could be conferred
upon only the Mayors of Sydney and Melbourne, while
the others would be offered the title of C.M.G. He was
told that he might havb that, and was strongly recom-
mended to accept it, as it was generally followed by the
higher title. On those terms he accepted it. (Laughter.)
It seemed strange that a man who had been six years
Mayor of Perth, 15 years in Parliament, and 10 years
Government whip, and had explored a large extent of
country, should be made only a C.M.G., while the Mayors
of Melbourne and Sydney, who had held office only since
November last, should receive knighthoods. When the
royal party’ came to Adelaide and Tasmania, they might
find some resentment at it.
“He was told he might have that.” A case of

will be handed out.”

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.—Always give, in ethnological and
natural-history items etc., exact date and exact
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
The following letter has just come to hand,
and is published for what it is worth :
Re the jackass that sat and sang to 10,000 of his
brethren from Royal Arch on the bridge—that jack is one
of a pair that came to town on Blazen Powell’s drunk ;
that’s tile cock-jack—know him by his cross eye. His
partner got killed trying to prove that snake-yarn. She
had a bang tail, like Governors’ horses. Laughed the
solo parts—Cross-eye jined in like. Thousands of people;
like me, if not too drunk, heard and seen this pair. Sat
mostly on public building. Seemed real pleased when
Peacock came out and jined in a three, O. I seed that
myself. I said to my wife, when I went ’ome next day,
“ Peacock ’ll be Premier.” My wife inquired if that was
the latest brand, and where I got that old hat. Well, the
next time I meet them pair of jacks was in the Melb.
bo»e-vard, at the planting of a dear friend. I see the pic-
ture now—the dear departed on the skids ; the fat, clean-
faced sky-pilot at the bow ; the grave-digger, hat in hand,
looking quite sorrowful and dry—as if he knew the de-
parted ; The undertaker’s men looked tired and dry also.
Harps, crowns, and halo, etc., were handed round. All
stood round and looked our best. “Steady,” said a voice.-
Snap—the voice of the preacher was heard—so was the
vo’c iof them two Jacks. Well they laughed like —yes—
just like that-er Peacock. The good man in his mind
prayed for the repose of the soul of them Jacks overhead.
The law protects Jackasses that have no decency, even at
a planting- anyhow the ptiotos came and—well I shudder
when I think if these two wretched Peacock Jacks had
only came to town on Queen’s Weeping dav— great Scott
and protected too —the villians. Wax. Madden would
have sounded alarms- called on the ’Ouse repealed
the Act, called out our trusty Tom Price and his pork
butchers. They would open out, makC a rear, side,
flank, or some great move, fire low and lay ’em out.
Then —I’m only thinking—our own white knight would
rush-wire ’Ome in the name of himself and Australia that
the insult to himself and Australia had been wiped out
in the jackasses’ blood. The silken bedclothes drawn
closer. I rise to make a think —offer reward, catch that
Jack with the swivel eye, present him to the dear Dook,
rememberence ever of us in Melbourne —Gorsave.—E.K.

*H.S.”: He that curious creature, the
dugong (Halicore Australis) —which, with its
three allied species, the manatees of America and
Africa, has given rise to the mermaid-myth. It
inhabits northern Australian waters, as far oyer
as Cambridge Gulf. It is smooth-skinned with
atrophied hind limbs and two large anterior
flippers. It suckles its young at breasts placed
human-fashion, feeds on the marine grasses on
the reef-flats, and is found in parties of from four
or five to over 20. Its length is about 9ft. —some-
times reaching 12, and it often tops lOcwt. Its
A DISGUISE.
HE (to girl who has been looking at him for
about ten minutes): “ Well, me girl, do you
think you would know me again?”
GIRL: “ No, sir; not if you toashed your face.”
oil, which is valuable for medicinal and other pur-
poses, is worth up to 20s. a gallon, the yield per
animal being from sto lOgals. The salted flesh
is fairly palatable. The aborigines go after
dugong" on moonlight nights in canoes, with
large heavy spears attached to strong lines.
Their plan is to steal cautiously up to the animal
and spear it first in the hinder parts and then in
the mouth or nose, by which means it is soon
drowned. The hides (over an inch thick when
tanned) are worth about 4d. per lb. green, and
the bones are very dense and heavy. The dugong-
spear is fully 14ft long, with a heavy clublike butt.
The big end is hollow and encloses a barb to
which the line is attached. About 150 years ago
there was killed in Behring Sea the last-known
specimen of a species of the same genus, which
was double the size of the existing dugong.
Kap I used to believe, with many
other bushmen, that a horse could not swim in
hobbles. One evening mate and I camped beside
a lake, m Western N.S.W., which had an island
in it. lo secure our nags we swam them over
about 200 yards of water, hobbled them, and swam
back ourselves to camp. Next morning we were
horrified to find horses missing. After some
trouble we tracked them to water’s edge, when
my mate dolefully remarked that if we went in a
bit we d find them underneath. But an inspec-
tion of the opposite bank revealed the track of
them leaving the water, and we found them both
safe and sound in a valley a mile or so away.

resented. But the mate hadn’t time to do any-

round and landed the lurcher two beauties that
put him senseless on his back, and followed tliis
up by chasing the second man right out of the
Ar lVe ’ manager then declined to move the
Mongolian, but the two whites came to the con-
clusion that underground work didn’t agree with
them.

“ Scotty ” on rabbit-destruction :
You can’t destroy well-placed rabbits. “J.W.C.”,
wit h bis one bag of pollard per mile, is an almighty ex-
pensive person. Take an ordinary run, say 30x30 miles =
0)0 s.m. That’s £9OO or more on the average, including
haulage and loss ; and it’s worth £2 more per mile for
distribution and revision. That’s £2700. Now, rum must
be subdivided into at least 36 paddocks in order to pre-
vent immigration and emigration. Rabbits get tired of
pollard and match-heads. 'Well, there’s 36x36miles = 1296
miles of wire-netting, which, putting same on made fences
and allowing extra cost for gates, gullies, rocks, etc.,
we’ll call £2O a mile (it’s more). Pay up another £25,920,
making with extras (say) £3o,ooo—which is just what
Robertson, of Oolac, is reported to have spent 40 years
ago, and his run is still good trapping-ground.

“Monaroite” : Bombala (N.S.W.) had a
fire the other night, and a
small wooden building was
reduced to ashes. The pre-
mises on each side were saved
by the heroic exertions of
about 300 men and the police.
When the average crowd goes
to save a place that place is
lost. There were three sick
women in one of the houses,
and they were thrown through
a window without time to
dress—the crowd had previ-
ously wrecked the windows
with its boots to facilitate
the salvage operations. Then
the household goods poured
through the broken casements
like a flight of grasshoppers,
and in an incredibly short
time everything was saved,
and either broken, lost or
trodden upon. Later on,
when it was found that the
fire wasn’t spreading after all,
the wreckage was carried back,
and the sick women got to
bed again in hysterics, and the
two houses looked as if they
had been rushed by herds of
buffaloes. Thus do the
brighter and nobler instincts of
humanity break through the
outer crust of. selfishness. There is
nothing more beautiful than a ten-
dency to help a distressed brother
or sister —even when it shows
itself by a needless and misguided
throwing of the brother’s tea-set
into the middle of the street, and
a rushing of the undressed sister
out into the public gaze.

Notes from a bushman’s diary :
Wednesday.— lt was late when we reached Melbourne.
It was raining, too, and bitterly cold at that. Orainped,
worn out, and sleepy, we were glad to take the first
accommodation that was offered. This was at an
alleged boarding-honse kept by two persons named
Murphy. We stayed there three days. The menu
for breakfast never altered—fried steak and onions, cold
and greasy. Michael carved the bread against his shirt.
Slices the size of a small plate, and an inch thick. He
also waited at table. He had the old bog habit of stealing
round on tiptoe and breathing confidentially in. one’s ear
when he thought anything was wanted. I believe this is
a hereditary instinct acquired through many generations
of lying in wait for the landlord.
Our room was 8 x 10, and contained three single beds.
The occupants were two men, two women, and three
children.
The price of meals had risen 150 per cent, since the city
had been exalted by the presence of a real live duke. I
breathed his name with great fervor each time I parted
up. So, keeping him so constantly in memory, I may be
considered a loyal subject.
All the restaurants were crowded to the doors. Our
plan was to note who was eating pudding—the last
course—and mortgage his chair by standing over it till he
was finished, and then fight for it if necessary. Once a
Bendigo soldier had mortgaged the next chair to mine,
and caused much indignation to a fat old gent by the
simple remark, “By God, this old cow eats a lot 1 ” The
old party rose with his mouth full of hot jam-roll and
told us that he was opposed to Jingoism since soldiers
stood over civilians in cafCs and placed restrictions on
their appetites. After which he had some more jam-roll.
We saw most of the sights. First sight we saw was a
woman of 14st. lying prostrate on the footpath, with a
flask gripped in one hand, asking the pavement “ Who
the h—’s the duke?” and telling it in answer that she
was as good as several kinds of awful duchesses. Gross-
ing Elizabeth-street, we saw two sailors bashing a citizen
for “ pushin’ us out of yer blanky way.”
Later we were attracted by immense applause, and
hurried to the spot. Found that a crowd which had
grown weary of waiting for the Duke was giving three
cheers for a passing sanitary cart.
Then we went into a hotel for another drink, and woke
up four days later in the Fitzroy Gardens. By that time
the Duke was gone. So were our boots. I wonder if he
took them.

“Eller”: Pe flesh-eating horses. In
nine cases out of ten this habit has been acquired
when the animal was young. Foals will nibble at
anything. Have seen one eating liver that had
been thrown to fowls.
When one of the detachments
of N.S.W. Lancers sent to S’ Africa
readied its first standing-camp it
saw some episodes more funny than
tragic. In one a pig was concerned.
An Australian Cavalry Major was
sitting in his sark, industriously
mending a rent in his pants. It
was just after Paavdeberg, and
after the commandeering of some
hundredsof waggon-loadsof British
supplies by I)e Wet. so biscuits
were short, and majors and troopers
alike were hungry as well as a oit
ragged. A pig dashed down the
lines towards the Major's camp.
Where the animal came from no
one ever knew. The Major-, with
visions before him of savoury pork
chops for tea, snapped up lus
sword, and dropping Lis nether
garments, gave chase, lunging with
right good will as he ran. The
hullabullo attracted attention, and
other hungry fellows, officers and
men-i-pure-bloo led subalterns and
“ rank outsiders ” of no class at all
—joined in the hunt. Too hungry
to regard eccentricities of costume,
no one, save, perhaps, the newly-
arrived men, noticed the figure-
head of the weird chase till the
Major, after leading his hetero-
geneous army through the Lancer-
lines, past the Enniskillens, then
through the Carbineers, and then
the Guards, at length gave up the
wild pursuit, and returned with
his shirt-tails flapping merrily in
the breeze, but with no pig.

“ Targah ” : Near Oooran (Q.) I once
overtook two exhausted-looking aboriginals carry-
ing a gin apparently very ill. Watched them
stagger up to a house, from which they were
turned away. Had same refusal at next house,
after which they staggered off towards the hotel
paddock. Soon afterwards I was surprised to see
the same gin busy light ing a fire. Upon being in-
terrogated about her quick recovery, she calmly
replied, “Baal those people gib it tucker, no
plurry use being sick.”
ALDERMAN ALBERT MEEK , of Maclean
(N.S.W.) Bellringer, Billposter, and Vege-
table Vendor. [ Collins, photo.]
“ Boko ” : The common ant-lion is a most
interesting insect to observe. Kept in a shallow
box of clean, dry sand, and well supplied with
ants, they work vigorously at their pitfalls, going
backwards in a circle and throwing the sand out
with the head. When the first circle has been
completed they reverse the motion, making
another inside —and so on until the trap is com-
plete. Lying at the bottom, the “ lion ” remains
concealed under the sand until an ant falls in. If
at first snap the prey escapes the waiting jaws,
the lion sharply flips sand up with his head,
causing the side to fall in and bring the ant back
to him. Ant-lions are very common round Sydney,
but it is not easy to catch them, as they are not
usually more than a-quarter of an inch long, har-
monise in hue with the sand, and can burrow
away very rapidly. Best way is to lean over the
hole and blow strongly. The sand goes, leaving
lion exposed. -


Residents of Maclean (N.S.W.) are getting
a lot of amusement out of their municipal council.
A difference of opinion over proposed baths led to
the retirement of Aid. Burt, who was nominated
for re-election. The opposition party nominated
an eccentric character named Meek, the local bell-
ringer and bill-poster, a dwarf in stature, and a
person of concealed knowledge. Burt withdrew
from the contest in disgust, and Meek was re-
turned unopposed. Various ratepayers bought
him a second-hand silk hat and other articles of
attire in keeping with his new dignity ; and at
latest advices the new alderman was hawking his
own photoes round the town, and taking himself
very seriously on the whole. Photo, of the new
alderman in his second-hand tall hat appears in
this issue.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
“X.X.X.X.” : I once found myself in the
courthouse of a Tasmanian mining township.
The plaintiff in the principal case turned out to
be a friend of the bench. After he had
finished giving his evidence, his Worship beckoned
him over, shook hands, inquired after his (plain-
tiff’s) brothers, sisters, cousins and aunts, ex-
pressed pleasure at their welfare, and—gave a
verdict for defendant. Meeting the latter shortly
afterwards, I stated that I thought it was all up
when I saw what a friendly interest the Bench
took in the plaintiff. “Oh !” he replied, “that’s
nothing; I was with the Attorney-General till
two o’clock this morning. I guess he fixed things.”

The Hotelkeeper and the Tourist.
“ I liad the pleasure of a long chat with Mr.
John Graham, whilst passing through Copman-
hurst, Clarence River,” writes a tourist. “We
were talking about dangerous experiences, and
Mr. Graham, who is the popular proprietor of the
‘Copmanhurst Hotel,’ gave his story so interest-
ingly that I send it on for publication.”
“Ten years ago, being then fifty-eight,” said
Mr. Graham, “ I was startled one morning by
sharp pains running through my feet. Later on,
my feet swelled to an enormous size, and the
pains spread right through my body. A doctor
who was called in said 1 had a very severe attack
of rheumatic gout, but despite his attention I did
not recover. 1 had one stretch of five months in
bed, and during other attacks was unable to walk
for weeks. 1 had to be fed by hand, being power-
less to move. If I wished to change my position
in bed, three men had to lift me. Another doctor
was called in, and, although 1 followed the
medical instructions carefully, and touched no
spirits of any kind, I still remained a sufferer.
Outward applications and herbal remedies proved
quite useless.”
“ Were you in this plight for ten years ?”
“Yes,” said Mr. Graham, “I was.”
“ But you look healthy and hearty enough
now.”
“Yes, because my wife induced me to take Dr.
Williams’ pink pills. After I had taken one box
I felt relieved. My appetite returned, the swell-
ing went down, and the pain subsided. Before I
had used eight boxes I was a strong, robust,
healthy man, free from pain and every vestige of
rheumatic gout. lam well known in the Clarence
River and New England districts, and many
people in this town can tell you how I suffered.”
It is impure blood that causes rheumatism,
rheumatic gout, sciatica and lumbago ; thus when
the damp, cold weather comes, or the patient gets
wet, the agonies begin, because the blood, im-
poverished and acid, only needs something to
start the pain. Dr. Williams’ pink pills enrich
the blood and cure rheumatism and attendant ail-
ments. As blood builder and nerve tonic, they also
cure anaemia, debility, neuralgia, colds, effects of
influenza and fevers, bronchitis, consumption of
the bowels and lungs, heart weaknesses, dysentery,

Dear Bulletin.
—Re horses scratch-
ing for water (“ P. L., ”
8., 30/3/’01). Writer,
while travelling sheep
from Robe River(nor’-
west) to Perth (W.A.)
via Cue, in ’96, came
across a long, dry
stretch where sheep
went without water
for seven days, and
men scratched and
watered camp horses
from damper-dishes.
In the bed of a creek
about 50 miles from
Peak Hill, we struck
a wild, white •• eyed
chestnut mare, “dog-
jKior,” with a foal.
The mare had
scratched about 2ft.
to lied-rock and de-
fended her little well
viciously. It took two
mounted men with
whips to keep her at
a distance while the
third man filled and
re filled a dish for the
camp horses and bags
for cooking. Have
met with other in-
stances of horses fol-
lowing the kangaroo’s
example in this way.
—R.L W.
Out in the far
West (N.S.W.),' one
census-taker, sent
back to the Q. border
from Broken Hill,
travelled by bike 1440
miles, for the most
part through desert
to which the alleged
break-up of the
drought has not made
any difference what-
ever. Another collec-
tor, sent out East from
the same place,
dropped two horses
by the waterless and
feed less track ; at one
place a feed .for two
horses cost 14s. The
most striking thing
another saw was a
butcher’s shop in a
township described
as “ the centre of a
large pastoral dis-
trict” shut up be-
cause there wasn’t
anything left to kill.
The township hadn’t
seen any fresh meat
except what had come by parcels post for
over three months. In the worst of these dis-
tricts the collectors filled in the papers as they
went—a second journey was out of the question.
Not that it would have affected the totals much if
they had been missed altogether.
* * * •
“ Pyal'a” : lie aboriginal custom of des-
troying deformed children (B. 9/3/01). Infanticide
is largely practised among the natives of N.S.
Wales and Central Australia ; the practice being
due probably to the difficulty of providing for two
children of closely-approaeliing ages. The gins
generally suckle a child up to the age of three
years, and sometimes four ; and, if another hap-
pens along during that time, they simply kill it at
birth. Very rarely does infanticide take place
when the child is a few years old. In such cases,
object is to better rear a weakly, but older child,
the survivor being supposed to gain the strength
of the killed one.

Judging by Victoria’s experience, £1,600,000 a year is

Assume Commonwealth could make 5 per cent, profit on
its sweeps, the other 5 per cent, going in expenses ; 32

Stockwhip : In England a foreigner can be naturalised if
(1) he has lived in the United Kingdon for at least five
years or been that period in the British service ; (2) if
he convinces the authorities that he intends to
continue in the United Kingdom or in the British
service; (3) if he pays £5. Naturalisation is not a right
even then, and may still be refused. When naturalised,
he gets a vote for the Lower House of the British Parlia-
ment —nobody has a vote for the Upper House. In the
Transvaal before the war, a foreigner could, after two
years’ residence and on payment of £2, become a
“ second-class burgher,” with a vote for the Lower
House ; and he might (though he seldom did) become a
“ first-class burgher,” with a vote for both Houses
twelve years later. Many British States grant
naturalisation on much easier terms than Britain
does, but it only holds good in that one State

ROYAL PRESENTS AND THE CONSEQUENCES OF THEM.
" Mr. Edmund Barton, Prime Minister of Australia, received from the hands of the Duke a most beautiful gold snuff-box, about 4in. long by 4in. broad and 2in. deep. It was handsomely
embellished, and bore on the lid the Royal arms and monogram in blue enamel, a large crown of rubies, the three Prince of Wales’ feathers in diamonds, tied in a clasp of emeralds, while on the
right and left sides of the cover were six large diamonds of considerable value. Mr. George Jenkins, Clerk of the Federal House of Representatives, was most graciously received by their Royal
Highnesses, and the Duke, in saying good-bye to Mr. Jenkins, presented him, as a mark of regard, with a gold pencil-case bearing the white rose of York and the badge of the Order of the Garter
in enamel. To Mr. Chomley, Chief Commissioner of Police, the Duke handed, on behalf of himself and the Duchess, a pair of sleeve-links, with the white rose of York in enamel. Mr. M'Culloch,
chairman of the celebrations committee, was presented with a gold cigarette case, bearing the Royal arms, with a monogram in the centre. The Premier (Mr. A. J. Peacock) received, as a souvenir,
autograph photographs of the Duke and Duchess of Cornwall and York. His Royal Highness presented to Sir John Madden a handsome card salver of solid silver and elegant workmanship. The
salver rests on four silver supports, and bears in the centre the Royal arms. Below the centre engraving is the following inscription:— ‘ To His Excellency Sir John Madden, K.C.M.G., from the
Duke of York, in remembrance of his visit to Melbourne, 1901. "— MELB. ARGUS.

ance of my giddy days and riotous bounding nights.

all professions, but had belonged to the legion that takes
presents and has a past. She asked me by letter oca
on her, and begged me not to give her away, as she had
kept square ever since meeting my friend, and always
meant to do so. She appeared quite sincere and in great
distress, so I-asked for time. Finally, after taking one
consideration with another, I could not see that it was
mv funeral any way, so I promised. It is a very nice
poU and should be argued before a full court of! experts
and old cats. As to the story, I think the int ® rfe
person who broke his neck to tell was a cad Without
the signature one would guess the writer to be a woman.
Women’s heroes are generally awful bounders when they
are not prigs.
Wellington (M.L.) City Council has fallen
into a stupendous orifice. Sometime since it
decided to acquire certain private lands for street-
widening purposes. In due course the land-
holders put in their claims—excessive as usual.
But the town clerk forgot to file the statutory
objection within the prescribed 60 days, and the
Courts have now decided that they have no power
to interfere. On present appearances the town
will have to pay £25,000 for £lO,OOO worth of land.
No doubt there is a way out if it can only be
found There are old moss-grown English laws
which people have forgotten to repeal that
will do almost anything, and if fcl '? n l£ l V ilk
only be found in time it is probable that the Lity
Council could take the land and refuse to pay a
pennv, and could order the land-owners to be
dragged to death by wild omnibuses as well. But
it is hard to find the right mouldy statute at the
right moment.

London Daily Mail’s description of the
magnificent style in which the millionaire-
bankrupt Hooley is living—said to be at the
rate of £50,000 a year—while his creditors
get 2s. in the £, is a nasty slap at the boasted
stringency of English legislation. Of course
Hooley is now merely his wifes agent, and
Hooleyism goes on as strong as ever. Bunch
hits the case off thus :
THE SPLENDID BANKRUPT.
Under its spreading bankruptcy
The village mansion stands ;
Its lord, a mighty man is he,
With large, broad-acred lands ;
And the laws that baulk his creditors
Are strong as iron bands.
His laugh is free and loud and long,
His dress is spick-and-span ;
He pays no debt With honest sweat,
He keeps whate’er he can,
And stares the whole world in the face,
For he fears not any man.
Week in, week out, from morn till night,
Prince-like he runs the show ;
And a round of social gaieties
Keeps things from getting slow—
As the agent of his wife, of course
His credit’s never low.”

A deputation of Victorian traders, giving
Cp-Operative Coupons, waited on Mr. Peacock in
support of the system. The deputation (to quote
the Akgus) was large enough to represent the
Continent ; and the various speakers were eloquent
regarding the benefits they had received from
issuing coupons. They stated that the public
were unanimous in favor of coupons, and it was
only a small section of traders who were hostile.*

Of the last Parkes Administration four
members have found their way into the Federal
ParliamentM'Millan, Gould, Bruce Smith, and
I umpkin ditto. The others did not seek election.
Sure signs of the coming N.S.W. State
election:
Mr. Mahon.v, M.L.A. for Annandale, and Mr. Horae,
member for the Glebe, have each obtained from the State
Chief Secretary’s Department a promise of 20 pairs of
blankets for the poor of their respective districts.
“Mr. Reid drew attention to the vague-
ness of the Ministerial declarations on the Queens-
land Kanaka labor question.”— Daily papev repovt.
Reid ! Reid! ! Wasn’t there a vague man of that
name who was going North in April (and didn’t
go) to decide whether he would be pro-Kanaka or
anti-Kanaka ?
Sydney Daily Telegraph in a fine frenzy :
Labour members must be compelled to choose their
side in Commonwealth politics like other members, and
be content with the influence to which their numbers
legitimately entitle them.
Well, they have chosen their side. There are
three sides, and theirs is one of them. Did the
fELEGRAPH never hear of a triangle V
Asa mark of respect to dear old partisan
Parkes, why did nobody propose J. H. Young
for leadership of N.S.W. Freetrade party. Young
was Parkes’s ideal Freetrader. That is, he was
mostly Protectionist, and where ham and butter
were concerned he was ultra-protectionist. But
seemingly Potato Young wasn’t considered Pro-
tectionist enough to lead the Freetrade party.
N.S.W. old Freetrade party, which, when
in hoffis, always said it couldn’t afford Free
Education, the money being wanted to pay for
free postage for the Herald and News and Tele-
graph, is starting to demand that the See Gov-
ernment be thrown out for not giving Free
Education. Of course, if the See Government
had promised to give it the demand would be that
it should be thrown out for “committing the
country to reckless expenditure.”
Did this cable really appear in the dailies
of 18th May, or is it a dream ?
ThcCapc University has provided facilities for prisoners
at bt. Helena to pass their examinations.
AiM the hog-like ignorance of these uncouth Boer
ruffians was the theme of every Jingo paper only
18 months ago ! At that time, they were sup-
posed not to be able to read large capitals, or to
recognise soap when they saw it.
Significant cablegram :
London, May 16.—Commandant Fourie, who was for
some time one of the most active of the Boer leaders
lias fallen under the suspicion of his followers. Thev
suspect that he is desirous of surrendering, and have im-
prisoned him on his own farm.
How does that chip in with the stories of Boer
commanders cruelly driving their men into battle
with sjambocks? In this case it looks like the
men driving their commander into battle.
Sydney Telegraph, in its anxiety to
secure the Labor vote for the cause of the British
and German importing firms, lately implored
the N.S.W. Laborists to remember that every-
thing they have gained has been through their
association with the Calico Jimmy crowd. Let’s
see ! They got One-Man-One-Vote from Dibbs.
They got Early-Closing and Old Age Pensions
and the 7s. a day minimum wage from Lyne and
Co. And Reid gave them a rail-painting job.
If the Federal Government would start its
researches after a Federal capital by passing an
Act to the effect that all private lands within the
Federal territory shall be resumed at
the present N.S. W. land-tax valuation
with not over 10 per cent, added for
disturbance, there would almost cer-
tainly be a painful silence among
some of the loud Orange-Canoblas
barrackers. It is generally under-
stood that when the Government buys
land it should pay twice or three
times its fair value. If the Federal
Government makes it dead certain
that it is only going to pay a fair value, certain
fersons who are enthusiastically marking out a
'ederal capital (in the public interest, of course)
right on or alongside their property, will get into a
much calmer frame of mind.
George Leake takes office as Premier of
Westralia, and so the old Forrest gang goes out
of power, after a glorious financial drunk of 11
years. Illingworth is Treasurer; Xingsmill,
Minister for Works ; Sommers and Gregory take
the Lands and Mines Department; and Richard
Speight, Victorian railway commissioner of the
boom-time and hero of the stupendous libel suit of
Space versus Time, was a kind of temporary
Minister of Railways for two days, and then
gave place to Holmes.
The strike of the Sydney Ferries’ Co.
employes—doubly inconvenient in view of the
natural increase of traffic over the York reception
—was ended in less than 48 hours by the conces-
sion of the men’s demands. The principal of these
was “ that 60 hours be allowed to constitute a
week s work, and additional hours be reckoned as
overtime. Surely a modest enough requirement;
and the ta,ct that the men had to go on strike to
procure the concession is strong evidence of the
= ty,m N.S.W., of a Board of Compulsory
State Premier See, who charges Opposition
.Leader Lee with socialism because the latter
genuinely or not, advocates State coal-mines to
supply the State railways, makes a sick exhibition
ot himself as a reasoner. State railways are as
much sodalistic as would be a State coal-mine to
teed the railways. In Prussia, where they are not
socialistic—only intensely business-like—the State
has lately been buying up mines so that it may
not be liable to have its coal cut off because some
tool ot a coal-owner quarrels with his employes
over 2d. or so, and starts a strike which may
spread all over the kingdom. J
Spite of Bigjohn’s boasted electoral con-
cessions to the goldfields, East Kimberley still
w S \ 07 ™ te , rs forone member, and Kalgoorlie4897 :

Mooted among some of the See crowd to
start another Unemployed Advisory Board to
assist the Labor Bureau. Such boards are abso-
lute shams, organised wholly in the interests of
political hangers-on. The unemployed get no-
thing, and are not meant to get anything, out of
them, field’s Advisory Board of a few years ago
cost a good deal in advice, and the value of it was
about ml. Of course, such boards don’t ask pay-
ment at the start. The office is purely honorary,
it is said. But notice how the Premier who
starts the business rounds up his favorite dead-
beats. Then, some evening, you observe a few
hundreds on the Estimates.
Mauger, of Victoria, remarked the other
day in the Federal House of Heps, that Protec-
tion had helped his State through the 1893 crisis
and its results. Whereupon Joe Cook said
proudly that N.S.W. got through all right with-
out Protection. Being told that N.S.W. got
through by extra borrowing Joe Cook got mad
and denied the statement point blank. The
Foreign-trader’s capacity for denying the most
obvious - facts when they don’t square with his
theory is calculated to bring a blush up behind
the ears of Ananias. The great bank crisis took
place early in ’93, and under Protection N.S.W.
pulled through till the beginning of 1896. When
it abandoned Dibbs’s Protective tariff it pulled
through by larger and larger loan expenditure
every year. (£1,270,000 in 1896; £1,477,000 in
.97 ; £1,644,000 m '98; £2,032,000 in ’99; £2,211,000
m 1900 ; £2,287,000 in the 10 months to 30th April,
1.101) These are Statistician Coghlan’s figures,
and the most frantic denials of Holy Joe Cook
won t shift them. These denials only serve to
inspire a grievous doubt about local-preacher
Cook's reliability.

A FAIR GUESS.
VISITOR : "What is the height of that arch , my
man? ”
COMMON PERSON : “It’s the height of folly and
extravagance.”
“ The heart of the Irish soldier beats as
true beneath his tunic as the Highlander’s heart
under his kilt ! Flavin, M.P. for Kerry in
British House of Commons.
King Edward VII. should be credited
with an initial deed of courage and straightfor-
wardness. Almost his first act, on ascending the
throne, was to pronounce for a White Britain
by sacking the Oriental niggers who had been
making the Royal presence all too smellful during
Victoria’s latter days.
Westralia’s imports for 1900 were worth
£33 11s. per inhabitant; exports, £3B 12s. per in-
habitant. The difference is £5 per inhabitant, or
£25 a year (nearly 10s. a week) for a family of five
ill at represents money or goods sent away to pay
interest on foreign debts, and if it wasn’t that
W estralia still pays much of its interest by fresh
borrowings, the surplus of exports would be twice
as Hrge. It will be more than twice as large some
day. The “ influx of foreign capital for the de-
velopment of our great natural resources ” is a
ruinous thing. We get the development, and the
foreign pawnbroker gets the resources.
S.A. Tories tried hard to induce new State
Premier Jenkins to coalesce with them and thus
crush the Labor Party, but Jenkins remained true
to his supporters and appointed two faithful
Liberals m Butler and Brooker. The Laborites
supported Kingston for seven solid years, and
Holder after him, and they intend to support
Jenkins if he is the kind of Jenkins that is ex-
pected. And, looking at the thing as a matter of
mere policy, it would have been absurdly foolish
of Jenkins to exchange that reliable support for
the unstable vote of Bill Copley and the greasy
right hand of fellowship held out by Robert
Horn burg and the Stomach Party generally.

ment how Protection reduces wages while Free-
trade makes the worker Jolly and Fat, N.S.W
experienced the largest strike it had seen for a
f/ri’ m £v liy , d i l ; ya ' , The employes of the great
Mort s Dock Go. dropped their tools by way of
enforcing a demand that their scanty Freetrade
wage should be raised to the figure paid in Protec-
tiqmst Victoria. The difference between the two
rates was, m some cases, as much as 12s. per week
It will take weeks of whirling Freetrade eloquence
to explain away this unpleasant matter. Men
don t strike for reduced wages unless they have
been drinking a good deal.

Ihe S.A. lories have spent more money
over the elections to be decided this Satur-
day than they have parted with for years. Dixon
lory nominee and advocate of the Snow private
syndicate tramway grab, is pouring out coin as if
it was inferior colonial beer in the effort to keep,
out Proud, who advocates State control. Mean-
while the Jenkins Government makes its first
great mistake by not declaring for State tramway
ownership. The dead silence of the Jenkins-
Government on this subject is a severe disappoint-
ment. When a man afflicts the page of history
with a name like Jenkins he is expected to do*
something handsome for his species, if only to
make up for being Jenkins.
The conduct of Tasmanian Premier Lewis
in accepting a C.M.G. has called forth much ob-
jurgation among politicians. Douglas and Dick-
son, both of Queensland, were the only other
1 rentiers in the Commonwealth who wore that
cheap degradation, and even they had it before
they reached the Premiership. Lewis is the first
man in these States who, while head of the
Government, accepted the meanest farthing
bauble that the British Government had to give,
and by so doing he has lowered the price of Pre-
miers and made a slump in the market. If there
are many more like Lewis, the time will come
when Britain will offer Australian Premiers a
J.P.-ship apiece, or offer to make them church-
wardens.
An Imperialistic notion gaining favor with
a superfluous class of Australian politicians is that
Australian Premiers, and such like, shall be made
eligible for Imperial appointments, say as
Governors of outlying dependencies. If this-
comes to pass, the next stage of Australia will be
worse than the last. The Australian Premier
who sells his country for a title is common enough,
yet a title is only an unsalaried gaud after all.
When there are also in view prospects of power,
a big salary—probably for life, promotion, a pension
in the far distance, the chance of being a lord, and
the certainty of being an Excellency—well, Aus-
tralia will have to put up its Premier’s screw to
£10 000 a-year, and make it more profitable to-
stick to Australia than to sell it. Zt is best
to get men who can’t be bought, but they are
scarce The next best thing is to bid higher than
the other fellow.
George Reid’s trump card in the first
Federal Parliament debate was interstate
jealousies. He is going to create trouble between
the various States at the very outset, if it is
possible to do so. A very special effort was made
all through his angry oration to stir up bitter
hostility between New South Wales and Victoria.
In a two hours’ speech he never uttered a national
sentiment. Petty displays of spleen and dis-
appointment were constantly to the fore. He
even went so far as to complain savagely of the
Peacock Government because its members had
not called on him and grovelled at his bumptious
feet during his stay in Victoria, and because he
had not been treated as a sufficiently extra-dis-
tmgushed guest during the celebrations. It was
the speech of a bitterly mortified music-hall
entertainer of the sixpenny order with a tendency
to drop to threepence—and it was a failure.

Maoriland is augry about some railway
passenger-cars, tly imported at a higli price
from America. 'ine islanders had expected to
receive something extra special, but, when the.
cars arrived, it was found that they were inferior
in workmanship and finish to, and more expensive
than, the ordinary every-day railway-car turned
out by Maoriland’s own mechanics in the
Addington (Christchurch) railway shops. There
are few things the people of this country
can’t make as well as the foreigner, if they only
knew it, but they seem to know it with great
difficulty.
“Mr. A. Saunders, the well known jeweller
of George-street, opposite the Redfern Terminus,
is always to the front where novelty and originality
are in demand. I have had an opportunity of
seeing his latest design in pretty brooches. The
accompanying illustration gives a faint idea of
what he calls his ‘ Federal Brooch.’ In the
precious metal and choice enamel it is one of the
fm
A Pretty and Effective Brooch.
prettiest souvenirs of a great event I have seen
for some time. A scroll inscribed ‘One Land
One People,’ is surmounted by the Federal
shield ; depending from each end of the sci’oll is a
gold loop chain ; while underneath all is a heart.
What more appropriate gift could be made in the
form of a brooch during the coming festivities
than this ? The price of the brooch in silk-lined
casket is 225. 6d. post free.” —“ Mab,” in Fkee-

Bead what VITADATIO is Doing-
The After-Effects of Influenza
Cured by
VITADATIO
READ WHAT MR. OOMLEY HAS TO SAY.
Glenelg, Adelaide,
July 7,1900
Mr. S. A. PALMER.
Dear Sir,—Before last Christmas I had a severe attack
of Influenza, which left me in a very weak condition. I
was weak, no appetite, and troubled with Diarrhoea. I
tried various remedies, but the result was not satisfactory.
I could not work as I wanted to, my strength failing me,
and my body feeling worn out. I continued in this state
until the end of February, when I was recommended to
try WEBBER’S VITADATIO. After taking a few bottles
I felt like a new man. All my strength and energy re-
turned, and I have been able to do my work, which con-
sists of splitting wood, and is, therefore, of a very hard
nature, with ease and satisfaction. Considering lam just
upon sixty years of age, I consider the result of taking
Vitadatio in my case to be splendid, and you are welcome
to mention my case to anyone. I work at Miller’s Timber
Yards, Glene'lg, where I have been working the last
twenty years.—l am, yours truly,
(Signed) ENOCH COMLEY.
For further particulars,
s. A . PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Everyone has heard of the man who was
ruined through drink ; it is time the man who
was saved through .drink had a show,
years ago a Sydney merchant struck a great finan-
cial snag at a very snaggy period of N.S. W.
tory. If he could get a little time to meet urgent
present liabilities, without asking for it and
thereby damaging his future credit, he reckoned
he might just crawl through; and drink saved
him. He came down one morning and told his
head clerk that his parents had both died m Eng-
land—which was strictly true, for they had been
dead many years—and that the shock was terrible.
Then he downed his grief in drink (not very
much drink, but enough to help him to cultivate
a hiccup and affect to be drunker than he was).
For ten days it was impossible to make him under-
stand that he should sign a cheque ; and mean-
time his clerk explained frankly that it wasn t
want of money but plain drunk that made it im-
possible to meet immediate liabilities. Also, lest
sober merchants should fear to do business
in future, it was explained on all hands that
the boss had never been that way before, and as
his parents couldn’t die a second time, there was
no probability that he would be that way again.
A certain amount of sympathy was even worked
up for him as a man of unusually deep affections.
When money enough had been gathered in to
meet the most urgent liabilities the old man
sobered up and wore crape on his belltopper. His
parents continue dead.
HAVE YOU
A BAD LEG
with Wounds that Discharge or otherwise, perhaps sur-
rounded with inflammation and swollen that when you
pre-s your finger on the inflamed part it leaves the impres-
sion ? If so, under the skin you have poison that defies all
the remedies you have tried, which, if not extracted, you
never can recover, but go on suffering till death releases
you. Perhaps y ur knees are swollen, the joints being
ulcerated; the same with the ankles, round which the skin
may be discoloured, or there may be wounds ; the disease
if allowed to continue will deprive you of the power to
walk. You may have attended various hospitals and had

Melb. Hkrald, in gushing .over the de-
parture of Duke of York, wrote of railway
arrangements, as follows: “The Royal train ar-
rived at Flinders-street at 12.22 p.m., and left
again. at 12.16 p.m., a pretty smart piece of work
reflecting the utmost credit on the station masters
concerned.” Rather !
Anthony Forrest, son of Alex. Forrest
and nephew of ex-Premier Bigjohn, whose pro-
motion, at the age of about 17, to be a boy-
lieutenant in the Westralian contingent to Africa
roused so much animosity, was killed there almost
immediately on arrival. He is generally supposed
to have been the youngest officer who fell on the
British side all through the war.
The Royal tour has been everywhere
marked by perfect concord and harmony, the re-
sult, in a great measure, of D.C.L. whiskey.
With scarcely an exception, the organisers of the
festivities have been unswerving in their loyalty
to the brand aforesaid, consequently they were
enabled to get through the maximum amount of
work with, the minimum of fatigue and worry.
D.C.L. quickens the brain without swelling the
head, and has well earned its title of the whiskey
of the wise.*
The last days of George Throssell’s brief
Ministry in Westralia were full of woe. His
predecessor, Forrest, wrote violent letters, deny-
ing the truth of Throssell’s statements about the
condition of the finances. There was trouble
about the legal status of the new Ministers he
appointed, when he took office. The general elec-
tion knocked his party into a cocked hat, and half
his Ministry couldn’t even get a seat. Then it
was discovered—or the lawyers reckoned it was—
that Throssell’s own appointment as Premier was
informal—in fact, that the formal appointment
had been quite overlooked —and he wasn’t legally
Premier at all, and his Ministry didn’t exist, and
never had existed. This was the last straw, and
the Throssell Cabinet passed in its resignation
after a stormy existence of 3£ months.
The Yorks’ visit has revealed to observant
Australians how little value Britain gives for this
country’s naval subsidy. The Juno and the St.
George are smaller than the Royal Arthur, and
are supposed to be of only the same speed (all
three are credited with knots) yet they made
a hack of her in fast cruising, the Juno especially
leaving her all standing, in their race across the
Bight. In Hobson’s Bay, however, the Russian
cruiser Gromoboi and the Yankee Brooklyn
showed themselves as far superior to the Juno as
she was to the craft of the Australian station.
This was a tactical blunder on the part of the Im-
perial authorities. The very pick of the British
navy should have been sent out; then no awkward
comparisons could have been made.
Patrick Perkins—the “ Jonah ” Perkins
of other days—died through a chill caught at the
opening of the Federal Parliament. Curious
that the only two men whose deaths appear
to have been indirectly hastened by Federa-
tion Dickson and Perkins both came from
the North. Perkins was born in Tipperary
(Ireland) 62 years ago, and was for many
years a prosperous Queensland brewer and a
very moderate Queensland politician. M ‘llwraith
made him Minister for Lands in 1879, and he held
the billet till ’B3. Perkins probably got ashore
alive from more wrecked ships than any other
man in Australia who didn’t follow the sea as a
profession in fact legend has it that any ordinary
captain would give the coast a ten-miles wider
berth than usual when he learned that Perkins
was on board.
The Duke of York was guarded in Mel-
bourne by some 40 detectives. This is Sydney
Truth’s account of the zealous “demons” at the
St. Paul’s Cathedral service, attended by the
Duke and Duchess:
These royal shadows watched bishops, canons and
deans, and the ordinary variety of vicars and curates.
They watched the choir, and searched the pipes of the
organ. Every prayerful man and woman, every maiden
and youth, were all under their gaze, and, when Ward
and Macmanamy’s round “Amen” rolled through the
edifice, it was repeated again with precision by the other
detectives in the Cathedral, just as the words u All’s
well sound in the night through Darlinghurst gaol
after the clock has chimed the hour.

“Jab”: Inter-State pug.-match for an alleged
i aBlde at , S y dn e.v Golden Gate A.C., Saturday
“fJA between Palmer (Melbourne) and Moloney (New-
castle), ought to shut the place up, and would if any
° ,? ame bufc pugilism were concerned. Palmer
couldn t or wouldn’t fight, and Moloney would but
couldnt. By-and-bye, after Palmer had jigged about
the ring asi far away from Moloney as the ropes would let
him, and Moloney had indulged in sundry pantomimic
rushes, sprawls, and slashes at the unoffending atmo-
sphere, Palmer lay down in the fourth round. Whereat
a section of the crowd screeched “ foul,” while more
yelled sundry something-elses, mainly consisting of com-
Fki n fi en k? ry nar k s regarding the management, the club,
the fighters (?) and) the officials. The “ barney ” was sus-
tained for some time ; meanwhile the “langwidge” the
strange cries from the throats of the howlers, and the
steaming atmosphere got in their deadly work, and one
had to stand it all as best one might, for getting out into
the street was an impossibility until the mass of hot
swearing, disappointed man thinned a bit. The means
of exit is only one way, and that through a narrow door
and a long passage.

“Bee”: A short time back went to an old
bruiser for instruction in the noble art at half-a-crown a
lesson, as per advertisement. Several turned up, and the
first lesson was something like this “ Before we start
graft, said the pug., “ I’ll give you some advice. Don’t
you young chaps think you’re cornin’ ’ere to learn to bash
and scrap, so that you can go round lookin’ for fight
becos that s not the thing. I’ll tell you what is the
thing. S posm you’re in a bar and a man insultsyou-
you feel like waltzin’ right through ’im just becos you
can box a bit, don’t yer ? Well, don’t do it. You may
urt that man or ’e may ’urt you ; that’s nothin’, but
think of the other people you may ’urt as well. ’E may
have a wife or a mother” (here a long dissertation on the
value of these problematical ladies), “and you may de-
pnve them of their sole prop and mainstay, so, I say
don t it that man. But if a man ’its you, that’s when :
it im back, and ’it ’im ’ard, jump on ’im, tear the cove
to bits (about a quarter of an hour was spent in describ-
mg the probable appearance of the cove after the

Murkan writers say that Bob Fitzsimmons
purposes coming home to Australia for a while, ere long
but this scribe doesn’t credit it. He knows the lanky
bruiser too well to think that he’ll leave the prospect of
P dl "eup a few dollars just to satisfy sentiment, and
there dbe nothing else in it. Fitz. could make very little
money here, though he is an “actor” and said to be
much the best of all the pugs who have taken to the
game. Mention of sentiment reminds that there’s little
of this commodity in Bob’s make-up. He is a plain,
matter-of-fact, cool, calculating customer, just as hard-
fisted out of the ring as in it, hence he is in a position
to withdraw altogether from the bruising biz. and live in
Easy-street for the rest of his days.

Doc ” : Why not schoolboy boxing champion-
ships in Australia as at ’Ome ? At Aldershot Hall (Eng.),
last month, feather, light, middle and heavy premierships
extending over days, were contested before huge crowds’
including big sprinklings of the “ naicest.” g Mothers’,
sisters, and aunts rolled up as they do every year to see
, P L b H yS r a ':, it LT 1 ' vhe /., the v ™ cham y pTons were
defined, Lady Buller handed them their prizes. School-
boy glove competitions could be conducted here on the
same lines, and with the same excellent results, completely
free from the slightest tinge of brutality or the merest
taint of undesirable associations. Writer commends the
SSHM the COnBideration of theNTw Pubho
Ancient : Yankee middleweight, Jack
O’Brien, has challenged present champion of England,
George Chrisp, who is also a middleweight; but George
isn’t taking any. Whereat ’Ome scribes bewail the low
condition to which the standard of British pugilism has
been reduced. Once a dozen champions could be found in
England at any weight, who’d close with offers from any-
where, and think it good luck to get them. This was in
the days when masters at the business like Nat Langham
kept “sporting drums” and always had a few front-
rankers on hand to spar for the “showers” from their
patrons.

Dear Bulletin.—Your issue of May 4, under
the heading of “ Sporting Notions,” states that Frank
Lelen, the enthusiastic gambler, died in London the
other week, aged 71. This is incorrect. He died on
May 5, 1888. He was my cousin. Mr. Frank Lelen, my
brother, who died 24th Feb. this year, was not a gambler,
and the paragraph referred to has caused me much
annoyance, and it will hurt the feelings of his family.
After 60 years strict attention to his business—that of a
nsh salesman—he died at the age of 73, honored and re-
spected by all who knew him. I believe your correspon-
dent is pretty correct with regard to my cousin, Frank
Lelen. — F. M. Lelen, Oharlton-street, Exeter, Sema-
phore, S.A.
* * * *
Third round of Sydney senior-grade Rugby was
decided Saturday. ’Varsity-Glebe match drew6ooo. Play
was close and brilliant right through. ’Varsity (with the
wind) in first half notched 9 points, but Glebe was atop
subsequently and made matters hot round ’Varsity posts
till “ time,” finishing with 6 points (two tries) to its
credit. The Glebe crowd had bad luck often, otherwise
they might have won. South settled North Sydney by 9
to 3, an advantage which Newtown got away with over
Balmain ; while Eastern put Western Suburbs down,
scoring 11 to 8. North Sydney and ’Varsity head the list
so far.
• * * *
“Old Timer”: Proposal to hold a regatta at
Grafton (N.S.W.)on the King’s Birthday (Nov. 9) con jures
up memories of bitter struggles in that neighbourhood
over a quarter of a century back, when Ned Trickett,
Lias Laycock, Mick Rush, Donald M‘Donald, and Pros.
Ooulon figured among the contending scullers. Peculiar,
too, that all those rowing giants of the past (bar Trickett)
acquired their skill on the same river (the Clarence),
which developed Harr}’ Searle. Mick Rush’s early sculling
was done in a butcher’s boat. Pros. Coulon would un-
doubtedly have proved master of them all had he lived ;
he was a splendidly-built, powerful fellow, with every-
thing physically that a champion needed, and a heart as
big as a cathedral. Rush and he often rowed double
sculls, and won a few good races together. The hand-
some,good-natured Irishman many a time said in present
scribe’s presence that Pros, would certainly have reached
the top rung had the opportunity been available. Ooulon
succumbed to swamp fever 27 or 28 years since.

Sculler Towns has been heard from per latest
mail. He almost despairs of ever hitching on to Canuck
Gaudaur, whose latest move of evasion, after expressing
willingness for a meeting, was to take on ex-Yankee
amateur Rumohr for an alleged bet of £2OO a-side. Towns
also deplores the fact that, after having won the Sports-
man Cup twice, and with every prospect of being able to
maintain his right to the trophy should opportunity offer,
it will slip from him, as conditions exact that the emblem
must be won thrice in two years before becoming 1 the
absolute property of anyone, and September next 'Cvill see
the end of that term. Small wonder that Towns seems
br ° k £ tl ci U Sr and alks of . comin £ home to the Hunter River
and N.S. vV. again. Pity there isn’t some international
association to control world’s championships, and see that
the right thing is done by making the holder of a title
enter the lists once within a given time, or lose bv de-
fault. J

“K.”: Further proof of assertion (Bulletin
par that lines upon which the Oxford eight’s
winning boat of this year was constructed were borrowed
from Sydney builder Neilsen’s ideas is supplied in late
Ome files. It is stated therein that a Cantab College has
ordered from English builder Olasper an eight in accord
with a design supplied by M.L. sculler Sullivan. The
feature about these short boats is that they are
very much more buoyant, and consequently not near so
great a tax upon strength as are the old-fashioned con-
trivances. Single sculling boats, fours, and eights turned
out on the same principle have proved much superior to
the original comparatively-deep-sunken craft.

It was in the early days of bicycles—front
wheels sft. high. A scorcher whizzed past an old Irish-
woman and her daughter on a country road. The old
lady—who was only able to see obliquely from one eye-
clung in a dazed manner to the fence with one hand
while she rapidly made the sign of the cross with the
other. “ Kathleen,” she said solemnly ; “ didn’t you see
Anti-Christ go by with a man on his back ? ”
The Procession.
With shriek of fifes, and bang of drums,
Behold the Royal Circus comes.
The quiet air is rent and torn
With voice of bagpipe and of horn.
The banners flutter in the breeze,
From windows and from balconies;
As if each house, with joyful shout,
Had hung its gaudiest washing out. ■
The Generals and Colonels ride
Like Star Performers, full of pride.
And feathers shade each martial face—
Enough to stuff a pillow-case.
The troops pass by—a stream of steel—
A proud sight for the Commonweal.
The people crush and crowd to see
This Royal Circus fine—and free—
That rolls along, with flags unfurled,
And all the madness of the world.
The bands play louder still, and lo
He comes, the Centre of the Show !
Who was it said, with cynic sneer,
“ A Prince should ne’er be seen too near ? ”
He meant, whoever used the phrase,
A Prince of these degenerate days.
Princes of old had brawn and bone
To wade through slaughter to a throne.
’Tis well for York, in many ways,
That he was born in later days.
He scarce could, at his best, do more
Than wade through half-a-pint of gore.
The martial music dies away ;
The day becomes a common day.
The plumed and brass-bound chieftains ride
Into Oblivion, side by side.
Alas ! I fear this wondrous Show
Gave Loyalty a fatal blow.
If not, then Wirth should deprecate
The competition of the State.
O.R.

He Wanted No Such Patient.
“There now,” said the doctor, “you go on
taking this medicine till it is all used up. Then
come back and let me have a look at you.”
This was in December, 1896. The woman put
the package in her pocket and went home. When
the stuff had been consumed, as directed, she
called again.
The specialist looked at her and made up his
mind in a minute, “I will have nothing to do
with you as a patient,” he said. “ It’s no use.
You are past help ; you are worse than when you
were here before; I can see the bones th rough
your skin now. What I tell is the truth, and
you will be wise not to deceive yourself with hopes
that can only break down under you.”
Rather hard, ugly talk ; but from a common-
sense point of view the doctor was right. For
four years Mrs. Agnes Briggs, of Norwood
Terrace, Paddington, near Brisbane, Queensland,
had suffered what she calls “dreadful torment
and pain” from dysentery. She tried everything
advertised or recommended to cure it, without
success.
She was an out-patient at the hospital for more
than a twelvemonth, and an in-patient for two
months. The medical men interested themselves
in the case; they tried right and left for the true
treatment, but were not able to lay hands on it.
This seemed strange to her, as she did not realise
how persistent, and frequently fatal, an ailment
dysentery is. She had never read the reports of
Army Surgeons on that point, and- possibly you
have not.
“During my illness,” says Mrs. Briggs, “I ate
but little ; food did not nourish me, and I grew
worse and worse, and thinner and thinner. For
three years I did a trifle of work, and then I had
to give in.” .
(At this crisis she consulted the specialist,
whose frank opinion has been quoted.)
“From January to October, 1897,” continues
the lady, “ I could do nothing whatever. Even
my children were cared for by friends. My
mother did all the housework, and, on seeing me,
she often burst out crying. I was so emaciated
and weak, she was sure I must die soon.
“ And now comes my extraordinary cure—a cure
so wonderful and unexpected that my friends
insisted on calling it a miracle.
“ A lady urged me to drop all other medicines
and use Mother Seigel’s Syrup only. Before
finishing the first bottle I was better, and after
taking it three months I was in splendid health,
and have been ever since. People can hardly
believe that the strong, healthy woman they see
now is identical with the skeleton they knew and
pitied two years ago.”
Mrs. Agnes Briggs, Sept. 21st, 1899.
Mrs. Annie Mathams, of Prince-street, Latrobe
Terrace, Paddington, Brisbane, Queensland, who
commended the Syrup to Mrs. Briggs, vouches,
in writing, to the truth of the above statement.*

Mick Simmons is doing a tremendous
business with his C. 8.8. Briar pipes. These pipes
are arriving by the actual ship-load, and Mick
Simmons’ success with them is unprecedented in
the history of the tobacco trade in Sydney. But

A great part of Sydney’s population has
visited Hardy Bros.’ showrooms during the last
few days to inspect the album which the citizens
of Sydney are presenting to the Duke ot Y ork.
There seems to be a great deal of miscon-
ception among cyclists as to the proper degree of
hardness to which Dunlop lyres should be
pumped. Many riders are disposed to ride them
too slack, and when so ridden the tendency for
the tyres to slip on greasy roads is much greater
than when they are pumped hard ; they are also
more liable to puncture, and to wear out much
quicker. Cyclists will find that “ Dunlops wear
much longer, puncture less, and run easier when
pumped that hard that there is only a slight de-
pression in the tyre when the weight of the rider
is on the machine. The heavier the cyclist, the
harder tyres want pumping.*
