Bt ARTHUR ADAMS. ss.
At Poverty Point.
Mr. Harry Stockdale has on view at 42 Norwich
Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney, his unique and com-
prehensive collection of Australian aboriginal weapons,
ornaments, implements, and utensils. Every part of the
continent is well represented, and some of the specimens
are, from their extreme rarity, almost priceless. Apart
from its ethnological interest, the collection forms a useful
object-lesson to anybody who undervalues the skill and
ingenuity of the race we have supplanted in Australia.
To the average Australia: 1 . —accustomed to sprays of
badly-painted flowers conventionally arranged, and two
little views in opposite corners of the sheet Blamire
Young’s illuminated address, presented by the music-
lovers of Melbourne to George Musgrove, came as a
revelation. Addresses of this sort are usually tawdry ana
common ; but Young’s illuminated address is a work of
art. It is in book form, double leaved, and the text m
beautiful missal-like characters. Along the top of the
first page storm-fiends are beating back Vanderdecken s
ship in a daring green sea. The Flying Dutchman him-
self is contemplating his past at one side, and along the
bottom is the cottage interior in Wagner s opera. The
second page is devoted to Tannhauser, and the artist has
drawn a wonderful forest as a background for his display
of the loves of the hero and Venus.
Soprano Ella Russell was very enthusiastic after a visit
to Marshall Hall’s Oonservatorium the other day. She
was particularly enthusiastic about Marguerite Hender-
son, one of Hall’s best pupils. When such voice and such
talent can be trained locally, Ella Russell wondered that
English singers should be so generally imported.
Peterson’s first students’ concert showed a pronounced
falling off from the concerts of Marshall Hall. The new
professor has no trace of genius, but his “ soiree was as
chaste as a young ladies’ boarding-school. Peterson is
milk and water ; Hall, rum and milk.
The Brisbane debfit of the young Queensland violiniste,
Flora Mackay, was a solid triumph, also a feather in the
cap of her early teacher, Rosendorff, who, recognising he
had an uncut diamond, persuaded her parents to send her
to the foremost teachers of the Old World.
The pit, at Melb. Majesty’s, found a local allusion in
the ‘ ‘ Royal Divorce,” where Stephanie said to Augereau,
“You spread your tail like a Peacock.”
Worthy Dr. Neild came from his retirement to cackle a
little at the shivoo for the opening of Rickards’ Melb.
Opera-house. The G.O.M, (Good Old Medico) counted up
how he had assisted at the baptism of the Royal and Iron
Pot in 1855, and since at all the playhouses.
“ Melb.” : Mrs. Sandes (Graham Berry’s daughter) sailed
upon the stage as Marie Louise in a “ Royal Divorce
with a refreshing apartness from the rest of the troupe.
They all seemed jaded. She was fresh as strawberries
and cream. Yet, of course, there could be hardly any
technique. But there was some degree of charm. I was
thinking of the father in a debate ; he always had a
feminine way.
“ H.” : The prophecy made by the fortune-telling
woman in Martinique to Josephine is referred to in “A
Royal Divorce.” This is not one of the historic mock
pearls, fudged up after the event. Sir Walter Scott and
Sir A. Alison have both left their testimony that they
heard of the prophecy before Napoleon became First
Consul. It was that Josephine would become a Queen,
and be dethroned. When she was a prisoner in the Con-
ciergerie, a myrmidon of the Revolutionary Tribunal
folded up her bed, saying “You won’t want this any
more.” She replied, “ You lie, man ; I’m to be a Queen.
“ Melb.": In disparaging the present “ Royal Divorce,”
one may forget the advantage of the former co. in the
gloss and bloom of a new thing. Life has no second
spring. So with a play not of the first-class. Of course,
one expects a “Macbeth” to be galvanised. “Royal
Divorce ” comes as a fish so long out of water as to be
hardly even damp, and it is flabby. Yet I looked for
better acting, and had to say that almost all was below
the Knight-Ferrar standard. Cecil Ward, Oathcart, and
Harwood, are the most useful men ever I saw, but
Cecil is here only utility. Knight had a Dr. Rentoul
tang of voice which helped to impose on one with a
Napoleonic personality. Miss Raymond is a comedienne.
She quite failed to convince as Josephine. Nor was
Plimmer, in Augereau, anything like so good as Carvill.
Trebelli Dolores sings at each of her recitals an air
from one or other of the operas that will presently be
performed by the new Italian co. An interesting treat
for musical people who intend to hear these operas in
due course, but rather rough on the artists who will be
criticised by comparison with the rare virtues of the
Franco-Italian prima-donna. Dolores is singing better
than ever. She cultivates her voice assiduously. “One
must continue to improve until the voice has passed its
prime,” says the adorable little woman, “ for if one takes
things easy the things become more difficult,” or words
to that effect. And how much greater is a versatile
Dolores in her art of taking pains than was, say, the late
Sims Reeves, who for many years confined himself to the
rendering of “ My Pretty Jane.” “ Come into the Garden,
Maud,” and similar flimsy ballads.
Ada Reeve, who seems to have taken this second trip
to Australia for the benefit of her health, was unlucky in
being quarantined through a small-pox case aboard the
Ormuz. But quarantine is healthy, anyhow.
Jean Gerardy, the 23-year-old ’cellist, who is said to be
going to astonish Australia by his performances on the
big soulful fiddle, comes with the reputation of having
played a solo at the Crystal Palace to the piano accom-
paniment of Paderewski. “ A rare tribute from one great
artist to another,” as Gerardy’s advt. truly states. If
Gerardv were a Pole like Paderewski, it might be
imagined that the latter was only giving a lift to a dis-
cordant fellow-countryman, but the ’cello artist is a
Belgian. ...
“Melb.”: What a master of the stage Dampier is !
Nature denied him every requisite, apparently. His duck
walk I have heard absurdly traced to a fall from a horse
when he was a circus-rider. He has no presence, no eye ;
his countenance is inexpressive, his voice bad, his delivery
awkward. And yet how the mind triumphs over all, and
impresses its stamp on the audience. In the ‘ Power of
Wealth ” he makes an impossible play not only credible,
but convincing. Not another actor in Melbourne could
persuade you that a man can marry the same woman
twice, as two men.
“Fiddlesticks”: At Trebelli’s concert on Saturday
niffht (Melb.), a great audience listened, with more or less
passive approbation, to the first eight items on the pro-
mmme —songs and piano solos, very high-class, and all
with French or Italian titles. Then followed three
English ballads ; and for the first time the great audience
waS genuinely, spontaneously moved, and exacted the
first encore of the evening. So much for the cant and
humbug as to Melbourne’s hungering for the classics,
as expounded by Marshall Hall and Co. Decades ago,
Bulwer Lytton, in his “ Parisians,” exposed the cant as
to English audiences being sincere in their admiration
for the imported ii classic ”in music or song. It is just
as true to-day. The classic-taste in music is purely a
matter of affinity and blood, and is outside of intellectual
appreciation.
“Alex M.”: Went to Sydney Royal, t’other night,
specially to take in Lempriere Pringle’s Mephistopheles.
Was not disappointed. Best all-round rendering we ve
seen here. Voice more of cantante quality than profundo,
but nevertheless satisfactory in the lower notes, and,
with the exception of a slight “ shoutiness ” occasionally
in the upper regions, excellently produced all through.
Exceeded in power of tone only by big Enrico Dondi (of
the old Lyster days) and, perhaps, also by Edward
Farley. In dramatic intensity, however, Pringle is far
beyond Dondi; while, in production, intonation and
evenness, he has much the advantage of Farley.
As to acting, Dondi was decidedly “ sticky ;
Farley, better, but nothing to boast of; Buzzi, too
grimacious and twisty ; and Tilbury (a finished singer,
but slightly lacking resonance), too canny—as distin-
guished from uncanny. Pringle—though not my ideal
Mephisto (an ideal which I shall never see in this world)
—reads a good deal of himself into the part, and plays
the devil ” with it in a sense not usually associated with
that phrase. He sneers not at all (in the ludicrously
patent traditional manner), laughs artistically little, and
assumes—when the diablerie of the situation is specially
insistent—a monumental impassiveness of face and figure
that gave me the only really devilish effect I have ever
had from any Mephisto.
Barron Berthald, Musgrove’s soulful tenor, wrote a long
letter to Melb. Age just before quitting Victoria, to pro-
test against some cynical criticism upon Christian Science
and the good souls who run the thing in London. It
appears that 8.8. is a Christian Scientist of the deepest
dye, and believes that faith will move mountains, also
cataracts, boils and tumors. He has no confidence what-
ever in the mundane science of medical men. When
Berthald’s throat goes back on him he moves apart, and
sits down, and believes as hard as ever he can that his
throat is getting better, or that it has been well all the
time—and it has always got well eventually. There are
other advantages, coo. Christian Science never sends in
a bill.


What kind of a joke is the N.S. W. steel-
rail contract ? It is proposed that capitalists
be invited : (1) to put up iron works in N.S.W. ;
(2) to deposit £lO,OOO with the Government as a
proof of good faith ; (3) to work their men only
eight hours daily ; (4) to pay not less than 7s. per
day for the cheapest class of labor and trades-
union rates for skilled labor ; and (5) to supply
the Government with steel rails at not less than
the price at which English or American rails could
be delivered. On these conditions the Govern-
ment will agree to take 100,000 tons of rails. That
is to say the new enterprise is to pay higher
wages for less hours than the English maker does
-the difference on these two points will probably
destroy all the ad vantage to be gained by the sav
mg of freight. And then the small new local in-
dustry is to turn out work as cheaply as do the
and If 9l d -e sta bbsbed weH-eguipped foreign ones,
and if it does that and deposits £lO,OOO as well, it
is assured of one comparatively small order.

which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,

Fig an acknowledged fact the world-wide over that no
single individual or combination of men can be ab-
solute masters of every phase and branch of art, science,
medicine, or other professed avocation of a limitless
sphere of operation and magnitude. At best the greatest
scientists Q P f the day—men like Lord Kelvin and Edison,
Yours truly . . .
HOWARD FREEMAN,
Sydney’s Leading Specialist.
Professor of Electricity as applied Medically, Member
Dr. Harmon’s College, Washington Cor., Cal., U.S.A.
Director and Founder of the Institute.
for instance, whose fame is universally known—class
themselves as infants in scientific invention and research,
and modestly tell us that they have but a smattering of
knowledge of the possibilities, extent, and development
We have only bo look at the fine buildings that occupy
the corner of Elizabeth and Bathurst streets to gain some
idea of the extent and importance of their professional
work. But to have the privilege of being shown through
the building by the Specialists themselves, and to see and
learn something of the real character of their wide prac,
tice, is a treat that only comes the way of a fortunate in
dividual like the writer. .. .
The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for eyeing
and examining the polished steel articles with which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. The collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Game
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way.
The correspondence department is a veritable eye-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half-a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they 7 were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the work of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feelings and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
V 1 What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people whose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped mornijng ana
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “I can assure you,” said Hr. Wallace,
“ we don’t put in these long hours because we are fonder

of electricity, and only a fringe of imagination of * he un-
told stores of strength and wealth wrapt up in this
it'requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field m any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions-that of prescribing for the ll s i and
diseases of mankind —we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march of civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles ” are not so plentiful as they used to
be the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of . cases, and work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
a What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison. Why !
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same way, when we talk of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows. „ . . , .
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
be pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are “Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Ricord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that conducted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and Surgical Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-
conveniences for our patients’ sakes.”
Noble sentiments, thought the writer, as the time came
for his departure, and the Specialists returning to their

we bracket in this category ? Dinners and drives and
conversaziones are more frequently uppermost in the
mind of top-loftical medicos than the speedy treatment
of sad and suffering patients.


All through the weary debate on the
Address-in Reply one indignant long-winded
person after another rose up to ask why
the Government didn’t instantly bring in a
Bill to deal with the Colored Labor question or
something like that. And Barton kept wearily
telling each jawful person in succession that
the Bills they were asking for were prepared, but
he couldn’t get a chance to do anything with them
till the crowd stopped talking about nothing, and
let the Address get through, and allowed the
Ministry to settled down to business. The habit
of demanding why the Government had been in
office all this time without doing this or that has
become so deep-rooted that the automatic M.P.
blows the remark off mechanically.
S. A. Assembly elections for certain Assem-
bly districts and a Council election for the big
Central District eventuate this Saturday. The
Central District contest is between millionaire G.
Brookman, who is making herculean efforts, and
Wallis, democratic nominee. The result will
greatly affect parties in the Council. With
Kingston’s place as democratic leader still un-
filled, the Radical party seems to lack enthusiasm,
as Well as numbers. Only recently the Labor
men wanted the election date altered because
.Tune Ist is a big race day. It looks bad that, in
a big Labor stronghold, and when Labor is fight-
ing a battle of such importance, the party should
be afraid that many of its supporters won’t vote
in their haste to get out about five minutes earlier
to look at a horse.
An important question is, whether a
Federal member may employ influence on a State
Minister to have State moneys expended in his
Federal electorate, so as to please his Federal
constituents and make his re-election all the more
secure. Minister O’Sullivan explains, indignantly,
that some N.S.W. road-and-bridge members, who
have gone to the Federal Parliament, where there
is no road-and-bridge department, and who never
had any idea about how to keep their seats except
by howling for roads and bridges, are back weep-
ing on the old official mat in the good old way
just when O’S. thought he was rid of them. After
the election the districts represented by these
people will also have a State member, and
O Sullivan says, quite rightly, that he won’t have
them both camping on his doorstep—he’ll sooner
kill one of them.

Another
CALLED
Liver
DUTY,
The Case of Mr. T. GLEESON,
(For the “ Suburban Tivies.”)
The destiny of great issues at stake de-
pends greatly upon the ability with which
they are handled. All audiences contain a
percentage of listeners who require plain
language to produce a comprehensive ap-
preciation of the matters under debate,
their perception not being sufficiently keen
to understand the purport of a brilliant
array of high-class words and phrases.
Learned men sometimes experience diffi-
culty in finding words to express uncom-
mon impressions, and Mr. Thomas Gleeson,
of No. 45 Turner-street, Redfern, acknow-
ledged himself similarly situated when re-
cently seen by a reporter.
“ Whenever I talk about it I can hardly
find words strong enough to express my
surprise and gratitude,” said Mr. Gleeson.
“ I was very ill for fully six months.”
“ What was your sickness like ? ”
«« I suffered from a severe attack of indi-
gestion, which nearly took my life. The
many complaints which arose from my dis-
ordered system made life not worth living.
To begin with I had a feeling of distention
or weight in my bowels, which were very
inactive, although I d.d everything that I
thought likely to give relief, without
success. Much annoyance was caused
through wind in the stomach, which made
a rumbling sound, and caused no end of
pain. I felt restless, and discontented with
everything. Things that used to interest
and entertain me I now looked at with dis-
like. Every little trifle that did not appeal
quite favourable to me I magnified into a
great trouble, making myself feel constantly
miserable. I could not help worrying my-
self with an unnecessary watchfulness for
some evil I expected to happen, whilst at
other times I was afflicted with a great
drowsy weariness, which made me unable
to move. I also had wandering pains in
various parts of the body, whilst other pains
remained fixed for months.”
“ Where were they most severe?”
'‘The agony I felt through a sharp,
pricking pain between the shoulders was
quite enough in itself ; but I also had a heavy,
stifling sensation in the chest. Wearisome
aches in the head never left me, and my
limbs were as tired as if I had been walking
without a rest for a week. Impure wind,
causing a nauseous taste in my mouth, arose
from the stomach, and my tongue was
covered with a thick coating. I changed to
a deadly colour, my lips being almost white,
and dark lines appeared under my eyes. The
act of stooping caused an unnatural rush of
blood to my head, and the giddiness which I
felt on straightening myself up nearly made
me reel to the ground. I was obliged to
hold on to something to save myself from
falling. A profuse perspiration broke out
all over me whenever I got a start, which it
took little to produce, and my heart beat
rapidly with nervous excitement when my
state of lethargy was suddenly disturbed.
It was a case of farewell to my appetite
from the start of my sickness. I had not the
least inclination to eat anything, and became
so weak that I was compelled to leave mv
work. I stuck to it as long as possible, but
when I found that every ounce of flesh had
left my worn-out frame I thought my case
was too serious to continue without laying
up.”
“ Could you sleep fairly well ?”
“ My mind was too troubled and irritable
co allow me any sleep, and when I got up in
the mornings I was like a dead man. I had
lost my strength and vigor, and always felt
languid and fatigued. I was so uneasy in
mind and body that I could not rest any-
where long. A sudden feeling of sleepiness
used to come on during the day, and then I
laid down, only to get up in a couple of
minutes andwanderabout like one demented.
Everything was a trouble to me, and my
painful afflictions became more numerous
and violent as time went on. I despaired of
a recovery, and gave way to despondency.”
“ I suppose you were taking medicine ? ”
“ Yes. I tried an endless variety of
medicines and pills, but not one of them did
me anv good. The only effect they had
upon me was to make me sick and increase
my misery. It was this bitter experience
which drove me to desperation. My sickness
seemed unconquerable.”
“ How were your sufferings overcome ? ”
“ By using Clements Tonic regularly till
it cleared the disease from my system. A
member of my family had reaped inestimable
benefits from Clements Tonic before I was
taken ill, and yet it did not occur to me till
now that it was the only medicine I could
expect to cure me. I made a start with
Clements Tonic, and it picked me up won-
derfully, reaching my weak parts at once.
The knowledge that I was raoid’y sinking
from exhaustion through excessive pain and
loss of appetite was dispelled by a few
doses of Clements Tonic, which made me eat
without feeling any bad after-effects. Con-
stipation troubled me no more, and my head-
aches were driven away. My eyesight,
which had been very poor during my long
sickness, was soon restored, and the melan-
choly languor gave way to renewed vivacity.
I never slept better in all my life than I did
after a dose or two of Clements Tonic, and
my limbs no longer ached with weariness in
the mornings. Once more I grew contented
and happy when I discovered that Clements
Tonic was working wonders with my debili-
tated constitution, and I was glad to be able
to go to work with a will. Every day im-
proved my general appearance, as my colour
came back and my emaciated frame got
stouter and more robust. The unhealthy
sensitiveness of my nerves was banished by
the stimulating and restorative effects of
Clements Tonic, which I continued to take
until it accomplished a thorough defeat of
my disease, not a single ailment of which
has troubled me since.’
“ Are you satisfied the cure is permanent?”
“ Perfectly. Nearly three years have
elapsed since my health was restored by
Clements Tonic, and I have felt grand all the
time. It is for that reason I desire to pub-
licly express my gratitude, which you are
at liberty to do in any way you think best.”

An Idyll of the Stalls.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The man first became interested in the woman
beside him because of the uncommon color of her
great, tranquil eyes, whose deep liquid brown,
with golden streaks converging towards the pupils
like the rays of the setting sun, gave, from
their very strangeness, a weird effect to all her
face ; while the very spirit of reflection seemed to
brood and slumber under her dark, level brows.
Later, the man noticed that this woman’s com-
panion, on the other side, a woman also, never
addressed her at all. He wondered at this, for
the woman, who seemed to be a lady, was richly
and tastefully dressed in an exquisite evening
gown, and it seemed strange that she should be
alone. Yet alone she evidently was—and through
the play she sat and dreamed and seemed to listen
to the dialogue on the stage with face unmoved
and attitude unchanged. It was a five-act play,
and at the end of the third act the man hesitated
to go outside, wondering if, by remaining, he
could learn anything of this woman. His in-
terest in her had developed into curiosity.
Undecided, he half rose, then re-seated him-
self His action attracted the woman’s attention.
She glanced at him, and evidently recognised
something that appealed to her in his strong,
earnest face, for, meeting his eyes, she smiled
softly before she looked away. The man blushed
slightly, unused perhaps to such a situation. He
felt called upon to address her, and sought-about
helplessly for something to say, not caring to use
the commonplace assumption of previous ac-
quaintance, for he felt that it was no ordinary
woman who sat beside him.
“You don’t appear very much interested m the
play.” he hazarded at last, a little confusedly and
almost in an undertone. .
The woman courteously inclined her head to-
wards him.
“Dramas of this kind are so much alike, she
answered. “ There is the hero and the villain in
every one, and then the inevitable injured inno-
cent. I could put up with the others, but the
innocent!” A shrug of the shoulders and a slight
lifting of her brows accompanied the words.
“Plays are never naturally realistic, though
they pretend to be,” said the man; “they ex-
aggerate too much.”
“Where I quarrel with them is that they pro-
long an incident until it becomes the history of
several lives. Now look at this one; a real vil-
lain would have tired of dogging the poor hero
like this all round the world long ago; indeed,
he’d have been only too g:lad to escape undetected
from the consequences of his first crime ; a crime
is, after all, nothing but an incident, isn’t it? ”
The man smiled. “ I don’t know—there are
crimes and crimes ” he answered.
“ What constitutes a crime ?” asked the woman.
“ The intention.”
“ Ah, what a criminal then am I ! ”
“A very beautiful one,” said the man, softly
sending forth this remark as an explorer on an un-
known sea.
The woman understood him thoroughly, and her
eyes, meeting his, told him as much, and told
him, too, that she would ignore his remark because
he was handsofhe and interesting and unknown to
her.
“Did you ever commit a crime?” she asked
after a moment.
“ I think most of us have at one time or another
in our lives, or if we haven’t already, sooner or
later we will,” answered the man.
The woman was silent a while thinking, then
she asked : “ What crime was it you committed—
a great one ? ” .
“ The worst kind of crime —it related to a
woman.
“ Ah ! did you love her ?
“ Why do you ask that ? ”
“Because 1 have noticed that often men treat
worst the woman they love.”
The man rose from his seat hastily Will you
come to the balcony and have some coffee before
the curtain rises ? ” , , . . . ~ ,
The woman stood up and let him mid her
shoulders with a wrap; then she took his arm,
and they left the stalls together.
Presently they were seated among the cushions
at each corner of a lounge, with fragrant cups
beside them. The woman leaned back luxuriously
and watched her companion light a cigarette.
“You have not answered my question, she
said at last. . , . , .
The man blew many smoke-wreaths before him
before he replied.
“I sometimes think I loved her, he said.
“ Did she care for you ? Ah, but I need not
ask that question ; you could not have hurt her if
she had not. Where is she now ? ”
“She is dead,” answered the man. Bet us
talk of something else.”
“ What shall we talk about ?
“ Ourselves,” answered the man, quietly. 1
have often wanted to meet a woman like yourself,
whom I had never seen before, and talk with her
of myself and her, quickly—at once—before we
could get used to each other, and lose our first
keen impressions, outlines of each other. Do you
know what I mean ? ”
The woman looked up interested. Ah, yes—
so that we might see our own souls for a second as
in a looking-glass. Is that what you mean ?
“ Exactly,” said the man, with beaming eyes.
“How sympathetic you are —tell me quickly of
myself.” ... ,
“You are grave, strong, eager —you are full of
longing,” said the woman, gazing into the mans
eyes. “You are longing for a woman’s love
longing for the power to love a woman you have
not met —and the longing will not let you rest,
though you are weary ; and, oh, you are curious ;
you are burning to know everything everything
—you would go all lengths, do anything, to
know.
The man sighed deeply. “Yes,” he said ;
“Yes.”
“You have a beautiful soul, but it is so envious
and so curious it will not be beautiful always.”
The woman moved in her cushions and looked
away. “What have I been saying?” she asked.
“ I feel a little foolish, as if I have been talking
nonsense. Havel?”
“ I don’t think so,” answered the man, dreamily.
“Now, tell me what you think of me?” de-
manded the woman.
The man met her eyes, and stared into their
strange-colored depths ; then he turned away—
silent, ashamed and embarrassed.
“Tell me,” said the woman again.
“ I can’t; I am treating you unfairly,” answered
the man. “ Instead of trying to read you, I have
been thinking only of myself, and wondering at
the things you have said, and when I look into
your eyes I feel somehow that you are right, that
what you have said is true.” .
The woman laughed a low ripple of sweet-
sounding mirth. ,
“ How like a man to get the best of the bargain.
But, come now —although I can’t know your first
impression, still half a loaf is better than no
bread—tell me what you think of me now ?
The man glanced at her again. You are
sympathetic, and clever, and beautiful, he said,
“ How commonplace ! What a cheat you are !
Why didn’t you add goodness to my other virtues,
and purity and sweetness ? ” sneered the woman.
The man recoiled a little from the sneer. 1
owned-up I treated you unfairly ; you’ve no right
to attack me about my second thoughts of yout .
“Yes I have; you promised to tell me about
my soul, and you tell me about my body. Never
mind, we won’t quarrel over it; our acquaintance
is too short.” , . , _
“What age do you think lam?’
“Thirty.” , , T
“ And four years. I tell you that because I am
rather a curiosity ; I have lived all those years
without ever once falling in love, or even finding
out what love means ? ”
“ Why do you tell me that ?
“I don’t know; perhaps because you readme
so truly. I never told anyone else before.”
“Wasn’t it strange our talking to each other
and becoming immediately so intimate,” said the
woman ; “we must be queer people. Listen ! the
audience is clapping; the curtain must have gone
up long ago.” ...
“ There is nothing worth watching on the stage,
and I know no one in the house,” said the man.
“ And it is more comfortable here.”
“ And more interesting ? ”
“ Perhaps,” smiled the woman.
“ I have told you twice, now, that you are beau-
tiful. Each time you have ignored me. Why ? ’
“ Because a compliment like that is usually the
prelude to an attempt at love-making.”
“ Wouldn’t you care to be made love to ?
“ What an opening you give me to absolutely
crush you ! What if I replied, ‘ Not by you ?
The man laughed. “We each seem to have
tacitly agreed not to take umbrage at anything
the other may say. I would not have been
offended.” _ , ~
“ Well, no,” said the woman, I don t care
about being made love to. I’m so used to it, I
suppose.” , , , j
“ Are you a good woman ? asked the man, sud-
d6“ What a question ! ” cried the woman, flushing
hotly. “ Where are your manners ? ”
“ It isn’t a question of manners. ”
“Yes, it is. Every woman is supposed to be
good, and it’s the worst of manners to even
suggest that there could be a doubt about her.
“I didn’t imply a doubt,” persisted the man.
“ I asked you to decide for me.”
The woman regarded him curiously “ By that
question did you mean to ask me am I one of those
poor creatures of passion who live by what is
called ‘sin,’ or a woman, of the class I flatter
myself I appear to belong to, who has 1 sinned ’ ?
“The latter is nearly what I meant,” answered
the man. . . .
“ Then it is rather my status you are inquiring
into than my morals. I think you have made a
slight mistake.”
“ Status and morals both,” said the man ;
“ more morals than status. I don’t want to know
who you are, only what you are—you seem to be a
lady; but I can’t tell whether you are good or
not; you puzzle me.”
“You are very frank. I shall be equally so.
I hope lam a lady. I believe lam good.”
“Then why did you speak to mein there so
readily ; that was unconventional, surely ? ”
The woman laughed a long, sweet, low laugh
full of mirth.
“Fancy! so you are prejudiced and narrow-
minded, too. I swear I’ll never meet a man that
isn’t. Can’t a woman do an unconventional thing
without being immediately thought unladylike
and her morals brought into question. Listen —
tell me, do you consider me attractive ? ”
“Yes.”
“Has not my unconventionality attracted you
more powerfully and made you more intimate
with me than a long conventional acquaintance
would ? ”
“ Yes; I think it has.”
“And yet you think that if I am a lady and
good, I have done something very much beneath
me ; and although you participated in, and were,
in fact, responsible for, my conduct, you imagine
you have a right to scold me for it.”
“I don’t think you should have done it,” said
the man, gravely.
The woman leaned forward seriously, her chin
on her hand, her eyes shining brilliantly.
“Do you know what you and others like you
are doing?” she said. “You are confining
within very narrow limits the attractions of virtue,
while you allow every privilege and give every
encouragement to the allurements of vice. You
say that an innocent thing is evil, and frown at it
if a good girl does it, but you take great pleasure
in encouraging and applauding a bad girl in the
very same thing. Applause —bah ! —yes, that s
the word ! —man’s applause ! It’s what all women,
by force of circumstances, are compelled to strive
for, good women as well as bad. It’s our only
reward, whatever we may do.”
The man watched the woman gravely while she
spoke, and was silent for a moment after she had
ceased.
“ We only respect good women,” he said at last.
“Respect! pooh! How do you distinguish—
you pretend to respect the others, too ; at any
rate, in nine cases out of ten you prefer their
society.”
“ You seem to have studied the subject.
“ And why not. Is a girl forbidden to use her
brains ? ”
“ How bitter you are ! ”
“Am I ? Can you deny anything I have said ?
“Surely there must be some line of conduct to
mark the difference between good and bad ? ”
“ And yet a while ago you asserted that it is the
intention which constitutes the crime? ”
“You are very clever ; it is conduct, though,
we are speaking of now, not crime,”
“ A distinction only,” cried the woman.
“ What is it you want?” asked the man. “ You
surely don’t wish to invade the territory of the
bad woman, do you ?”
“ I want fair play,” answered the woman. “ You
men favor bad women too much. I want to invade
their territory to drive, not the bad women, but
you gods of men out of it. If it is a bad thing
for good women to be there, it surely is not the
best place for you, who pretend to the position of
our instructors, our judges ?”_
“Of course my position is indefensible, ad-
mitted the man, very seriously.
The woman broke out into a rippling peal of
laughter, then stretched forward and patted the
man’s face lightly and familiarly with her hand.
“ Ha, ha, ha ! Poor boy ; there, it won’t do it

AMRROSE PRATT.
Do You Remember?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wonder if you remember !
To-night I was carried back to the old scene—
to-mght of all nights—when I was listening to
‘ Tannhauser.” Strange, was it not ? The circle
of bright faces, the consciousness that my new
gown became me well, his chatter and Wagner’s
music, why should not these suffice me—that I
must go back like a child to the memory of a time
when you and I rode for a whole summer’s day
round the fences of the station up on the far
North-West plain ?
Surely you remember. Cannot you feel the hot
wind in your face now ? Do you not recall it as
you ride your solitary rounds over the yellow,
cress-grown run? How, in sheer lightness of
heart, we put our horses over the logs that lay
beside the road, and settled down to a three-fur-
longs gallop across the race-course plain: and
after that they started, and reefed and pranced
eager to be off again—do you not remember’
Ihose long, long fences ! Just past the old cattle-
camp lay the wide stretch of plain, where the
unbroken horses and the mares and foals stood to
stare at us cantering past—and then, with a whisk
of their tails, galloped away to a safe distance and
turned, while the big chestnut stallion snorted
defiance at us.
Do you remember the belt of timber at the edge
of the plain, and the great coolibah out in bloom ?
You stood up in your stirrups to reach a cluster of
the collarene, and fastened the creamy, heavy-
scented flowers in my dress with a kiss and some
foolish words. And you lifted me down under
the shade of the clump of box-trees and placed the
saddle for me to rest on, and boiled your quart
and unpacked your saddle-pouch. We ate our
bread and meat and brownie, and drank our tea.
Ihen you lay at my feet and said more sweet
things while you smoked, and I scratched my
initials on your blackened quart—are they there
J5 nder i ~H owl laughed, and pretended
that i did not believe what you said—just so that
you should force conviction on me with kisses !
. How still it was under the box-trees ! A little
impudent thirri-thirri was flirting his tail a few
yards away, and a family of apostle-birds busied
themselves over a crust I had thrown to them •
but no other thing disturbed that long, sweet
noon-tide. Presently you saddled the horses, and
we rode away again—past more fences, to the
boundary-rider’s hut, where his wife brought out
more tea, and her shock-headed, bright-eyed
children peered up under their dusty fringes of
hair.
We quarrelled a little after that, you and I,
and rode at least a mile in silence, and I wouldn’t
let you take my hand ; but we forgot our quarrel
m the excitement of killing the big brown snake
that glided away from under my mare’s forefeet.
When the sun was low we drove the sheep up to
Bngalow Tank, and X sat, with my foot swung
clear of its stirrup, watching you send them into
water and listening to the bleating of the ewes
the deeper calling of the rams, and the croaking
of the crows—that all seemed to make a natural
bush harmony.
You sang as you moved about the tank—simple
old songs—l’m afraid you wouldn’t have appre-
ciated Wagner to-night. You sang “The Broken-
down bquatter,” and “ A gallant young stockman
lay dying ! ” . . . What a little fool lam ! The
thought of those old times has brought the tears
to my eyes!
Then home, over the plain, turning purple and
grey where the far-off trees melted into shadow ;
and a last long kiss hidden by the timber beyond
which the station roofs lay white in the evening
light.
Do you remember ?
N.S.W.
NELLIE M. FIELD.
In Adelaide, not long ago, a man aged about
70 stepped into a crowded tram-car and looked
round for a seat. A very profuse and patronising
young fellow jumped up, saying, “Sit down,
dad.” The old fellow told him to keep his seat!
“No, no, dad; I’m better able to stand than you ”
said the showy colt, laying his hand on the old
man to push him into the seat. “Better able to
stend than I am, you young pup ! ” said the senior.
W ell, we 11 see. Then there was a very audible
biff, and the youth was stretched on the floor
And yet we are told that kindness to the aged is
a virtue. A little less ferocity on the part of the
aged might also be beneficial.
The City.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Th f Cr ° Wda our motlo y broods,
And plants its citadel
U P° n ._ the Delta where the floods
Of Evil plunge to Hell.
Through fogs retributive, that steam
From ooze of stagnant wrongs,
The towers Satanically gleam
-Oenance at our throngs.
It nucleates the land’s Deceit;
Its slums our Lost decoy;
t is the bawdy-house where meet
jewd Wealth and venal J,;v.
Grim wards are here, where Timour Trade
His human cairns uprears :
ere, Silent Towers, where girls bewrayed
Unseen rot through their years. 7
D tl^ rg °Z lea ’ neath 6ach eave grimace;
Hebt s mildews sour the soil;
At all there grins a Shylock face :
Round all, Debt’s suckers coil.
Here Thnft with Art obscene endowed,
A sterile haven finds
Where Mal t h„ s . o »»„. s ' „ hey . faced orowd
Slink from the genial winds.
Tim Dead’s miasma o’er us creeps :
I heir mandates dull our brains-
Inheritance, their steward, keeps’
Title of our demesnes.
Phylacteried ascetics brood
On their precedence here ;
There, Science tampers with our food
Or taints our atmosphere.
And Art spurns Poverty, her spouse,
I o be the courtesan
Of ogre of the counting-house
Or ribboned Caliban.
The City curbs the wrath that bays
Rebellious in our souls,
By soothing fumes, and pageant days,
And sweet Circean bowls.
With Saturnalia of the Serf
Our discontent it cures ;
Its I raud, to dalliance of the Turf,
Hysteric Folly lures.
I he Babylonian Venus sways
In every city park ;
Her idiot niece, Abortion, plays
Beside her in the dark.
Here, Oflice fawns fidelity
When stroked by gilded hands ;
In bramble of chicanery
Belated Justice stands.
Glib Sophistry our mobs deludes,
As showman does his beast,
By serving up their whims as foods
From wholesome Wisdom’s feast.
From craze to crime they bleating rage,
Pursue what least is wise,
And, stoning the unselfish sage,
Impostors canonise.
At times in free-lance echelons,
Or called, at times, “ the State,”
Übiquitous its myrmidons
Our foison desolate.
Exactions on its counters perch ;
Our marts Commission raids ;•
Sleek Simony, behind the Church,
Prepares his ambuscades.
Dame Rumour, organised, the Press,
Spirts slander—for a fee;
Or, masked in Public Welfare’s dress,
She gags or dirks the Free.
Great Spider Intellects here lurk
In bank and in exchange;
And through the feebler folds of Work
Hyaena sweaters range.
And o er that hovel-burdened waste
Where Indigence is pent,
The Huns of Property have raced
On withering hoofs of Rent.
Yet not all black our horoscope,
For, urged by Guardian Fate’s,
On hoyden Disobedience, Hope
Rebellion procreates.
And awful Exorcists contrive
The potion and the thong,
That from the City’s breast will drive
Its incubus of Wrong.
Ripe knowledge of our ill and good
In fellowship of woe,
Makes fertile streams of Brotherhood
From Ego’s glacier flow.
The outcast sons of Art and Want
Tyrtaean songs prepare,
To nerve us ’gainst the guns that daunt
From bastioned Mammon’s lair.
Self-sacrifice averts His frown,
When, angry, God at last
Our Gadarenian droves adown
Disaster’s cliff would cast.
And those Bohemians of the mist,
Arrayed ’gainst Law, ’twould seem,
Are cleansing for The Harmonist
The City of His Dream.
